The Red page
The Aerial Age is Here
Claud Grahame-White and Harry Harper
tell us in non-teeimjeal language of the
extraordinary progress made in aviation
under the stress of war. In Air Power;
A' aval, Military , Qommercial (Chapman and
Ha'll, per Sydaej- Book Club) the authors
remark that the next great war will begin
where this leaves off, and it will be a war of
the air, possibly over and won in 24 hours.
They suggest that it may be found possible
to defend a country from aircraft by causing
atmospheric disturbances by the discharge
of high explosives, or by charging certain
areas of the air with electricity, . which
would" interfere with the electrical ignition of
the aeroplane engines. The raiding aero-
plane of the immediate future will be noise-
less and practically invisible; and they dis-
!puss seriously the suggestion that all adinm-
trative buildings, arsenals and factories
ould be housed below ground. With all
ir vessels submarines, and all our cities
igouts, this world would be a pleasant
11 ace to live in. And among the possibilities
air warfare is the gassing of the inhabi-
nts of whole provinces by poisonous fumes
leased from hostile aeroplanes. “The fate
vaiting a nation which lags behind in the
ce for aerial power may prove terrible. It
ay find itself ravaged, defeated and ren-
ted helpless.... as the result of a blow
hich is struck and completed within a few
)urs.”
However, strategy, which on sea or land
is been worked threadbare, will again be
ossible in the air. The handling of an
nmense air fleet, extraordinarily mobile,
ad moving at speeds unknown on laud or sea,
ill give his chance to the coming Napoleon
? the air. Another possibility is the in-
ision by an army landed by aircraft, with
i course light artillery. The authors deplore
ae fact that this war has come too soon for
viation.
What the public does not know is that the
lodern aeroplane is inherently stable: that
!, no matter how it is twisted or upset by
usts, it is so scientifically designed that
utomatically it immediately rights itself
rithout the necessity of any effort by the
Hot. It is not worth while to make the
far machine thus automatically stable:
peed, weight-carrying ability, the necessity
or armor and other vital considerations take
ireeedence over mere automatic stability.
In inherentlv stable machine attacked by
. machine that had greater speed owing to
ts special unstable design would be de-
troyed. If the modern . stable machine
urns upside down, side-slips or falls tail
irst, it automatically resumes its normal
losition for flight. “It is impossible for the
vind, or for a pilot, even by a deliberate
aishandling, to force such a machine more
han.temporarily from its normal flying posi-
ion. Given only sufficient air space, the
raft will recover itself.” This is the
nachine that will astonish the commercial
vorld after the war.
The most interesting portion of the book
'or a public that will have to get accustomed
;o air travel is the authors’ discussion upon
its safety. As long as the aeroplane is in
notion, it is supported by its cushion of air,
iust as safely and surely as is a railway
rain on its metals, or a motor-car on its
•oads. Air travel of the future will possess
i luxury and a comfort unknown and im-
possible on either land or sea. The oscilla-
tion and grind of wheels of a train will be
lacking in air travel. There will be no
vibration from engines, no seasickness, no
air sickness, “for machines will drive through
Ernsts and eddies with a momentum like that
of some huge projectile,” no noise except a
faint continuous moan, made by the air as
it rushes past the polished hull. There will
be no feeling whatever of earth contact, no
tiring sense of movement for the eyes, as in
the case of trains. The earth, where it can
be seen at all from the clouds, will be far
below. In case of engine breakdown, a dan-
ger hardly to be feared at all, owing to the
provision of a number of motors for every
machine and the carrying of repairing
mechanists, there will be on every trade
route a series of aerodromes, down to which
a damaged machine may safely glide.
And it is safer to be in an aero-
plane in case of an accident than .in
a motor-car: if a motor-car's steering
gear goes wrong the accident is immediate,
buf if an aeroplane gets out of control the
pilot has plenty of time to recover himself, or
allow the machine to right itself. There are
no fences to hit until lie reaches the ground:
the motorist hits his fences a second after
his mistake or mishap. Curiously, too, there
is less nerve strain on the aeronaut than
on the motorist. He has not to dodge pedes-
trians, and his road is wide enough to obvi-
ate any possibility of collision. And instead
of breathing a mixture of dust and petrol
fumes, as do motorists on a main road, the
aerial tourist breathes the sort of air that
we go to the mountains to breathe. In fact,
doctors in the immediate future will pre-
scribe aerial tours for the business man suf-
fering from nerve strain.
J*
The authors are rather discouraging as
to the cost of aerial touring, though they
prophesy with confidence that as. soon as
there is a certain standardisation in manu-
facture a high-class touring aeroplane will
cost no more than a first-class motor-car;
while the running costs will be less. Com-
pare, for instance, the wear on aeroplane
tyres and on motor tyres. Aeroplanes will,
of course, soon be used for the carrying of
mails and of urgently wanted goods.; they
should prove cheaper than our present means
in the sparsely-settled parts of Australia.
“The first machines used for passenger
work may carry either 25 or 50 people.
Existing-type aeroplanes. with motors
developing 500 h.p., have been able to raise
the weight of nearly 30 passengers. With
increases in engine-power, and with improve-
ments in construction which will yield a
greater power for a given weight, the institu-
tion of the first passenger services should
follow within a year or so of the termination
of war.” But the authors differ from the
I conclusions l-eached by a projected passenger
aeroplane company which intends to ply be-
tween India and England, ultimately taking
in Australia. The chairman of that company
recently announced that it would be cheaper
to travel by aeroplane than by steamship,
the chief saving being of course due to the
tremendous shortening of the journey and
the smallness of the crew carried. The
authors think that till aerial travelling is
popularised, very high fares must be charged,
and that journeys will have to be run at a
loss, owing to the inability of the aircraft
to obtain full and regular complements of
passengers. And “if you can carry passen-
gers by air at a speed twice as great as
that of the fastest express train, you are
entitled to demand higher fares.” The authors
aptly point out that as the taxi, more expen-
sive than the-cab, immediately created its
own public, a new public that was prepared
to pay higher rates for speed, so the aero-
plane, with its wonderful increase in speed
and comfort, will create a similar new public.
A book such as this will help to bring that
public into being.
Swinburne
The much-heralded Life of Algernon
Charles Swinburne, by Edmund Gosse (Mac-
millan, per Dymock’s Library), turns out to
be the conventional discreet official biogra-
phy of a literary figure. It is full of hints
and repressions: an admirable presenta-
tion of the poet; the man seld'om in-
trudes. Swinburne’s life-long failing is 'the
subject of innumerable hints, but the reader
is left in doubt whether it was wine, woman
or something worse. For the lack of a little
plain speech Gosse subjects the memory of
his friend to the accusation of unpleasant
vices. If Swinburne was the victim of fre-
quent bouts of drunkenness why not say so,
and thus slay forever these suspicions put
into his readers’ minds by Gosse’s good
manners ?
Swinburne was an extraordinary being.
“His physical strangeness exceeded, per-
haps, that of any other entirely sane man of
imaginative genius whose characteristics
have been preserved for us.” He was sft.
4|in. high, with a tremendous head, made
more extraordinary by his fluffed-out red
hair. It looked like a balloon, tethered to a
ridiculously tiny frame. His chin was weak
and receding, but he had a lofty dome of a
brow, curiously like Shakespeare's. “A tropi-
cal bird, high-crested, long-beaked, quick-
moving, with rapid utterance and screams of
humor.” An extremely restless man, his
hands were always jerking and his toes
always skipping. Gosse notes as his great-
est outward peculiarity “a kind of fairy
look,” which he had never seen repeated in
any other human being. Among so many
flattering descriptions of the poet it is a
relief to come across the opinion of a con-
temporary at Eton that he was “a horrid
little boy, with a big red head and a pasty
complexion, who looked as though a course
of physical exercise would have done him
good.”
J*
It must be remembered that Swinburne
never had to work for a living ; his family,
recognising his genius, saw to it that he had
an income, small but sufficient for his needs.
Ilis first book was the stillest of all
still-born books. But his second, Atalanta
in Calydon, sent him like a skyrocket into
celebrity. Here was “a new r poetic voice in
England, a voice so full and pure and vibrat-
ing that no one could for a moment question
its importance. As a matter of fact, no one
did question it; what was remarkable was
the unanimity with which the critics and the
public vied in welcoming a poet more subtle
and palpitating than any other who had
appeared since Shelley!” Without impugn-
ing the marvellous contents of that volume
it may be said that the public had been pre-
pared for this sensational debut by Swin-
burne’s coterie of now powerful friends, the
chief of whom knew everybody and was an
indefatigable wirepuller. Swinburne was
log-rolled into publicity, but, in the Ameri-
can phrase, he delivered the goods. And
Swinburne went on from success to.success.
He took a delight in shocking his prudish
public, and it paid. It is hard for us to
imagine ourselves back in that public or to
visualise their attitude to Swinburne. There
was even the threat of the criminal prosecu-
tion of the publisher, and in the celebrated
criticism in The Saturday Review Swin-
burne was termed “the libidinous laureate of
a pack of satyrs,” and told that he “re-
vealed to the world a mind all aflame with
the feverish carnality of a schoolboy over
the dirtiest passages m Lempriere. Bu
official Gosse refuses to divulge the name o
the writer of the review': the ieadei is
merely told that he was an Oxford man,
afterwards no less famous than Swinburne
himself.” Of course, we can guess, but why
should we have to? hv
The only public speech evei made by
Swinburne was when he was ashed to pro-
pose the toast of The Press at a public
dinner. Swinburne rose to his feet and
shrieked out the words: “Ihe press is a
damnable institution, a horrible institution,
a beastij' institution,” and then sank back
into his seat and closed his eyes. . , ,
In his old age, shepherded and dominated
bv Watts-Dunton he developed into a sturdy
little man: for the last 80 years of bis life
at Putney he ate like a caterpillar and slept
like a dormouse. A necessary pendant to
the character of this extraordinary bird-like
being is the tribute paid to his personal
courage. He was afraid of nothing, neithe
horse nor sea; he w r as a great rider tlioug i
not a good one, and more than, once he
risked his life in swimming. But it is curi-
ous to note of this pre-eminent poet of the
sea and glorious worshipper of its angriest
moods that the only storm he ever experi-
enced was one in the English Channel, which
delayed his boat a few hours; he went on
magnificently describing that solitary storm
in prose and verse all the rest of his lire.
A Bit of Brown Trouble
Lionel Curtis, that smart young English-
man with a mission to save the Britisn
Empire by riveting its component parts into
a rigid Federal Parliamentary system ruled
by a separate Imperial Parliament elected
by all the Dominions and the United King-
dom, has struck trouble in India. In the
review that this page (28/9/T6) gave of his
two valuable publications, The Problem of
the Commonwealth and The Commonwealth
of Nations, it was pointed out that his gran-
diose scheme was useless unless he couid.ht
the large brown slab of India into it, or at
least consider the case of India. Lionel
Curtis met this objection by visiting India.
A copy of a private letter written by him to
one of his Round Table group found its
way into the Indian press. At once a storm
of protest sprang up. It "was asserted that
Curtis had made a fatal mistake in travel-
ling with Valentine Chirol, “one of educated
India’s most deadly foes,” and in staying at
Government Houses, where he would meet
merely officials. The curious misapprehen-
sion taken by a section of the Indian press
was that The Round Table group aimed
at placing the Dominions in the position of
over-lords to India; whereas the predomi-
nant feeling in Australia is one strongly
adverse to having any say in India’s devel-
opment or government. Also, a phrase in
his letter led the educated Indian to infer
that Curtis had classed Indians with the
Negroes in Africa. The rumor spread that
Curtis had advocated “the subjection of
India to the colonies at the cost of blood-
shed” ! Whereupon Curtis has published A
Letter to the People of India (Macmillan),
in which he endeavors to allay the storm.
Curtis made the mistake of trying to bridge
the chasm that lies between the English and
the educated Indian. As The Round Table
is now regarded with hostility and distrust
by India, it appears to Curtis that the only
service it can now render to India is to re-
main silent. The factor which most im-
pressed him on his tour of the Dominions
was the rooted aversion we hold to any
scheme which means our sharing in the
government of India. “They feel as if there
were something inherently wrong in one
people ruling another.” Curtis made the
usual Englishman’s mistake of telling the
Indians the obvious fact that they “must
gradually be schooled in the management of
their national affairs.”
Unfortunately, as this page has already
pointed out, Lionel Curtis is unable to con-
ceive a scheme of union for the Empire that
does not involve a Parliament moulded on
that of Great Britain. He wishes to intro-
duce that European idea into India, appar-
ently assured that it would - work in the
hands of Orientals. In Australia there is a
growing discontent with party government;
Lionel Curtis hopes to fasten this out-worn
mechanism upon an Oriental country. By
the time he has got - the clumsy thing into
working order in\ India, Australia, at least,
will have found something better. That is
the cardinal weakness in all constitutions:
a constitution must have the possibility of
growth and evolution within it, else it dies.
And to tie the new and mottled British
Empire up in the red-tape of a constitution
is to strangle it. And apparently the final
result of Lionel Curtis’s stay in India is
that he wants his proposed Imperial Govern-
ment to have an Upper as well as a Lower
House—just when England has found out
the futility of the Lords, and Australia is
writhing under the constitutional incubus of
her Senate. His Imperial Upper House is
to provide a home for representatives of the
Feudatory States and the Islamic commu-
nity, who would not be able to find place in a
purely elective assembly. And whom would
Australia send to that House of Imperial
Peers? Or would we have the satisfaction
of remaining unrepresented, while Mahara-
jahs revised the bills submitted bv Aus-
tralian representatives in the Lower House?
It looks as if Lionel Curtis's grandiose
scheme is drowned somewhere in a sack in
the Indian Ocean, with something large and
brown tied round its neck.
Wanted: Australian Nursery
Rimes
“Ah Gee'’ writes: In our Australian litera-
ture there is a considerable gap which I
propose lied Pagan# should till. I mean
nursery rimes. Surely Australian nursery
songs are as important (nearly) as Austra-
lian drama or fiction. This field has been
almost entirely neglected, in spite of a large
and expectant public. But not an uncritical
public! Bet no contributor despise the nur-
sery rime. It is not easy to get exactly the
right touch of infantility—the proper blend
of humor and inconsequence. At present we
have to give our youngsters “Banbury Cross”
and “Dick Whittington” and “I’ve been to
London to see the Queen” —which are quite
out of place in this land of kangaroos, rabbits
and politicians. I am not suggesting mere
parodies of the present models. The same
general flavor, no doubt, should be main-
tained, but in subject and metre there is any
amount of room for originality.
The idea commends itself to this page. A
guinea will be given for the best nursery rime
written by an Australian —in these competi-
tions the term Australian always connotes
the Australasian —for Australian kiddies.
No parodies will be accepted, though some
excellent Australian versions of well-known
nursery rimes have been quoted on this page.
Competitors need not deliberately drag in
Australian references or Australian color—
an Australian kid wouldn’t. A good Aus-
tralian nursery rime may have no direct
reference to Australian conditions, yet may
be indubitably Australian in content. The
Australian kid has an outlook different from
that of an English kid: the sort of songs he
wants to sing will not be those that the
English kid sings. Entries for this difficult
competition must reach, and be addressed to,
this page by October »>,
Alex. E. Montgomery: One reason why
we do not write as we think (“Copabella,”
5/7/T7) is that, though we can formally set
forth the results of the thinking-process, the
process itself involves mental operations
much too swift and elusive to be intelligibly
clothed in words. And, even when the
thought-sequence is deliberate and objective
enough to crystallise into word-form, it
would, if spoken or written, come but partly
within the ken of hearer or reader. Even as
regards terms, rapid thinking makes much
use of “counters” —pausing not in its head-
long course to find the coin-of-the-realm
which has to be produced if we would ad-
equately present our ideas in print. Fur-
ther, it is an unwritten law of ordinary con-
versation that we shall not be expected to
pause for a precision of expression that
might savour of the scholastic. Blessed —or
cursed—with a craving for accuracy (I do
not here refer to syntax), I frequently find
myself substituting for the only absolutely
correct term, in certain connections, a more
familiar word, which, though it but approxi-
mately serves the purpose, is less suggestive
of the university 1 lecture-room. Evitat an
artis demonstrationem.
“Derwentside” : Isn’t it time a plea was
put in for the publication of a series of lives
dealing with the makers of our Federation?
The absence of some such accessible material
is felt by this coot (doubtless only one
among many), who considers it the duty of
all Australians to get greater insight into
the foundation of their-nation. Among the
sub-nations of the Empire the history of
Australia’s development from the inaugura-
tion of the Federal movement to its accom-
plishment contains many useful lessons not
alone for the professed student of govern-
mental institutions, but for the average
reader also. With the exception of Lyne’s
volume on Sir Henry Parkes and Morris'
Higginbotham what else have we? Where
can be found a life of Crowland’s great
democrat, Charlie Kingston, Duncan Gillies
of Cabbageland, to name but two of a
lengthy list? The time too recent for a reli-
able estimate? Lord Morley, no mere novice
in the field of political biography, and one
who carries a weight which it is sheer imper-
tinence to treat with other than respect, dis-
poses of such objection. In the introduction
to his Gladstone I find the following: “Not
all particulars.... can be described without
risk of unjust pain to persons now alive.
Yet to defer the task for 30 or 40 years lias
plain drawbacks, too. Interest grows less
vivid; truth becomes harder to find out;
memories pale and color fades. And if in one
sense a statesman’s contemporaries, even
after death has abated the storm and temper
of faction, can scarcely judge him, yet iu
another sense they who breathed the same
air as he breathed, who know at closer
quarters the problems that faced him, the
materials with which he had to work, the
limitations of his time —such must be the
best, if not the only true, memorialists ana
recorders.” Whatever faults Yanks may
have, the instilling into the minds of their
youth a sound foundation knowledge of 1 1
Republic’s makers is not one of them. vv ■
are apt to forget when dead those who ha
made the product of Bill Anzac possih e.
Professor Scott’s little gem of a history has
done much to fill a gap, but his professea j
limited canvas still leaves room for the p 1
I make.

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Exchanging Pilots.
The Nor’-East trade with long low croon is
murmuring o’er the tide.
The wardens of the Olden World have Hung
its portals wide;
The stars that shine o’er Southern Seas are
dipping low astern,
And heading fair to Northern Bear our
shaded headlights burn.
We’ve seen the searchlight sweep the sky,
and marvelled at its sway ;
Cape Otway beacon free of mist and bright
Agliulus’ ray
Showed us our path for thirty miles, but
what are these to thee
That lit our way through spume and spray
ten thousand miles o' sea?
Bright constellation of the South, how often
have your eyes
Looked on and winked when Mother Moon
stole on us by surprise,
In cosy nooks down Lovers’ Lane, staring us
through and through,
As if caress and rumpled tress came under
her taboo!
Night on the silent buslilaud track the
ghostly gums between
We’ve seen your sentinel alight in golden-
silver sheen ;
And peering faint through soot and smoke
of the city’s grimy stack,
We’ve marked our joss, our glitt’ring Cross,
from Perth to Cairns and back.
The drover urging on his herd, through pes-
tilence and drouth,
Across the long, lone continent has hailed
you coming South ;
And seamen battling o’er the Bight, through
tempest sleet and hail,
Have smiled and cheered as you appeared
triumphant o’er the gale.
A thousand sons from your domain keep vigil ,
as you go,
The last old link ’tween us and home, the
last, perchance, we’ll know;
For myriad hosts have steered this course
who'll never lift again
That golden gleam and lustrous beam in bold
Centauri’s train.
Oh, many a weary night must pass ere you
will light again
Our way from Ilawkes to Devouport and
round the Austral main ;
From East to West of Sunset - Land, old
friend of tramping rover,
Our course is laid, eight bells have made and
the watch has taken over.
Adieu! Your mate now* leads us North
where bloodlust, courage, hate
Commingle in the heart of man, defying foe
and fate—
Your mate that shines, o’er fairest France,
war-wasted, bloody-red,
And guards in graves of Anzac braves your
own immortal dead.
AT SEA.
JACK HAMILTON.
The Sea.
“Oust unto dust, aye, and ashes to ashes” —•
That’s for the fellows that’s buried ashore;
Not for the sailorman, solemn, that splashes
Over the side, while the engine-hell clashes,
Tellin’ the engines to muffle their roar —-
Holdin’ their breath, hear them muffle
their roar.
What are we watchin’? The slowin’ screws
smother
Makin’ a swirl on the sea’s level floor.
What does it matter? The sea is his mother,
“Salt o’ the earth to the salt o’ the waters,”
Hard-bitten lips to the kiss o’ the foam.
Maybe he’ll find ’mong the sea’s singin’
daughters
Balm for the pain of the man-world that
slaughters,
Rest from the life that knew nothing of
home,
Never a light in the windows of home.
All that we see is the waves closin’ over.
All that we know are the black thoughts
that come.
Yet- —what’s the use? Ain’t the sea his true
lover ?
MAORI I. AND.
WILL LAWSON.
Two of a Kind,
:Tis thick grows the hedge by the old stone
wall,
And two in its shadow may cling and kiss,
And no one be any the wiser at all—
But he held my arm and he called me
Miss.
'Twas high talk he had of the moon so slim.
“Dian,” he called her. and “Cynthia fair”;
Many a speech spoke he neat and trim,
But divil a word o’ the curl o’ my hair.
Sure, what was the use of my waist at all.
When he held my arm and he called me
Miss;
Or the shadows thick by the old stone wall,
Or the rose in the hedge that awrnke for
bliss!
’Twas a weary way and a long, dull way
For soles worn thin by the waxen floor;
’Twas not an hour from the peep o’ day
When I turned the key of my father’s door.
With an eye that dipped him disdain in a
wink,
My hand in his for a flash had lain ;
Then he clipped my waist till he made me
blink,
And cried, “Come back, and walk home
again.”
N.S.W.
NOBA McAULIFFE.

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
The ‘contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
Of sufficient merit and interest and, adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 30, 1917.
THE HORRIBLE BUSINESS OF
SOBERING UP.
When Labor was throwing majorities into
the Eederal and State Parliaments the crowd
was busy giving three cheers for the victory
of Solidarity and the reforms that were to
come. For a time the Parliamentary force
created by Labor was a clean and decent
thing, and during that period it served the
democracy well. Then it became something
else and did other things. Its principles went
into the dustbin. Its platform became a
subject of jest. Ideas in relation to reform
perished. The mere maintenance of political
majorities was substituted for the realisa-
tion of Labor policies—-and still the crowd
went on throwing up its hat over the figures
of the ballot-box. It followed the machine-
made politician to the last ditch, until the
day came when there were Labor Ministries
that stank to Heaven, and their; prostitution
of the public estate and evasion of duty
became a scandal as rank as any the people
liad known. Australia has seen no more
demoralised Parliaments than those it
achieved when cheering crowds hoisted
Fisher anrl Holman and Jack Scaddan on
their shoulders and the population turned
handsprings of delight over the triumph of
the hour. In reality Labor drew a blank;
the crowd was fooled. And it is because of
a growing knowledge of this and a dread of
what it means; of a conviction that Par-
liaments are institutions where the public
welfare is made the sport of warring com-
petitors for place and profit; added to a
natural human impulse to give something
or other a wallop and the survival of the
notion that getting together and giving three
cheers means arrival at the Promised Land
—it is from all this that the cui’rent stx-ike
upheaval has sprung. The crowd followed
like jumbucks the men it formerly called
leaders. It is doing the same thing again.
And just as it cheered what was rotten
and futile a day or two ago, it is cheering
what will give it no more than the same old
mouthful of ashes to chew again. Labor set
out as a political force to get salvation
through the orderly processes of law and
democratic institutions. Having failed to
win through in half an hour, it allows itself
to be taken charge of by another crowd and
Starts off to save the situation by becoming
an industrial rabble in pursuit of something
which cannot be defined and intent upon
turning a new-won democracy into a thing
of scorn. Wherefore are many people once
more obsessed with a nightmare that the
end of all things has come and popular gov-
ernment has broken down and the Devil
arrived to claim his own at last. These fears
would only be realised if Labor’s mistrust
of democracy was founded on something
more substantial than is the case. The
sins of our Parliaments have certainly been
many and glaring. But just as this is the in-
evitable outcome of casualness, ignorance
and a supine acquiescence in political trick-
ery, so will a return to the sane democratic
conceptions of citizenship which Labor stood
for in the days of former trial and tribula-
tion be certain. Australian men and women
are not going to give up the struggle to
govern themselves and hand the job over to
someone who reckons he can make a better
job of it. They have learned something
about democracy these last few years. They
will be none the worse in the end for learn-
ing something about the other thing.
Organised labor that was yesterday the
obedient servitor of politicians is to-day the
marionette of committees which want to
exercise over the State an authority greater
than that of Parliament. “Send us to Par-
liament and we will see you get work and
higher wages and improved social condi-
tions,” said the candidate for Parliament —
and Labor unionists pushed him in. They
got work and higher wages right enough—
and then they were heavily taxed, and the
higher wages began to evaporate, and each
successive million squandered on the main-
tenance of shirk in high places and go-slow
in other places became more evanescent than
the one before. Then when the other fellow
had got pushed into Parliament another
voice was heard. “Never mind,” it said,
“Parliament matters nothing. You can be
its master. Stop work ! Tie up the nation!
Be united as workers, not as citizens; then
all industry will be yours. We will have
an industrial commonwealth and you will
decide the conduct of every industry and
the distribution of all created wealth.” And
with this rigmarole in its ears, and hearing
frenzied exhortations to be loyal to union-
ism, Labor installed its governing junta, and
when someone crooked a finger thousands of
men turned themselves into a class apart
from their fellows, made war on their State,
and with a fatuity almost pathetic chal-
lenged the right of the democracy to govern
itself. Now, it is no good imagining that
men would do this sort of thing if they did
not feel they were suffering injustice. This
paper is not going to say—demented and
treacherous though the attack made on the
nation by the hare-brained crowd who are
stage-managing the strike may be, and abom-
inable though the record of many Labor
Ministries undoubtedly is—that the toiling
men and women of this country have no
injustices to complain of. They have many.
Some they have had made for them. Some
they have manufactured for themselves—
and none are more sinister than these latter.
No argument can be found to dispute this:
that a very large number of men and women
find themselves steadily deprived by the exac-
tions of landlordism, profiteers and Govern-
ments of practically every advantage nomi-
nally secured by years of incessant effort.
Nor can it be controverted that the worker
looking around him sees industrial and social
parasitism in affluence —a social scheme at
which the gorge of every normal, healthy
man rises in disgust. No one with even a
glimmer of imagination can suppose that
tremendous alterations have not to be made
in the structure of society, or that economic
changes of which the aim must always be
the world’s treasure for the world’s workers
can be x-esisted indefinitely. Neither is it to
be expected that the battles of democracy will
be waged with feather pillows. But even
if we grant the justice of Labor’s complaint
that Parliaments have in a large measure
failed as instruments of reform, and that
the worker is too often skinned, it is im-
possible here in Australia for Labor to
escape the i-esponsibility for this, or to
prove that it has not poisoned the wells
against itself. And it is as certain as the
multiplication-table that every one of the
ills the common herd are suffering from by
reason of their follies in the field of politics
will be accentuated by their denial of democ-
racy’s primary right. The crude syndi-
calism that Australian unionism is flirting
with now is evei'ywhere the proclamation
of despair. Do Australians, possessing a
wider liberty to. order their affairs than any
other people on earth, say that the only use
they can put this liberty to is to sm-render
themselves body and bones to the guidance
of a coterie of individuals who have no
notion of what they want to do, where they
want to go, or how they are going to set
there?
* * * *
During the past five or six years The
Bulletin, sorry for the army of dupes who
were being preyed on by political adven-
turers, has warned them many times that
they were being sold. It warns them again
that they are being led farther downhill
than ever. This paper is still sorry for
them. It is more sorry for Australia.
Issues are being raised in the Commonwealth
that cut right into the heart of the national
life; and Labor can make up its mind with
certainty to this—-that, so far from the influ-
ences which opposed it in the past and bit-
terly resented the fine things it stood for
being genuinely disturbed by these later
developments, the reverse is the case. Every
opponent of Labor in Australia is watching
its perfoi’mances with glee. He knows that
its power is being wasted. Its leaders or
manipulators or whatever they may be now
preach the glad tidings of a changed society
resting on the basis of an industrial com-
monwealth —of goveimment of the people by
a few of. the people for some of the people.
And their Commonwealth means what?
They .cannot explain. They do not
know. To every social or economic
movement there is a practical side.
The rant about an industrial commomvealth
built up by stiukemongers begins nowhere
and ends in the same place. It offers Labor
nothing but a diet of hot air and distorted
visions of Naboth’s vineyard. It offers no
reform that can be put into words with any
meaning. Its most passionate advocates
cannot give it even the semblance of reality.
To call men and women to fight in a holy
war of regeneration—to build up a new
State and put forward the clock -of human
progress and all the rest of it and then
present them with a large mass of dough is
about the limit of whimsicality. The men
who are the bell-wethers of Labor in this
business call themselves Socialists. They are
no more Socialists than they are Esqui-
maux. They are not even good citizens.
They are mere wild-eyed propagandists of
chaos. The future of Labor in Australia is
in the hands of Labor and will be deter-
mined by its intelligence. But the future
of Australia is in the hands of all the Aus-
tralian people and they will keep it there.
All this talk about “the magnificent exhibi-
tion of class loyalty” afforded by thousands
of men striking at the nod of a disgruntled
cabal is sheer drivel. The only thing it is
an exhibition of is that men are to be led,
even though 90 per cent, of them know they
are being led by messers. The day is close
at hand for Australia to face in earnest the
problems of existence, and though there is
great uncertainty about some of them it is
at least sure that these are not going to be
worked out on the basis of Labor organised
for the purpose of living on what it can
borrow or knocking off work in order to
improve its economic position. Years of liv-
ing in a fool’s paradise has puffed Australia
up with hot air. What is really happening
now is that the population is painfully
getting back to where it was before the spree
started.
SHALL IT BE A SHEEP-RUN OR A
NATION?
That Australila has huge national wealth
in its primary industries is not a cause for
unmixed thanksgiving. To live on the back
of a merino sheep is comparatively easy, but
it is not inspiring; and the fact that we have
immense quantities of saleable things to ex-
port which are produced by the employment
of few men has given the Australian public,
first and last, a distorted view of their own
position. If Australia’s ideal is the sheep-
run, then its industrial workers are mere
parasites on the animal which is the real
possessor of the country. But if we hope
to found a real Commonwealth of men, then
the sheep must take its place as one of our
minor assets while we build up a larger
wealth from the countless national activities
that enable men to live as citizens of a civil-
ised State. All these things were pre-
sented clearly enough to Australians at the
time when Federation came into being; but
because it seemed possible to get-along with
moderate comfort on the sheep’s back and to
import all that we wanted from the cheap
foreigner, the national plan was never put
in working order, and tariff-sinkers allowed
things to slide from year to year on the plea
of lots-of-time. Now we are face to face
wdth the realities of the position created.
Large bodies of men engaged in crafts and
industry which the country has never con-
sidered seriously as part of its national life
are ready to down tools on the slightest pre-
text or none, having the idea that somewhere
there is reserve wealth which can be drawn
on indefinitely to pay the people’s way
whether those people earn a living or don’t.
The thing is not to be wondered at. Their
belief is based on what tariff-sinkers and
the importing crowd generally have taught
them. If no Protection is needed to foster in-
dustries and the country can get along with-
out them, it is only a step to thinking that
the existing industries can stop when and
how they like for an indefinite period with-
out a serious impoverishment of the people
as a whole. The point which is missed is
that, if such can be proved to be the case,
thei’e is no place at all for the majority of
workers in our larger cities, and that sooner
or later they must sink to the level of mere
pensioners of the sheep and its owner. In
other words, the industrial worker is in a
vague way coming to regard himself as
belonging to a class which Australia —the
Australia of mutton and wool —keeps going
as a hobby, and that it doesn’t matter the
toss of a worn threepenny bit whether he is
earning his wages or isn’t.
* * * #
No doubt Australia as the world’s prize
sheep-run is quite a feasible idea; though
as to who might from time to time own the
sheep only the nations with the best fight-
ing outfit could decide. Worked to its limit
as a place devoted entirely to primary pro-
duction, the continent would still be very
wealthy; but there would be no place in it
at all for the general body of workers, and
no use for cities. A few good wharves, a
terrace or two of houses near them for
waterside laborers, and a few general stores
would be all that could possibly be required
at the place where the railway'touched deep
water. On the sheep-run plan, all the other
things that we now possess are merely futile
and irritating and a waste of the good cash
the sheep earn. The great bulk of the workers
under such a scheme could go on strike until
their money was exhausted; and after that
they could die and be buried at the sheep’s
expense without any national sorrow. But
Australia as a nation would be an entirely
different proposition. Self-contained and
manufacturing all things of beauty and use,
nor people would work for their country’s
sake, developing their highest patriotism in
their efforts to produce wealth for the
benefit of all. Then it would really be the
man who counted, for the whole system
would rest on a basis of manhood ; and to
down tools and arrest the national progress
would be known and recognised as the
basest kind of treason. Under the national
system, as opposed to the sheep-run idea, popu-
lation _ would be the thing desirable • and
every increase of homes would mean an in-
crease of work with an increase of wealth
Mages, as industry expanded and became
better and better organised, would naturally
continue to rise until they reached heights
which the most sanguine of unionists have
never dreamt of; and they would do this not
because of some artificial stimulus which
while it gave the appearance of betterment
really lowered the purchasing powe“ of
money, but because the funds out of which
wages could be paid had been enormTuslv
increased through constant production
There could be scarcely any limit to the pros'
perity of a country with large natural re-
sources which eliminated the idler and kept
steadily at its job. Australia has all alon»
had free choice of nationhood or the sheep B-
run, but has never yet come to a decision
Certain words and formulae have, of course
been accepted which were supposed to indi-
cate that we had some national purpose; but
the very first step on the road, which con-
sisted in turning down the foreigner’s
goods that we might make our own and
depend upon ourselves, has never been seri-
ously taken. We have had a mock tariff
which kept us as a rather disorganised sheep-
run while we boasted on the strength of it
that we were a nation ; and, as a result, the
great bulk of our workers, instead of being
Australian citizens in the best sense and
tackling the national task, feel that they are
a mere accident in the scheme of things, and
that their chief job is to get the best they
can for the moment at anybody’s and every-
body’s expense. In a downright and honest
sense the real strike leaders are the import-
ers and tariff-sinkers who combined to pre-
vent us taking the first serious step in
nationhood. To-day those importers and
tariff-sinkers profess to deplore the reck-
lessness and uncertainty of labor, but forget
or ignore the plain, outstanding fact that
they were never willing to give industrial
labor the solid foundation on which it might
have risen to discover its own national soul.
The task long postponed has now become
so difficult that one may well tremble to
face it. Labor appears to have gone wholly
insane; the great war thunders on, confus-
ing and complicating issues; the thousands
of Australians abroad may return at any
time, through a sudden declaration of peace,
to find the country utterly disorganised and
even the sheep-run itself mad with a dingo-
riot of conflicting wall and uncertain pur-
pose. Yet at this moment the tariff-sinker
is busy doing nothing, and the S. M. Herald
rejoices that there can be no “additional
specific or ad valorem duties.” Alleged
statesmen have asserted or hinted that during
the w r ar a Customs wall was impossible, owing
to the international complications it would
cause; and then the same men have given
us convincing proof that no such difficulties
existed, by the absolute prohibition of things
described as luxuries as a temporary measure
designed avowedly to conserve ship-space.
So the day after to-morrow, to which reform
was put off in the easy years when things
could have been easily remedied, has arrived,
and Parliament is making no move towards
grabbing perhaps the last chance that may
offer. The result must be the straight-out
win of the sheep-run over the nation. Fate
does not juggle with these things. There
is a cause behind every effect. For 17 years
Australia has been playing with the indus-
trialism on which self-contained nationhood
might have been built; and the result of
play where we should have been in earnest
is that great body of meaningless discontent
known as Labor. Its real discontent, though
it does not know it. is based on the bitter
fact that hitherto very little of it had any
serious meaning. Houses have been built,
factories set going in a half-hearted way,
suburbs speculated in, Millions Clubs
formed, and all manner of things labelled
“Progress” indulged in; but because there
w r as no real tariff wall to put industry on a
sure foundation, our varied efforts have
been only an ornamental fringe about the
sheep-run which has remained the real Aus-
tralia. And now, as the chances of
reform recede and the days darken,
the effect of the sheep-run’s victory
begins to be evident. Between the sheep
and its owner will stand the “poor white”
who might have been an industrialist but
wasn’t because industry was put out of the
picture—killed because the workers wouldn't
force the hands of their leaders to do more
for them with an honest tariff than all strikes
and Arbitration awards could ever do. And
the “poor white” of Australia will be a
sorrowful feature. The sheep don’t want
him; the importer will find no. joy in him
when he has ceased to earn wages. His
suburban home-site will sell for about 2}d.
a foot, and he can go just as slow as the
slowest I.WAV. person wants him to with-
out anyone else worrying. It is the natural
trend of things if the sheep-run wins; and
striker and tariff-sinker are laboring together
to secure its victory. But a Commonwealth
of men is even now possible, and Australians
are dying in the war-cloud for that.
AN OLD TABLE, SOME TREES AND
THINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
About the vicinity of Lonsdale-street, in
Melbourne, there are nests of Chinese
cabinet-makers’ shops. Each day I passed
them on my way to the hospital. Each day
I was drawn to linger and gaze more and
more curiously through the workshop doors.
The ant-like industry, the wastelessness, the
planning and piecing! They seemed to
create something of use and beauty out of
stuff that you and I would turn down
in building a dog-kennel. The stink of the
gluepot, the three-plies and lacquers, the
make-believe of transfer-papers, the patient
graining and staining! Birds-eye maple,
walnut, blackwood, oak—they turn them all
out of the same cheap deal. What end of
the earth the deal comes from I don’t know.
One tried to sell me a table to-day. “Allee
same blackwood,” he assured me. I didn t
want it.. I had watched its growth from
waste pieces, and marvelled how much it
looked like the honest thing.
I thought of another table. It was made.
I suppose in a day, with no tools but a maul
and wedges, an axe and an adze and a few
big bolts. Seems back in the dawn of time
on the old Gippsland -selection, but really
under 30 years ago. It was built of fiddle-
back blackwood. Even rough-hewn, the
grain showed out in live, gleaming ripples—
like the ripples in the back of a moving tiger-
snake. It stood firm as a rock, with massive
legs sunk in the earth through holes cut m
the slab floor, and held about a dozen around
one end. Its slab top had never known a
plane, and'when there was a tablecloth on
(which was only when there were visitors)
you had to be very careful to get a spot
level enough to set down a cup of tea. 1


don’t remember any other furniture in the
big kitchen except packing-cases and rough
benches and shelves—and the rafters we used
as gymnasium bars. There were some more
or less unsound chairs and an English-made
deal table and chiffonier in the front room.
The floors, too, were all of adzed blaekwood
slabs, which, new-scrubbed, looked like great
rashers of streaky bacon laid side by side.
A great ehock-and-log fence was round the
orchard ; one side of it was nearly all fiddle-
back.
The old table is in the harness shed now. A
while ago a traveller came along who knew
something of wood, and offered £l5 for it.
In the old days we never thought there was
£l5 worth of stuff in the house altogether !
The harness-shed then was an old stump,
with its top roofed over ; the fowls roosted
there as well. In the stump you could have
cut an arch through which a buggy could
have been driven. On the table valuation
the orchard fence would be worth hundreds
now. Where it’s gone to I don’t know.
There’s an imported wire-netting now to
keep out the imported rabbits. I remember
mother always insisted on “plenty of black-
wood” on baking days; and the fireplace
was the size of a small room. Blackwood!
It sounds like cooking with incense
and myrrh. The traveller who wanted the
old table bought at fancy prices some logs
that had escaped fire through having been
buried in a swamp.
, Pood Dad! Pioneer in the Gippsland
mountain forest, he butted against the clean,
straight, sound bole of blackbutt, the giant
bluegum, the blaekwood and the despised
messmate and ironbark, all of a density and
height that made the light dim underneath —-
where the sweet musk and hazel and tree-
ferns grew. Felled and cleared at what toll
of sweat and labor ! Roadless, railless, iso-
lated, for us the years passed as in a siege.
That majestic timber wealth so painfully
hewn and burnt off to grow—grass for
cows ! To-day, after the cost of clearing has
been paid with lives, much of the rougher
land is worth less than it was in its virgin
state; for the scrub growths are constantly
striving to reclaim the clearings; bracken
and rabbit infest it, and it is too steep and
broken to cultivate.
We used to go out with crowbars and
shove the burning-off fires together last
thing before bed. Sound, clear-burning,
clean-smelling wood. Only this comfort is
left. The felling of it developed some of
the world’s finest axemen.
To-day the Chinese cabinet-maker is piec-
ing together his cheap deal to get the length
of a table. I think of him making the
grain and the polish. Maybe he will sell
it on time-payment to furnish the home of
one of those pioneers’ descendants.
* * * *
Some day I shall got hold of a tract of
that tree-murdered land, and I shall plant
trees, trees, trees. It need not be very good
land. There’s a trait I like about the
feathery-leaved wattles. They will grow
in soil so poor that most else despises it;
and because of their power of conserving
nitrogen about their roots and their decaying
leafage when they die (which they do early,
as though declining to monopolise the earth
overlong), they leave their foothold enriched
rather than impoverished. A human could
pray for no more than that—to give fra-
grance and beauty while he lived, and to die
leaving his world the better for his having
lived. The slain bluegum and blackbutt
and messmate they shall be repatriated as a
right. Every yard of water-frontage now
wastefully eroding shall have its blackwoods
and wattles; and straight, sentinel poplars
shall weave in the air of grey European
waterways. If there is a sour, swampy
flat, it shall be sanctuary for willow; the
bovine-loved leafage of hanging boughs will
pay their footing. The ghost-pale stem of
the lemon-scented gum and the flame of the
scarlet-flowered will find space on the up-
lands. The darkness of pine and fir, too,
if only for their soft sound in the wind.
There are sure to be fertile, moist, shel-
tered gullies, too steep to cultivate or even
graze, where walnuts and chestnuts will
flourish as well as in their own homeland.
The chestnut is the staff of life to some
mountain peoples. And how welcome the
concentrated nutriment of the walnut!
If fate is very kind there may be, even
so far south, a slope sheltered and sunny
enough for a dark grove of olives. And
the choicest soil shall be occupied by a little,
luscious-fruited orchard.
I dream of the revelry of bees among the
trees; the homing calls of birds. I shall not
reap the timber harvest. Other folk shall
go a-nutting ; other eyes shall see the glory
of my forest. What matter? I shall sleep
the sounder for having given life to it.
L'FOU.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Oiling the Dove’s Wings.
The chorus of alleged' scorn with which the
l’ope’s mediation proposals have been re-
ceived by the Allied press looks as if it had
more bluff than sincerity about its top notes.
At any rate, the cry that the proposition
was “made in Germany” may fairly be dis-
missed as too thin. No doubt the old poli-
tical relations of Rome with Austria and the
Italian kingdom have still some slight influ-
ence in the Papal council; but what is of
more importance is that there are far more
Romans lighting on the side of the Allies
than for the central empires. Only in three
States of the German Empire —one of which
is Alsace-Lorraine —do the R.C.’s form a
majority of the population ; and in only four
others of the remaining 23 do they amount
to 20 per cent, on a count of heads. _ In
Austria proper, which is a comparatively
small State, almost the entire .population is
Roman ; but in the larger Hungary the
Pope’s followers count little more than oO
per cent. Add to these facts the pietism of
Prussia, which is Lutheranism stiffened to
the last point of inflexibility, and it seems
preposterous that any Papal council should
be suspected of betraying the interests of
the 80 or 90 millions of Catholics who,
without counting Latin America, are repre-
sented by the Allies for the sake of the 4S
millions or so who, on the most liberal esti-
mate, adhere to the same faith in Germany,
The Pope’s proposals may not be satisfac-
tory and the Allies may agree to turn them
down promptly; but that is no reason for
flouting the first decent attempt at mediation
by about the only force left in the world
which could make the attempt without look-
ing ridiculous. While the Kaiser calls Gott
to the bloodfeast and English Archbishops
combine with the Nonconformist conscience
to urge on the general slaughter, there is
surely nothing wrong with a Christian move-
ment towards international healing. Evi-
dently the effort is premature, but, equally
evidently, the Pope is bent on doing the
world a good turn; and to attempt things
like that a bit too soon is better than
attempting them a lot too late. The weak
spot, of course, is the suggestion that Prussia
should be trusted again; yet it is entirely
a question whether the Allied guns can
hammer the enemy to such a finish that he
won’t have to be trusted in the end. Also,
conversion by gunpowder has sometimes
proved effectual as a means of grace; and
the time may not be very far distant when
a count of German graves, which represent
the price of a broken promise, may make
for good faith. On present appearances,
Germany is certain of a licking when the
full force of Wilson’s effort is felt; but if
the Pope at his second try could find a way
by which the licking might be taken as
administered before the event, the world
would have no just grounds for a disdainful
snort.
The Arbitration System.
Justice Higgins explained at Melbourne
last week that the reason the Federal Arbi-
tration Court could not handle the strike
of waterside workers and seamen was that
there was no industrial dispute with em-
ployers—only a quarrel with the Govern-
ment about the cost of living. Also in
N. 8. Wales the various unions were strik-
ing merely out of sympathy with a union
which had a quarrel with the State Govern-
ment. Therefore as the strike did not
extend from one State to another as an
industrial dispute, and as the Federal Court
had no jurisdiction over State railways, the
Court had no legal status as an intermedi-
ary. This is no doubt all very well as a
statement of the law, but it only makes the
machinery of arbitration still more farcical.
Everybody knows that the administration
of the system has degenerated into a jest,
and everybody knows that the whole trend
of organised Labor in Australia during the
past year or two has been away from arbi-
tration. The strikes now raging are being con-
ducted for purposes which if achieved would
finally turn the Court into a ridiculous excres-
cence on the body politic. Their mere happen-
ing is the best of all evidence that either Labor
must get back to where it stood in relation
to the Court, or arbitration must be ruled
out as a futile experiment, and the weary
old scrap with the industrial bludgeon be
resumed. In the midst of a general strike
the thing looks like something that had
passed under a locomotive; and this paper
can conceive of no possible change which
would make arbitration worth thinking
about if strikes “because of quarrels with
Governments on economic questions” are to
be substituted for strikes against industrial
conditions. The loophole would give the
promoters of isolated strikes and general
stoppages a legal protection for the delivery
of whatever assaults they pleased on the
public. The economic position of the
workers may before long be horribly bad,
and it would be a cruel thing if. through
the misguidance of their new leaders, they
had no tribunal to fall back on. Yet if
strikes and filibustering against the State
are to be the order of the day with Labor —
well, the place for Arbitration Courts, as
they are known to-day, is the museum.
Who Shall Govern this Country?
Amidst all the wild confusion of the hour
it is at least clear that the industrial dis-
turbance in N. S. Wales is the cause of
great public inconvenience, national loss,
private embarrassment and social bitterness.
The vital question is, How can the plague
be stopped? It is quite within the compe-
tence of the Government to end the conflict
ro-morrow. It could start the railways going
full speed ahead, turn white the multitude
of articles now regarded as black, and give
the thousands of country-men toiling on the
wharves a chance to go home—all by pub-
licly making a short speech on these lines: —
The thing has gone far enough. Time-cards will
not be introduced into the State workshops. Every
man’s job is open to him. Let all go back.
The result would be the end of the strike,
three cheers for the Government, and the
resumption of business as usual. It looks
such a simple thing to say, too, and the
Government is being abused by the
stage-managers of the strike for not
saying it, and reproached by members
of the Legislature who depend on the
votes given by selection committees for
their jobs. Yet these people know that the
Government dare not say it. The pur-
pose of the strike is not to keep time-cards
out: of the workshops, but to determine
whether the Government under pressure
created by the capture of the transport ser-
vices can be compelled to cave in. And the
issue there is not whether there should be
industrial order, but wdiether there should
be any government in the sense in which
it is known to the Australian people. No
talk about “misunderstandings” and “intro-
duction of sweating methods” and all the
rest of it can get away from that. The strike
promoters have known what they were doing
at every stage of the game. Out of their
own mouths they stand convicted of launch-
ing what they call “the class war.” From
their prints and the proceedings of their con-
ferences and by the mental paralysis that has
seized their Parliamentary nominees there
is overwhelming testimony of intention to
challenge the right of popular government
held by the Australian people and vest it in
union delegates. And it is because the chal-
lenge is made by this strike that no Govern-
ment, unless prepared to betray the people’s
freedom—to sell the hard-won rights of democ-
racy to govern itself —could surrender or
even think about it. It is not peace or even
a reversion to previous conditions that the
State would gain by going backwards; for
however much the rest of the community
might be anxious for this, the propagandists
of “the class war” are out after something
very different. What they aspire to is the
control of the State. The vision is of govern-
ment by committees and cliques, as far re-
moved from democratic control as the Grand
I.lama and the Great Mogul—the dream of
foreign peoples confronted with no alterna-
tive but revolution as an escape from a social
and political environment they were power-
less to otherwise influence. A few months
ago the challenge that is being made to-day
was levelled against the Commonwealth by
the coal-miners. The settlement on that occa-
sion was dictated by a cowardice almost
unbelievable. It encouraged another effort
in the same direction. If this wins through
the idea of free government functioning
through democratically-controlled institu-
tions becomes a memory. That is why the
N. S. Wales Government has no choice. It
must either live or die. and if it dies its place
must be taken by a Government that would
walk wherever what is called the industrial
arm of Labor pointed.
The Vague Demand of Tudor for
Something Cheap.
Frank Tudor, leader of the Federal Labor
party, induced a public meeting at Rich-
mond (Melb.) last week to pass a resolution
calling upon the Federal and State Govern-
ments “to deal with the ever-increasing cost
of living.” As a preliminary, Tudor com-
plained of the price of meat and hosiery and
of the rise in rents. Wherefore it was, in
his judgment, time for the people to “wake
up” and for Governments to “do something.”
And the people who listened to him, feeling
the burden of prices heavy upon them, said
“Hear, hear!” and gave three cheers for
doing something unspecified. Practically all
Tudor did was to point in detail to some of
the rises that had taken place. Anybody can do
that. What neither Tudop, nor anyone else
has yet succeeded in doing is to produce an
agreeable process by which rents, imported
commodities and articles of local production
can be reduced in price. They simply dance
round the subject and leave it where they
found it. Take rent. This can be reduced
by (1) sweeping away all duties on material
of every kind, manufactured or in the raw,
used in construction; and (2) placing a
heavy tax on unimproved land values. That
this would reduce rent is certain. Does
Tudor propose that these steps should be
taken? If not, what are his alternative
methods? Or take meat and wheat. The
Government could compel every landowner
to produce so many sheep and cattle and
swine each year and sow down so many
acres in cereals, and hand over a propor-
tion of his produce to the Government at a
price fixed on the basis of the consumers’
requirements and abilities to pay. If Tudor
recommends that this should be done he
ought to say so. If he knows any other
way he should explain what it is. Should
he agree that price can only be controlled if
production is controlled, he must grapple
with the difficulties in front of him, and not
pretend there are none. Then, as he yearns
for cheap hosiery and hats, and so on, it is
no good trying to gull the public that start-
ing State workshops is going to lead to
“cheapness,” whatever else may be achieved.
The certain method of securing cheap socks
and hats is to throw the tariff overboard.
That will secure cheapness without a doubt.
Would Tudor support the Fusion if it
moved that way? If not, how does he pro-
pose to go about the business of cheapening
hats and socks? The Bulletin agrees that
the rent charge on the Australian citizen is
as a general thing outrageous; but it knows
of no single step taken by the party which
Tudor leads to lift the burden. On the con-
trary, it knows of dozens of things done by
Labor Ministries to help push rent upwards
by inflating land values. But, while The
Bulletin agrees that high rents are no good
to the Australian worker, it does not agree
that the prevalence of cheap goods in Aus-
tralia would be an advantage to him, or that
there is any practical method of providing
cheap legs of mutton unless the State pro-
ceeds to control production. If Tudor
imagines that this latter can be done in five
minutes he is a trustful person.

The Neutral Cloak.
The Commonwealth Customs have lately
been confronted with some of their own fig-
ures in proof of the contention that German
goods are still coming to this market. Pre-
vious to the war imports of gas-mantles
from Holland, Italy and Switzerland were
nil, but during 1916 nearly 300,000 were
shipped to Victoria alone, a vast majority
coming from Holland, where they had
apparently been re-packed, or had been
manufactured by uewly-established German
firms or by Dutch manufacturers who had
procured most of the material from friend
Fritz. The evidence in a case tried by the
City of London Court in November last
year was conclusive as to the fraudulency of
most of the Dutch Consignments of gas-
mantles passed by the English consul as
bona fide local manufactures before being
shipped to Britain. False certificates of
origin are easily obtained by trickery from
Consuls, and even a witness for the British
Home Office told the Salford Court last
December that “such certificates could not
be taken as absolute proof that the goods
referred to were not of enemy origin.” If,
then, Government experts condemn the gas-
mantles shipped to Britain from neutral
countries for British home use. what can be
said for those shipped from Holland and
elsewhere, via British ports, for British
Dominions, which are not subject to any
special examination? Victoria, containing
less than a fourth of the Commonwealth
population, imported 300,000 gas-mantles
from foreign countries last year. For the
present such goods must continue to be im-
ported from somewhere, for the only local
factory in operation before the war depended
on the importation of German material, and
is now out of the running. The natural
question is: Why not prohibit all importa-
tion of gas-mantles from “neutral” coun-
tries? Soon after Italy had ceased to be
neutral mantles ceased to arrive here from
that country—a fact which speaks for itself.
There are at least seven companies making
the necessary article in Great Britain, and
they are fully equal to supplying all de-
mands from this hemisphere. Eight months
ago the Maoriland Government practically
prohibited the importation of foreign mantles
into the Dominion. But Prime Minister
Hughes, despite his Win-the-War protesta-
tions, refuses to follow the Maoriland lead.
He continues to speak of British consular
certificates as guarantees of the origin of
the goods certified, notwithstanding the ad-
mission of the Home Office representative
in an English court. It is his only excuse
for not shutting out the foreign gas-mantles,
and it doesn't ring true. Excuses offered
by William: Morris Hughes rarely do
ring true.
Victimisation.
The loud plea of the present strikers that
there shall be no victimisation after the
men who down tools have done everything
they know to victimise the public raises the
whole question of rewards and punishments.
One of the prime curses of our political life
has been that men guilty of serious offences
against the public good, of gross mismanage-
ment of public estate and public credit, and
of shameful neglect in carrying out policy
measures which the public really demanded,
have been allowed to carry on without any
sort of punishment. If the same thing is
to be allowed everywhere and under all
circumstances, it is not very difficult to see
the pass that the country must come to.
Through leniency in the past with regard
to the breakers of industrial agreements
Labor legislation became a byword and a
thing of scorn ; and a continuance and exten-
sion of that leniency seems likely to plaster
contempt in large letters of mud over the
face of Australia. In very many previous
strikes the men who have come in to help
Governments and the public out of their
difficulties have had scanty reward for their
services. The strikers have returned proudly
to their employment after a noisy holiday,
and the other fellows who toiled and bore
abuse in the time of stress have been pushed
to the wall. Admitted that generosity is a
good thing and nothing is to be gained by
making martyrs; yet there ought to be
some system and good faith about the gener-
osity. ' Punishment is regarded by the best
moralists as practically useless; but there
is a difference between punishing the guilty
and rewarding him for his guilt at the ex-
pense of someone who was not only not
guilty but worthy of reward. Strikes punish
the strikers themselves, but they punish the
public far more; and while nobody wants
the public to be vindictive, it has at least
a right to so adjust things when the trouble
is over that it won’t be again at the mercy
of those who gave it no sort of consideration
in trying to serve their own ends. It is
almost an axiom in the ranks of Labor
generally that nothing serious happens to
the striker; and it is this more than any-
thing else that makes striking so easy and
union leadership the reckless thing it is.
If the public once shows itself in earnest
about the matter and without victimising
the striker insists that its own friends in
the hour of trial are not victimised for the
striker’s benefit, the whole aspect of union-
ism might change in an hour. Then the
worker would seriously consider what he
had to win by downing tools, knowing that
in the event of faibire he had a lot to lose.

“Fax” : I know nothing of (he Sydney strike
from the local Labor point of view, but I
do happen to know how the matter strikes
one young Melbourne milk-carter in the
South Yarra district. He was filling my
milk-can when I broached the question of
the threatened strike of carters. “No more
milk, no bread —no anything?” said I. “No,”
said young Milk-o. I timidly suggested that
life would be sadder without those things.
He answered more in pity than in anger:
“Well, it’s the public’s own fault for turning-
down the Labor party last election. They
arst for it.” I didn’t tell him that person-
ally I wouldn’t suffer if all the trams and
trains stopped running and the normal food
supply were cut off—l could easily walk to
and from town and live on sardines and
tame vegetables. I merely looked at the
youth and wondered whether his notion of
“the public” extended to those who didn't
help to turn down the party at last election.
Evidently his prescription for the cure of
all political complaints was Complete Rest
from Work. Milk-0, you’ll observe, made no
reference whatever to card systems—none
to capital and labor, work and wages, em-
ployers and employed. Simply “the public”
had fairly earned a clout on the head for vot-
ing one way instead of the other. As a voter
for Labor at last election I am to be pun-
ished for the sins of my proud and haughty
neighbors. The bread of adversity and the
milk of human unkindness will be denied
Marching Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Miners and others on strike will start on a route
march to Sydney to-morrow.”
We’re marching on, we’re marching on,
though no one knows quite where.
Our boots are on the South Coast road, our
banners in the air;
But what the deuce it’s all about we neither
know nor care..
We will not work, we will not work; our
tools, you note, are down.
The mines are left behind us and the pleasant
beer is brown ;
And anything may happen on the dusty trail
to town.
We do not care, we do not care. We will not
own defeat.
Without a thought the miles go by beneath
our feet.
Who troubles for a place to sleep or any-
thing to eat?
We’re moving on. we’re moving on, though
no one knows quite where.
In Hunger Park the grass is scorched with
blasts of heated air—
But curse the “rats” who try to think and
damn the “scabs” who care !
N.S.W.

and Save 10s.
The practice of using such habit-forming opium extracts as laudanum, paregoric,
morphia, etc., for the home-making of cough mixture cannot be too strongly dis-
couraged. Their frequent use does untold harm, especially to children of tender
years. Besides dulling the brain, opium preparations cause constipation, one of the
greatest sources known of human suffering. The discovery of HEENZO (registered
name for Hean’s Essence) has made the use of all harmful drugs entirely unneces-
sary. By its aid anyone may, in a few moments, prepare a pint of the finest-quality
cough, cold, croup and sore-throat mixture it is possible to have. There is certainly
no other medicine which acts more speedily in the loosening of phlegm, soothing and
healing inflamed mucous membrane, and allaying bronchial distress. Besides being
so good medicinally, HEENZO (Hean’s Essence) is a great money-saver. A pint
of ordinary cough mixtures, put up in a lot of little bottles, would cost at least 12/-.
HEENZO costs only 2/-, thus effecting a clear cash-saving on each bottle used of
10/-, a big consideration these times. If your chemist or store cannot supply
HEENZO (Hean’s Essence) you may obtain it direct from the manufacturer,
G. lIEAN. Chemist. 178 Castlereagh Street, Sydney. In addition to being such
a wonderful cough cure, when diluted, HEENZO, used full strength, quickly stops
toothache, earache, stomach cramps and diarrhoea. For this reason many people get
two bottles, one of which they make up ready for use when coughs and colds come
along, and the other they hold undiluted for use as occasion may arise.
