Since tearing a hole in the Russian front
southwards, the German seems to have taken
a spell in that quarter. But some-
thing may be heard from further north
in a day or two. What is already known
is that Fritz has been busy in the direction
of Riga, and that there have been Russian
withdrawals. A substantial Kerman success
in that region would probably make them
masters of all Western Russia and even
place Petrograd within their grasp. The
meaning of this would be that the war must
be won on the Western Front or not at all.
There are said to be hopes of Ivan finding
his punch again. But there is no evidence
so far to show that he is not involved in a
greater chaos each succeeding week, or to
justify optimistic anticipations about the
morrow. Until someone is able to estab-
lish at least the figment of authority and the
Russian arms are stiffened, the Kaiser will
have less to worry him from that direction
than the rest of the world counted on.
Nevertheless, he is getting quite enough to
think over in France and Flanders, for
there, although it cannot be said that the
Allies are definitely in charge of the situa-
tion, they are giving Von Hindenburg a
severe mauling. At Verdun the French ha.ve
hit back with disastrous consequences to the
vanity of William junior, and at Lens and
north of Ypres a bloody toll is being taken
from the invaders. There the Allies will
certainly resume operations after the winter
stronger than they were before, for the
United States will be in the fray by then
and the mauling of the German will be
resumed with fresh claws at the old address.
A good many weeks ago a Bulletin corres-
pondent declared that ships filled with flour
are practically unsinkable. He suggested
experiments. For his own part he had filled
tins, boxes and bags with flour, thrown them
into water, fired expanding bullets into them,
and found that they remained afloat. In
view of (1) the dangerous shortage of food-
stuffs in Britain, (2) the surplus of food-
stuffs in Australia, and (3) the destruction
of food-ships by German submarines, the
matter seems just about as important as
any possibly could be. But when it was
brought under the notice of the Federal
Government, the real point was overlooked,
and the letter-writer was informed that “the
question of shipping flour instead of wheat
to the British Government has already re-
ceived special attention, with the result that
it has been arranged that subject to ship-
ping facilities flour will be dispatched to the
full extent that it is available.” If the theory
is good, national safety requires us. not to
ship flour as part of a general cargo
—nothing is gained by that —but to
ship complete cargoes of flour. Five hun-
dred tons of flour in a general cargo of 5000
tons can unquestionably be sunk ; the enor-
mously important claim is that a ship loaded
wholly with flour is, for all practical pur-
poses, unsinkable.
From the Reps. Gallery:—
Bill Higgs was suspended last week, and for one
brief, unbelievable moment we thought he would
stand his ground and compel Clapin, the small Ser-
geant-at-Arms, to evacuate him forcibly. We had
gorgeous visions of the undergrown Clapin trying to
fold up about seven feet of perversity into a handy
parcel. But Higgs denied us that ultimate ecstasy.
Still, one lives in hopes, and there are reckless
optimists among the press gang who expect that one
day something large like Forrest or Page will
become horribly peevish and confront Clapin with
the old problem of the irresistible atom versus the
immovable mammoth. Clapin’s size is really a
menace to the peace and security of the House. Per-
sonally I think he is Irish enough to welcome any
diversion as a relief from the childishness of some of
his duties. His principal task is to jump down
from his perch and remove the mace from the table
every time the Speaker leaves the chair, with the
result that in a rush of resolutions, when Speaker
Johnson is travelling constantly between his throne
and the front Ministerial bench, Clapin is expected
to be a sort of jack-in-the-box. Really he is much
too expensive and intelligent to be employed in this
way. His office ought to be abolished.
The long-suspended question of a Coali-
tion Government in South Aus. lias been
settled at last —after portion of this week’s
Bulletin had made an early strike-time
rush to the machines. Peake throws over-
board Ritchie, Gordon and Barwell of his
own team and takes in Blundell. Jackson and
Styles—three of the Vaughan six whom he
lately turned out of office. Knowing nothing
of law he becomes Attorney-General himself.
The other most curious feature of the part-
nership is the disappearance of Crawford
Vaughan himself. It is understood that he
does not care to sit in office with a crowd
which is making a stern investigation into
the land-purchases of his late Ministry. His
brother, recently Attorney-General, has en-
listed; and his brother-in-law, Clarence
Goode, is the other ex-Minister missing. The
coalition is a distinct score for the Adver-
tiser, which has all along pressed it as the
decent thing. The Register treats it as a
grab for office by a crowd lately ejected from
it. The Herald says in effect, “A plague
o’ both your Houses! Give us a general
election, and see how many ‘National’ Labor
men will be left.”
Some person, apparently rendered quite
insane by a bite of the Yellow Pup, writes to
the press to suggest that there is some con-
nection between the Sinn Feiners in Ireland
and the strikers in Australia. The national
Irish movements, first and last, have been a
struggle for the recognition of the rights of
Irishmen to work. Irish industries, where
they entered into competition with English
and sometimes where they didn’t, were sup-
pressed by English law ; and even the culti-
vation of the soil was for long hardly per-
mitted to the people who tried to live on it.
Against these enforced conditions of idleness
the people strove. Sometimes there was
rebellion; always there was political agita-
tion ; and gradually a little progress was
made. The Sinn Fein movement, whatever
its faults, came into being through the fixed
belief of a lot of disappointed Irishmen that
the milder political parties would never
force from England a recognition of the
rights of the nation to work to the best ad-
vantage. Anybody who can see in such a
movement anything resembling a general
strike must be strangely constructed from
the ears up.
It is always the same when the politician
sets out on a policy of land purchase. In
S. Aus. it is the late Crawford Vaughan
Government that is now bitterly assailed.
A. T. Saunders an old enthusiast in land-tax
matters, is the most forthright, and lets go
in this fashion :
Here is a direct charge. [Dates and figures of
a Government land-purchase given in detail.] Mala
fides or gross if not criminal negligence, careless-
ness, absence of foresight or business incapacity is
evident on the part of Ministry, some Minister or
Ministers, or some civil servant or civil servants,
or of some of all mentioned. I defy Mr. Vaughan
to publish the valuation of the 400 acres for pro-
bate. I defy him to procure and publish a report
from the Land Tax Commissioner on his valuation
of the land.
But what is the smell going to be like after
the politicians have finished with Millen’s
“repatriation” scheme, which is mostly a
scheme for the purchase of land before the
war taxes get properly to work?

“Centurion”: “Karee” (B. 16/8/T7) is
firing at a mirage when he says that the
discharge of “cashiered officers and other
undesirables” is marked “good.” An officer,
cashiered or not, never gets a discharge of
any sort.
Harry Septimus Badgery, who died at
Exeter (N.S.W.) the other day at 77, was
one of 12 locally-born brothers. Only one is
left. Henry Badgery, the father, was the
pioneer settler of Sutton Forest. He went
there in 1822 with Dr. Charles Throsby,
who, with Hamilton Hume, first explored
the Bong Bong country. Son Harry, after
taking up land, was hard hit by drought, and
in 1864 started to get busy with an auction-
eer’s hammer and proved one of the best
stock salesmen that N. S. Wales ever nodded
a bid to. In 1869 he married Julia,
daughter of G. M. Pitt, another stoeksales-
man, and they formed the firm of Pitt, Son
and Badgery. In 1878 Badgery entered
Parliament as member for Maitland, and in
1880 and 1882 he was elected for Monaro.
In 1885 he had a portfolio in the short-lived
Dibbs Government as Minister for Works,
but at the general election following he was
beaten for Camden and retired from politics.
.T. P. Ford, killed in action the other day
at Messines, was one of the earliest to enlist
in Sydney, and left as bombardier in the
first contingent which sailed to argue the
point in Europe. He wore a sergeant’s
stripes at the Landing; and though later on
wounded and out of action for a while, he
was back again in time for the evacuation.
In France he won a lieutenant’s stars at the
Somme, and was a second time w r ounded at
Lagnicourt, where his name figured for gal-
lantry in Haig’s dispatches. Ford was an
old boy of the Christian Brothers’ school at
Waverley, and was a railway clerk before
enlisting.
Died at Pinjarrah (W.A.). the other day,
aged 69, Edward McLarty, pioneer pastoral-
ist and agriculturist. lie was for many
years member for the South-West Province
in the Leg. Council, but retired at last elec-
tion. There was no keener judge of stock
in the State.

At Melbourne King’s the attraction of
classic drama with pretty trimmings is
fetching the public that numbers Benedick,
Beatrice and Dogberry among its acquaint-
ances, or knows something of them by
repute. “Much Ado,” which bettered intel-
ligent expectation on the first night, has
settled down into a smooth and easy per-
formance all along the line, excepting the
three unintersting watchmen who effect a
tame capture of Borachio and Conrad, and
would like to take out a warrant for the
arrest of that “deformed thief Fashion.”
The spokesman for the Watch offers no per-
sonal suggestion of the sort of numbskulls
he and his mates are supposed to be, and
the arrest of the two drunks is the worst-
treated incident in the play. Elsewhere
the caviller finds that lan Maclaren and
Kay Souper talk a trifle too rapidly at

loud he has the same tone for maundering
as for drivelling. But these matters are
very small flies in the ointment. “Much

show, stuffed with honorable virtues, as the
well-spoken Messenger remarks of the lead-
ing bachelor. Every man is a bachelor
widower in this artless narrative seem
ingly; every girl emphasises the fact that
she is a maid. Matrons are conspicuouslv
absent, and for the giddiest sort of spinster
there is no admission. Balthazar th
sweet singer, is, by the way, a 'she-bov
whose misspelt name turns out to be Twhetf

The Royal Comics are beginning to pass
away from Melbourne Majesty’s in a brief
series of weekly revivals. Last Saturday
brought “Belle of New York” back to its
faithful admirers in much the same shape
as on a previous occasion when the little
Lunatic and Bronson Holland chased one
another round the candy store. Smith’s
version of the courteous German who car-
ries a carving-knife shares the honors on
the male side of the cast with the Kenneth
Mugg of Yilliers Arnold. lie is rather
different from a number of previous Polite
Lunatics, and his restraint is a change from
the usual Phil Smith. Florence Young
makes the sort of Cora Angelique who would
never abandon her prey until impecuniosity
threatened him ; Minnie Love is a Fifi who
combines the tenderness of a dove with the
clingsomeness of a limpet; Gladys llon-
crieff’s fitness for the name character, apart
from her pure and innocent voice, lies in
the fact that when she pretends to be a wild
Violet the pretence is very amateurish;
and Nellie Payne is an even sweeter Mamie
Clancy than before —so sweet that the Order
of Larrikinesses ought to present her with a
testimonial for idealising the type. Next
Saturday a revival of “The Merry Widow,”
with Minnie Love as Sonia.

The threat of a sugar famine in Melbourne
can have no terrors for sentimental house-
wives as long as “Daddy Long Legs” is on
sale at the Royal. People who carry boxes
of sweets to the “Daddy” performance would
be capable of taking coals to Newcastle.

Two or three new turns at Melbourne
Tivoli have ousted the best of the old turns
from their pre-eminence. The artists that
flavor their feats of muscle with essence of
humor are still on top, Ruth Budd being a
notably nice young thing in mixed enter-
tainment. Ruth is the angel whose limbs
recite the poetry of motion while her face
sings snatches -of song as she hangs topsy-
turvy in mid air.

this week. The violently 7 pro-German clan

by the success of the Russian boys who first
came here in velvet knickers; but, seeing
that no famous composer—not even Brahms

“Turn to the Right” is still turning that
way at Adelaide Royal. It seems to be a
modern American development of the old
Robertsonian style of play, featuring the
simple delights of a pump with real water and
a kettle with real edible preserves. Richard-
son, Kent and Junior do all the work, with
Margaret Calvert as everybody’s mother.
Gaston Mervale has been ill, but is coming
back into the co. At the Tivoli, the Ander-
son Co. has left Illyria (which seems to be
somewhere close to Anthony Hope’s Ruri-
tania) for Naples. Here —in “The Sailor’s
Wedding”—a section of the British Navy
drops in to sample the daggers and spaghetti
and Camorra and other local products.
Prince’s Players are merry at the Majestic,
where also the Hamptons strive to persuade
the public that something new still remains
to be done on wheels. The Queen’s —a house
getting a great vogue for this class of work
—has had all it could do to hold the people
w r anting to revel in the music of the three
Cherniavskys and the Scotney-White pair.
Mr. White now adds ’cello solos to his
vocalism. The most striking picture in sight
is “The Libertine” at Wondergraph. It
opens up the whole question whether a girl
should be told everything, and has led to the
usual newspaper correspondence. Phil
Pinkelstein is manager. He is an old press
hand.
Bigjohn Forrest estimates that his 10 per
cent, tax on unmarried eligibles, with a £lO
minimum, will yield only £500,000. What
becomes, then, of Hughes’s recent estimate
that there are still 150,000 eligible single
men in the Commonwealth? The further
they go the more evidence these Fusionists
supply that they have no sense of responsi-
bility.
Killed in France, Major Horace Kings-
mill, D. 5.0., formerly of the Commercial
Banking Co., Sydney. He was a lieutenant
in the Australian Artillery when the sparks
flew from the Devil’s anvil, and he went to
Gallipoli with the first contingent. After
that, France, where he won his decoration
for gallantry as observation officer. Three
brothers have commissions in the A.I.F. Dad
is clerk of petty sesions at Albury (N.S.W.).
“Huz” : Passed out recently at Melbourne
Hospital, Elizabeth Amelia Henrietta
Somers Cocks. She passed her 70-odd years
at Sandridge (Port Melbourne), and met
romance at 21, when she was courted by
one John Patrick Somers Cocks, an English
gent, then on a visit to Australia. In the
Cocks family is the Somers baronetcy with
a fat estate and rent roll, not to mention a
castle in an English western county. The
lovers were married in ’77 at Sandridge, and
the bridegroom returned to his native land
soon after. The unsophisticated bride seems
to have had a dread of ‘"Home” sassiety,
for she could not be prevailed upon to leave
Australia ; and hubby died within the next few
years without having been reunited to his
wife, who continued to live as best she could
at the old address. The marriage is regis-
tered in Melbourne, though “Burke’s Peer-
age” records that “J. P. Somers Cocks died
unmarried in 1879.”
“Filkoil”: The highest professorial autho-
rity in Australia on the I.W.W. or its Euro-
pean progenitor, the syndicalist movement, is
Edward Shann, of Perth Uni. The dapper
little prof, is a Victorian and took part m
many team debates with the late J. A.
Arthur, Bendigo’s ex-Labor representative,
when the latter was the moving spirit on the
democratic side in Melbourne Uni. Shann
spent some time in France and tackled the
whole trade-unionist movement from top to
bottom. Tennis is his only vice.

“P.”: Versatile Alf Stephens, the old
Pollard comedian, has been killed in France.
He w r as a Melbourne native, who graduated
with the Liliputians, making big hits in the
early ’nineties in “Rip Van Winkle,” “Oliv-
ette,” “La Mascotte,” etc. Later on
he scored as the old smuggler in “Paul
.Jones” and as Bronson in “The Belle of
New York.” He also played the polite lunatic
with the Royal Comics about 1907 and made
a last appearance in these parts with the
Wren-Musgrove co. in the following year.

“Gazer” : Time the bell rang to knock off
the bad habit of crediting a ring in cattiness
to women. At Balboni’s Melbourne opening
concert the Auditorium was a nest of rival
tenors. After a preliminary look round, the
one on show became as nervous as a tyro.
He went pale, grabbed the accompanist’s
chair, leaned over and looked at the music
as often as he could (he knew it backwards,
but anything to dodge for a second the bat-
tery of rival tenor eyes!), got as far behind
the piano-lid as possible, and altogether
acted like a musical debutant. Spite of this
he was a success. A modest, nervous tenor
is a rare sight, and arouses rare enthusiasm.
At the second concert the tenor battalions
were conspicuous by their absence. Balboni
was another man. In a flash he recovered
his old aplomb, and was as easy in his
manner as the steadiest old stager.
“E.T.”: “Mary Goes First” is not to be
H. A. Jones’s only last play. Henry is just
producing in England an up-to-date “parable
in farce” called “The Pacifists,” in which he
attempts to show what happens in the usual
provincial town when its inhabitants pursue
the principle of peace-at-any-price in their
municipal and domestic affairs. Sounds
promising.
Maoriland continues to strip itself of non-
essentials: Picture shows are the latest to
be marked “not essential.” This doesn’t
mean that they are to be closed up immedi-
ately, but that the snuffer may be put over
them at the discretion of the Government.
Somerset Maugham, known to Australian
playgoers by “Lady Frederick,” “Jack
Straw,” “The Laud of Promise” and
“Penelope,” got married in N’York the other
day to a daughter of Dr. Barnardo, of the
Homes. Maugham is, among other things,
a surgeon. He served at the Front until his
health gave way; then he went to N’Yark,
where' his latest comedy, “Our Betters,” is
now booming.
Marie Lloyd has been having more trouble
Avith her third husband, the jockey Bernard
Dillon, who visited her house when
in liquor and assaulted the police who
came in answer to her shrieks for
help. His witnesses to character could
only say that he was an angel when
sober, but the other thing when he wasn’t.
Robert Sievier attended the court to take
Dillon in hand, pledging himself that the
jockey (who is now in the army) would
keep off the drink; but there was another
charge of being an absentee from his corps,
and the magistrate thought it all worth a
month’s hard labor.
“S.S.”: The Melbourne performance of
“Much Ado,” an interesting one in many
ways, goes to strengthen an old idea of mine
that the attempt to dress a Shakspearean
play with historical exactitude is a mistake.
The tendency of stage managers is not
merely to give what men wore in the
days represented in the costume play, but to
give all they wore. Now, not three actors in
50 can bear themselves like men in wholly
unaccustomed clothes, and even if they can
swank in the glad rags of a long bygone
generation, there is another three-to-one
chance that their physical imperfections—
woefully apparent in such deeds —will undo
them. Why should not the effort be to mini-
mise the sartorial mischief, while retaining
the necessai*y atmosphere? Instead of piling
on wigs, hats, plumes, cloaks and laces with
reckless prodigality, cut out every feather,
and every puff and frill and effeminate hat,
likewise the superfluity of boot, and let as
much as possible of the man appear ! If the
producer at the King’s had discarded three
large bales of Avigs, hats, beards, gaudy un-
necessary garments and unsexing flounces,
“Much Ado” would have seemed a much
cleaner, neater, more compact and incisive
entertainment.
Irma Caron’s view of it: —
“Stargazer’s” opinion (B. 16/8/’l7) that “Gilber-
tian effects are secured by the employment of delib-
erate, extravagant and wanton contrast between the
man and his spoken thought” suggests burlesque
rather than satire. Between the two a line must
be drawn. Surely Gilbert’s wit and cunningly
sugared home-truths are sufficient to ensure success
without the exponent assuming a pantomimically
grotesque appearance! How would “Stargazer s
rule of “deliberate, extravagant and wanton con-
trast” work out if applied to poor Jack Point, the
broken-hearted jester of “Yeoman of the Guard”?
Wirths’ Circus is .in trouble over the
strike, and has had to down tents and pay
off artists. The show is held up at Brisbane.
“Roland A.”: Oh, these Yankee over-
actresses! Saw one of them in “Bella
Donna” the other night just after I had
enjoyed the quiet artistry of John Hare and
co. in “The Vicar of Wakefield.” The con-
trast hurt. The end of “Bella Donna” is
sufficiently tragic to go without over-empha-
sis, but the Yank lady or her bawling
director couldn’t realise it. When Bella,
chucked by her aAvakened husband and
sacked by "her Egyptian lover, opened the
little gate leading to the desert, she started
to express her emotion by staggering like
a groggy pugilist. And she went on doing
it until' I quite expected a policeman to
flicker into view and arrest her for drunk
and disorderly.
“J.F.H.”: Londoners are wondering
whether Melba’s magnificent voice will ever
echo again around Covent Garden. That
famous home of the lyric drama is still com-
mandeered by the Government as a war-time
warehouse, and it is regarded as ominous
that Melba has given up housekeeping in
London. Lady Sarah Wilson moved
into the gorgeous mansion in Great Cumber-
land Place where her friend formerly dis-
pensed lavish hospitality and gave birthday
parties that made 3mur head swim. But
though Melba is absent, Australian operatic
talent is admirably represented at the
Shaftesbury by Beatrice Miranda, the prima
donna of the Carl Rosa co., who is singing
splendidly in Italian operas. Opera also
reigns at Drury Lane, where Sir Thomas
Beecham is doing the thing regardless of
expense. Maorilander Rosina Buckman is
his bright, particular star in “Tannhauser”
and “Tristan and Isolde.”
“Mooltan”: For the quick-change compe-
tition I nominate the Allen and May Opera
Co., which came to Calcutta in the ’7o’s;
and as it was the first co. to play in English
it was well received. It opened in October,
and put on a complete change every week
for over four months—“Maritana,” “Bohe-
mian Girl,” “Rose of Castile,” “Girofle-
Girofla,” “Martha,” “Madam Angot,” “The
Grand Duchess,” “Daughter of the Regi-
ment,” and many others. The co. was
largely Australian, and included Alice May,
Maggie Liddle, Florence Howe, Fanny
Lester, Amy and Alice Johns, Henry Hal-
lam, Howard Vernon, Tom Rainford, T. J.
Wilkinson, Bates Brown, Charles Lester
and (I think) C. H. Templeton. G. B.
Allen was conductor, and Charley McMahon
callboy. The late F. H. Pollock was in
front of the house; there was an efficient
chorus and a fine orchestra, and as a touring
operatic organisation it would even now be
hard to beat.
THE TAITS.
The veteran John Douglass is the first at
all well-known Londoner to be killed in an
air-raid. He used to be manager of the old
Standard theatre, was author of many tre-
mendous melodramas, and was chiefly proud
of having been the first to introduce the tank
or real-water effect on the stage. He was a
well-preserved 74 when the bomb found him.
The critic in reply :
Last week’s outburst from "The Cracked Tenor”
suggests that he is very cracked, poor fellow! He
is appalled at the thought of the Cherniavskys
being mentioned in the same breath with the Ver-
brugghen Quartet, and accuses the critic of imply-
ing that there are “people in Melbourne who con-
sider the music of the Cherniavskys preferable to
that of Verbrugghen and his colleagues.” Let
me say in the first place that no comparison was
drawn between the scholastic chamber-music played
by the expert quartet party and the more florid
classical music with which the trio of young
Russians appeal to the “moderately musical” pub-
lic, whose name is less numerous than legion. As
for “Cracked Tenor’s” affected amazement at the
existence of people who prefer richly-colored melody
to Beethoven quartet strains —well, he can find
hundreds of thousands in Melbourne, Sydney, or
any other big city outside of Germany who prefer
a tinkling music-hall song-tune to a Wagnerian
operatic number, and are positively bored by sugary
Italian opera because they “don’t understand the
language.” These are the people whom it were
desirable to educate in a moderately musical taste,
by means of cheap orchestral concerts and limited
instrumental concerts such as the Cherniavskys sup-
ply. We want all people to be moderately musical
and intelligently artistic in all respects, so that
they may be able to appreciate the performances of
immoderately musical and artistic professional ex-
perts. But we don’t want them all to be masters
or earnest scholars of any art —especially the art
of music, whose professors are about the most
jealous, envious and intolerant lot of souls on
God’s earth. Germany is immensely more musical
than Australia or any English-speaking country,
yet the result is a bad advertisement for music.
It hasn’t tended to soothe the nation’s savage
breast.

THE POET’S LYRE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No longer are heard in C. J. Dennis’s mountain
home at Toolangi, to which he has just taken his
bride, the strains of the banjo of his bachelorhood.
He made the instrument himself —of native black-
wood, gafivanised-iron, the skin of a cat and the
sinews of a wallaby. Let it sound its own requiem:
Just like the ’arp that onst on Tara’s wall
’Ung dumb as steak inside a butcher’s shop,
I am the ghost of this ’ere festival:
It ain’t no cop
For me, what Low onst put in a cartoon ;
I’m second fiddle in this ’oneymoon.
The dinkum days that ’im an’ me ’ave spent!
Though sooperseded by a gramophone,
I never tumbled it was permanent —
That on me own
I’d lag soo-blanky-perfluous on the stage,
A. silent oracle, like last week’s Age.
’Struth, it’s enough to make me whip the cat
’Oose skin was tanned me apron for to make,
To think I should be outed—on me pat.
It takes the cake!
Though / can’t play no “Chanson Sans Paroles”
Songs without words is surgin’ through me soul!
Songs without words! Ar, they was good ole times
W’en songs ’ad words —it seems but yesterday
’ls fingers on my strings thrummed out the rhymes
(And ’e could play!)
That were to bring ’im fame in “Ginger Mick.”
’E scooped the pool—’twas me that took the trick !
I scanned ’is lines for ’im without a ’itch ;
To me ’e owes the metre of ’is verse;
Ar, crooil fate wot narked my concert-pitch!
It makes me curse
The luck that turned, the forehune that betrayed,
My maker, an’ the day that I was made.
Alas, no more may sound an’ strings combine
As in the days ere I was carst aside;
Yet I am something more than tin an’ twine —■
I ’ave me pride;
An’ ’e don’t recognise, by word or sign,
The mess ’e’s made o’ this ’ere life o’ mine.
Yet, callin’ back the old familiar tunes
That galvanised the iron in my frame
(Now silent, spare me days, for many moons),
I’d do the same
As I ’ave done, an’ all my soul evoke
To re-inspire “The Sentimental Bloke.”
The wind ’as mobs o’ trees fer its delight,
But only one was chosen for ’is lyre:
Mine was the wood ’e tipped would best ignite
The poet’s fire ;
And of my fuel, may this coal glow red
W’ich my forgiveness ’eaps upon ’is ’ead!
VICTORIA.

ALL'S NOT GOLD THAT GLITTERS.

YOUNG MRS. CASH: “Yes. I know—one

“Three Star”: When Billjim dies in
France, as much of him as can be found is
removed to the nearest military cemetery
and the Battalion padre presides. The
remains having been given decent Christian
burial, the padre forwards to the Graves
Registration people—a branch of the Army
—the number, name and rank of the de-
ceased, and the number of the grave—36 s.w.,
N 36, A4OGS, or whatever it happens to be.
For every cemetery there is also a plan, with
the numbers marked on it. The late lamented’s
people can with these aids find the exact
spot if they ever visit France to see the par-
ticular wooden cross that interests them.
Of course sometimes there’s nothing left of
Billjim to collect, and then a wooden cross
is unnecessary ; and sometimes he dies in an
inconvenient spot in No Man’s Land and
can’t be brought in, and again no wooden
cross is necessary. But in the great
majority of cases there will be no more diffi-
culty in locating him than if he were planted
in some quiet, cypress-decorated plot at
home.
“0.K.”: The case of young Grimwade, an
aviator who fell into the hands of the enemy,
and is now an injured prisoner on parole,
restricted to Switzerland, may help the aver-
age slacker to understand what Losing the
War might mean. The example is the more
striking- because the captive (son of one of the
partners in the big Felton-Grimwade druggist
firm) had sufficient social influence to pro-
cure his partial freedom eventually. When
he was first a prisoner he committed the in-
discretion of making a sarcastic reply to the
taunt of a German officer. The swanking
Hun asked him how it was that the German
navy couldn’t find the British navy in order
to bring it to battle. Grimwade curtly re-
plied that it was no use looking for it in the
Kiel Canal. For this the wounded Aus-
tralian was sentenced to two years’ im-
prisonment in a fortress. Had he been an
unfortunate Billjim from the trenches the
same natural retort would have earned him
at least a smack across the face, with pro-
bably a flogging to follow. The Hun has
little sense of humor, and none that isn’t
tinged with savagery: the military swanker
who shoulders a civilian of his own national-
ity off the footpath would kill an enemy for
cutting a joke at his expense. Yet the pros-
pect of having to live under such conditions
has no terrors for the young Australian
slackers, who can’t see beyond their dream of
triumphant Trades-Hallism. Let them take
the case of young Grimwade to bed with
them and pray God it may become a night-
mare to scare them out of their sleep.
A young reprobate writes from Bagdad:
I’ve just been reading that number of The Bulle-
tin in which “Montalex” describes this city as about
“the last word in sordid and unromantic squalor.”
Correct ; but there are two things that he forgot
to mention—-the graceful carriage of the women and
the number of vagrant, unlovely dogs. Any weights
that the women have to carry are put on their
top-pieces, and to develop gracefulness let me recom-
mend the idea to the flappers that I left behind me.
However, that’s a detail. Dogs are the important
item. How many tripehounds there are in this city
I don't know, but judging by the hell-din they raise
at night there must be close on 80,000,000. Re-
cently the heads told off a military policeman
to stalk the main street and shoot mongrels on
sight. The bag was 800. and you wouldn’t
know the difference. So-long. Don’t forget to tell
the girls to carry something solid in their —I mean,
on their heads.
* * * •
“Murkah”: American tramcars have but
one entrance, and there the conductor stands
all the time. The passengers as they file
past show their coin and dump it into a
glass receptacle. A lever tilts the floor of
the glass money-box and slides the coins into
an iron safe below. The giving of
change is discouraged. This sys-
tem, coupled with the flat-rate fare,
makes tickets superfluous. For
every passenger the conductor
operates a foot-controlled tell-tale,
which registers on a dial at the
front of the car. Therefore the
stray inspector (who need not be
in uniform) has an easy job. Such
a system makes the gathering of
fares a matter of ease and certainty.
For Australian use our section sys-
tem introduces a difficulty. But as
cars built for the Yankee manner
of fare-collecting have only two
outlets, one controlled by the driver
and the other at the conductor’s
end (used only in case of rush),
the employment of vari-colored
tickets, as at present, would make
checking out a simple matter.
Arguments that one-entrance cars
would not cope with Australian
traffic are met in advance by the statement
that the crowds of New York and Chicago
are successfully handled by them.

“T’gatta”: Defence Minister Pearce fur-
ther advertised his feebleness and the stupid-
ity of his Department the other night, when
he told a waiting audience of Tallangatta
people over the telephone that he could not
allow a touring band of disabled soldiers to
give their advertised concert. The bands-
A POPULAR LINE.
SHE: “Your son’s a contractor, isn’t he? What is his special line?”
HE: "Debts.”
men had arranged to tour N. S. Wales, and
had received a permit to do so. Being hung
up by the strike, they crossed the border in-
tending to run a few performances in Vic.
to keep themselves financial until they could
proceed with their original plans. Naturally
everybody helped; good band music isn’t
plentiful in the country, and the musicians’
pensions are small. But when the audience
turned up the announcement was made that
the State War Council had prohibited the
performance on the grounds that the men
lmd no permit to tour Victoria, and that
their performance was likely to interfere
with the recruiting band that is to tour
these parts. Then the long-distance tele-
phone handle was seized and the indignant
country folk waited for a full hour until
Pearce was “raised” at Parliament House.
He assured Tallangatta that he “could do
nothing.” The fatuous “authorities” simply
couldn’t have discovered a better way to dis-
courage recruiting in this district if they had
used their intellects on the problem for a
year. The bandsmen were given till 7
o’clock next morning to leave the town and
till 12 to leave the State! Tallangatta mur-
mured its sympathy, took up a collection to
defray the visitors’ expenses, and now goes
about asking how much grace Australia is
going to give the dullards who are responsi-
ble for such senseless persecution.

From Billjim in Palestine:—
Perhaps Abdul is running short of barbed-wire;
anyhow, he has adopted a “new” dodge, that is as
old as the Crusades, for preventing the cursed Chris-
tians from rushing his trenches. About 10 yards
in front he digs four lines of cup-shaped holes, each
4 ft. deep, and connected only by a narrow strip of
earth. These pits, by reason of the strip of earth
connection, can only be observed at close quarters,
and the result is that the charging party are
brought to a sudden halt on the edge of a network
of man-holes. Then Abdul gets in his fine work
with machine-guns, hand-grenades and trench-mortars.
Saladin, the Infidel leader, won the Crusaders’
obstacle race by bringing long planks into action.
But the modern machine-gun complicates the old
problem for us.
* * * *
William Turner, secretary of the Austin
Hospital for Incurables, has mentioned an
idea which might be a big thing for this
country if it were adequately carried out.
It is the “Listerian Home,” a non-charitable.
non-commercial establishment for women
about to become mothers, where these valu-
able citizens would receive necessary care
and instruction before, during and after the
arrival of little Bill or Jane. The distin-
guishing mark of the procedure would be
that the only account to be submitted to
little Bill s parents after the ceremony would
be for the actual cost. It is an idea which
should appeal to everybody but the doctor
and the nurse who run a private hospital
and make their profits by a low dietary and
a high fee scale. The nearest approach we
have to it is an' “Intermediate Hospital”
here and there —mostly, as at the St.
George’s, at Ivew (Vic.), with a church or
tabernacle in the background.

HIS LAZINESS THE SPRING!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His Laziness the Spring is here, that golden-featured
fellow!
He makes the cresset of the twigs a blaze of green
and yellow;
He spins a web of tender grass, and where his brown
hand flung it
He twists white daisies in and out and then lies
down among it.
His Laziness of Spring is here. Ilis active days were
over
When he pushed up through Winter's trail the nod-
ding heads of clover;
At work that shivering July, to conquer chilly
weather,
With languid cloud and drowsy sun he now makes
pact together.
His Laziness of Spring will take the effort from your
fingers
And whisper how a lilac mist about the bamboos
lingers,
Until down goes your brush or pen, and led by some
green elf
You wander through the sunlit arch to see it for
yourself!
A dainty column of sweet pea, a favor of the Spring,
Has trimmed its wire pedestal with rosy gophering,
And to and fro a spider swings, too plump and easy-
hearted
To worry that he missed the goal he aimed for when
Ire started.
A bee that meant a lily-feast declines upon the clover,
And when the spotted lady-bird who counts her
children over
Finds one is missing from its home, too indolent to
weep,
She creeps beneath her floral quilt and settles down
to sleep.
Far off, upon the rocky Point, a black-winged bird
flies slowly
Beyond the haughty coco-palms and tree-ferns squat
and lowly;
The quarry-picks clink listlessly, a sail in Garden
Reach
Seems like the flicker of a hand that beckons from a
beach ;
And bud and grass and sail and leaf and every
floating wing—
They are but courtiers to my lord His Laziness the
Spring!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

From the report of the Health Officer on
the Lower Wattle Grove (Tas.) State
school: —
The building is a disused apple-shed, built of
wood, weatherboard on the outside, paling-lined in-
side. The roof is shingle, and leaky in parts. The
schoolroom is 22ft. x 13ft. and 7ft. high. There are
two doors, which open directly to the outside. The
two windows are too small to admit sufficient light.
The chimney is of corrugated-iron and smokes badly.
There are several cracks and apertures in the walls.
There is no porch or verandah or convenience to
deposit wet garments. There is no shelter-shed.
There is no water available in the immediate vicinity.
Go on the land, Billjim. go on the land!
You’ll be marrying presently ; and then little
Billjim will be provided with a palace like
this, while the poor wretch who sticks to
the city will have to send his kids to a mere
suburban hovel that cost 20 or 30 thousand
pounds.
* * * *
“Saint”: The Ricketty Islands have
lately been getting a hint that the old devil
under them is stirring in his sleep. Through-
out the southern part of the North Islaud
frequent tremors were felt for five days. On
the S.E. coast, which was the centre of the
disturbance, hardly a chimney, was left
standing. Billiard-tables and pianos were
overturned and broken, and one. astonished
individual was thrown out of his bed and
had it overturned on top of him. Perhaps
the worst experience was that of the light-
house-keeper at Castlepoint, who felt his
70ft. tower sway to and fro, while the rocks
around him fell into the boiling sea.

“K.”: Hats off to Blogg! John K.,
chemist, of Melbourne —one of the Blogg
Brothers —has discovered that some of Aus-
tralia’s towering eucalypts
are merely nodding red
roses. A synthetic “Otto of
Rose” (why “Otto”?) is
now produced in the labora-
tories of his firm, and the
Australian girl who uses the
perfume never guesses that
the hush it came from is
taller than a cathedral and
wouldn't grow a rose in a
thousand years. Another
Blogg triumph is a synthetic
violet scent made from the
essential oil of a Bananaland
tree. I can vouch for its
power. The inventor pre-
sented me with a tiny phial
of the pure 100 per cent, pure
violet with the direction that
“it should be reduced with
100 parts of pure alcohol to
realise its true odor.” I put
the phial in my waistcoat
pocket and went in search of
pure alcohol. On the morn-
ing boat people started to
look at me and sniff. It
seemed an unusually fresh
morning. Struck by a sud-
den doubt. I felt in my
pocket and found that the
cork had come out. The suit
has been airing and scenting
our yard for a fortnight, and
a poet two streets away was
misled into writing two
Spring poems while others
were still sniffing about winter wi-i-nds.
“Cudgewa”: Three bags of human bones
are being retained at Edenhope police station
(Vic.) as Exhibit xl in case an old-time mur-
derer should some day be roped in. The
tragedy took place about 1864, when a man
THE BRAKE’S ON.
THE ONE : “Tom and Ethel are such fast friends.”
THE OTHER: “Yes—isn’t it hard luck her husband knows it?”
and his wife and child set out from Hors-
ham in a waggonette intending to go to Port-
land. They never got there. A man named
Cook, said to be on intimate terms with the
lady, was known to have followed the party.
Ten years later three fencers camping in the
Maryvale district were scared at hearing
what they declared were a woman’s shrieks
from the adjoining scrub. Next morning’s
search discovered the bones of a woman and
child beside a decayed log. Further search
revealed the remains of a man, but whether
those of the husband or Cook nobody knows.
The Law has nothing but the three bags of
hones, and many Hops have grown grey
caring for them. As Cook and the husband
were both over 30 at the time of the tragedy
the guilty one (if he is still alive) is well
over SO, and would make a very poor hang-
ing. All things considered, the Law might
now give the undertaker a chance and get
on to something fresh.

“Primus”: I was over two years abroad
with the A.1.F., and I never heard of an
officer being given a discharge of any sort
on termination of his appointment. Where
did “Karee” (B. 10/8/T7) encounter one?
I suppose controversy will always rage
around the question of batmen, but if an
officer is filling his job properly I’m satisfied
that he absolutely needs a batman to do a
hundred and one things which he hasn’t time
to do for himself. Batmen are not passen-
gers: they do their whack of fighting just
like anyone else. As to other points stressed
by “Karee,” (1) when I was at the front
reinforcement n.c.o.’s did revert to the ranks
on joining their units; (2) saluting in the
trenches was not merely not compulsory, it
was not allowed ; (3) officers had exactly the
same rations, blankets and luggage as their
men and carried the last-named to the
trenches in exactly the same way, viz., on
their backs; (4) as to men messing with
officers, or officers with men, all I can say is
that a little separation is good for every-
body and meals provide an excellent oppor-
tunity.

“Peter”: Having found that the task of
smashing open his patent air-tight telegram
results in the message being reduced to con-
fetti, P.M.G. Webster now stamps each wire
with the direction : “Open here from Right
to Left.” This advice costs the Department
more rubber stamps, not to mention extra
labor, but I am afraid it won’t reduce the
charges of disorderly language against those
who are compelled to solve Webster’s mys-
teries. The green and pink missives are still
a puzzle, and I apprehend the day
when William will be induced to
fasten each ’gram with a combina-
tion lock, whereupon numerous un-
employed safe-breakers will at last
earn an honest living by teaching
the way-in by correspondence.
“Trallwm” : Some time ago I told the tale of
the festive subdivider who included 10 feet
of street in his workers’-liome blocks, but
not in the title, and built the humpies up to
the edge of the true boundary. When the
council presently served street-making
notices on the block-owners, the latter woke
up to the fact that the surveyed street cut
off their verandahs. They got no redress;
the titles were ironbound on the subject of
depth, and not one of the buyers had run
over his little lot with a tape. The gentle
subdivider has now put up a similar deal.
This time he sold his 21ft. x 43ft. dog-runs
by means of a bait in the building contract
that undertook to make the street upon
which the blocks fronted, street-forming
charges being the bugbear of the small- pro-
perty-owner buying rent-purchase. This sub-
divider ran a double line of pitchers down
each side of the “street,” formed a footpath
of earth behind, and tipped a few loads of
metal between. The little homesteads were
rushed. The owners then asked the council
to take over the street. “That sort of street’s
no good to us!” it replied, and served notice
upon the residents to pay their quota to-
wards the proper construction of it. The
poor devils will have to do it. The street-
making clause in their contracts has un-
happily omitted to state “to the satisfaction
of the council.” There was almost a row in
England 20 years ago w r hen it became known
that one of the “milk-blended butter” firms
had employed the first civil lawyer in the
U.K. to draft an advt. that would beat the
Pure Foods Act. Apparently there are law-
yers here, too, who will assist the boodling
hind-dissector to tlirt with False Pretences.
“Blue Pill” : The old trouble between doc-
tors and lodges in Victoria seems to be com-
ing at last to an earnest scrap. The case
for the doctors is that the workers, who con-
stitute Sawbones’s employers, have had rises
to pay for the extra cost of living, aud that
Sawbones’s living has gone up as much as
anybody’s. The doctors therefore want a
few shillings a year more from each mem-
ber, and claim that that will impose no hard-
ship on that person, because his pay has
been put up for this very purpose. They
have said all this many times before; but
with the market very empty of doctors on
account of the war they declare that this
time they are going to stick out. It is the
younger generation who are raising the loud-
est howl. There is still a powerful minority
of older doctors, most of them with a pretty
fat cheque coming in every quarter, who are
satisfied with present conditions. But these
few will be overwhelmed. The lodges for
their part declare that if the doctors insist
on, say, a sov. a year from each member,
instead of 165., they will get the sack in-
stead. The matter is to be arranged by the
different lodges in the various districts com-
bining (as they did some time ago at
Prahran) to put in a pound a head for hir-
ing their own exclusive doctors. One dis-
trict with 2500 members, for instance, can
allow two doctors £1250 a year each, plus mid-
wifery fees and a certain amount of private
practice—say £2OOO altogether, with no bad
debts. It is an attractive bait, and the
beauty of the scheme from the lodges’ view-
point is that they know they can get the
men. In short, the lodges practically admit
that they are able to pay the extra bit and
will do so if they are pushed ; but they won’t
pay the men who do the pushing.

There was a flare-up in Perth three
months back when Westralian Recruiting-
officer Burkett was refused permission to
speak at a charity concert held in
Government House ballroom. On August
11 another concert was held—same swell
audience; same place (Ellison-Macartney
is generous with his barn). At the
end of the programme his Ex. introduced
Lieut. C. R. Heppingstone, who is relieving
Burkett, and in a guarded little cameo of a
speech said he had invited the officer to
speak to remove any misunderstanding as to
the attitude of Government House audiences
towards recruiting efforts. As to the times
and places suited for those efforts he still,
however, had his own opinions. But as
Heppingstone bagged at least one recruit, the
time and place were evidently better than
his Ex. thought.
RESERVATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Women war workers in certain English counties
have refused to wear trousers.”
Lo, war is war. The foemen reel and bleed.
Our men must fight amid machine-made stenches.
We battle on to match a nation’s need,
Defeating hunger in the long home trenches.
Gladly we strive; but that which narks and vexes
Is the official mixing of the sexes.
The furrow runs amid the upturned sods;
Each teamstress dimples sweetly as she ploughs
hers,
But holds it still a bit above the odds
To sheathe her legs in patriotic trousers.
Though toiling hard, she dreams of subtle, charming
Loose lingerie made suitable for farming.
John may come home without an arm or leg;
But she can keep the turnips growing greenly—■
There is no reason he should starve or beg;
No reason he should find her less than queenly.
She holds that bifurcated moleskinned Misses
Might scarcely be provocative of kisses.
So speed the plough! But leave our petticoats
A little of their ancient tender flutter.
Little we care for politics or votes;
But girls have hopes while cows give cream and
butter. .
And howsoe’er war’s sun at last in gore sets
Leave us the triumph of our frills and corsets.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

PERSONAL
Friends writing from London say that
George Reid is these times a victim to in-
somnia. One of our sleepless knights.
Bishop Sadlier will not wear the Anglican
gaiters at Gippsland, after all; he prefers to
stick to his Maoriland billet. The Gipps-
land job goes to George Harvard Cranswick,
whose father is a canon of Sydney Cathe-
dral. Cranswick, who is “low church,” will
easily be the youngest Anglican bishop in
the Commonwealth, as he is only 34. He
was born in Sheffield (Eng.), but came to
Sydney with his father when a few months
old. Dad went as curate to Dr. Pain at
Darlinghurst; the son now succeeds Pain as
Bishop of Gippsland. Young Cranswick is
an old boy of The King’s School, Parra-
matta, and a B.A. of Sydney Uni. After
working for a while as a schoolmaster in
Geelong (Vic.) and Armidale (N.S.W.) he
went to Oxford to acquire the bleat that hall-
marks an Anglican parson. He served his
time in a Cold Country curacy; then bossed
a missionary school in India for four years.
Part of the time he was chaplain to Azariah,
the first native Indian to wear an Anglican
paitre. More recently he was expounding
the Word in Sydney; and a couple of months
ago he succeeded the veteran of Australian
Anglicanism, Dean MacCullagh, at St.
Paul’s, Bendigo. He is married to a daugh-
ter of E. C. Hordern, of the Pitt-street
drapery firm.

Died last week in Maoriland, William
Lane, disillusioned dreamer. Lane felt that
society was all wrong. He believed that,
without altering human nature, it could be
organised on much better lines. He despaired
of Parliamentary methods —despaired, at any
rate, of rapid action that way. Some men
in the circumstances would have whispered
“Revolution.” Lane was too gentle, too for-
giving, to be a revolutionary of the gun-
powder-and-steel sort. The only way out
that he could see was to take a select party
into an empty foreign land and form there
the ideal settlement to serve as an object-
lesson for the world. The Worker, of Bris-
bane, of which he was editor, was his plat-
form. From this he preached with eloquent
fervor the glories of the New Australia that
he was to found in Paraguay. The pilgrims
left Sydney in the midst of the Labor awak-
ening of 25 years ago. It is enough to say
now that the scheme failed —and Lane came
back to Australia still hardly daring to be-
lieve the evidences of his own experience.
But put this at least to his credit: he tried.
A most forcible writer, he was at once re-
employed on the press, and for the past four
years was editor of the N.Z. Herald, of
Auckland. Aged 56. ‘
Just-dead John Haynes once scored off
that doughty old fighter Henry Copeland.
The Lyne Government had been formed and
Copeland was shut out of it. “Uproarious”
didn’t half describe his condition. . He
wouldn’t look at his old colleagues, and the
Reidites sent him off in another direction.
He wanted a room to himself—a sort of
political Fort Hudson. Speaker Abbott was
importuned every evening. “Would he set
a room aside for him?” “Would he have
one built?” After about a month of this
the Speaker at last announced that he had
provided the room. “Has it got padded
walls?” John asked.
What roundabout ways lead to the
trenches! During the Panama Exposition
in 1915 Arthur Henry Banninger, of
’Frisco, and his pard (now cobber) Ralph
Viers Leggett, of Kansas, were struck by
the exhibits in the
Australian Court.
After the show had
closed they dis-
cussed the country
that produced
those things, and
stowed away on a
vessel outward-
bound. They were
found and com-
pelled to work
their passage, but
had no trouble in
getting jobs when
they reached Mel-
bourne. At last
America butted
into the firing-line,
and they remem-
bered that they
were citizens of
the TJ.S. So they
marched together
to Melbourne Town
Hall recrui ting
depot, and nre now
in camp.

Melbourne's well-

The three Lindsays, Norman, Lionel and
Percy, are to have a joint exhibition in the
Fine Arts Society’s new galleries in Mel-
bourne. Norman supplies water-colors,
Percy oils and Lionel etchings. Principally
by virtue of Norman’s nudes drenched in
sunlight, the show promises to induce Block
saunterers to get a move on up the hill.
“Masetto”: Stored away somewhere in
Melbourne Public Library is a large group
of portraits of early colonists, some of whom,
by the way, did a
little towards found-
ing the institution.
This interesting relic
has not seen the light
for ages. Surely in an
Australian institution
like the Library a few
square feet of wall
could be found for an
important exhibit like
this. If it has been
put under hatches be-
cause it isn’t suffici-
ently “artistic,” some
of the alleged art
hanging about should
have gone before _ it.
The Historical Society
might ask to have the
old identities honor-
ably hung again.
Never mind the an-
tique cut of their
clothes and whiskers.
CAPTAIN J. C.
GLANVILLE, Aide-
de-camp to Sir Newdy-
Nudigatc, new Gover-
nor of Tasmania.
While Edmund Milne was at Orange
(N.S.W.) as Superintendent of the Western
District railways, he was nicknamed
“Old Ironbark,” and regarded as a
certainty for the seventies or eighties.
It was a surprise to many of his
old friends that he should have gone off
duty at 55—though he had been ill for some
time. Facially he somewhat resembled Abe
Lincoln, but was not above medium height.
Lean, wiry, hard-working and plain-spoken,
he was a good specimen of the Australian
bushman. A crack shot, holding many
prizes, he was_ proud of his organisation of
the Railway Rifle Club, which provided many
good Billjims. He was a better writer than
speaker, and through all his articles on the
explorers and the aborigines there rang the
note of genuine patriotism. He knew the
traffic men well and they respected him, and
were proud of his climb from telegraph-boy
to second Commissioner. He was a great
admirer and loyal supporter of his chief,
James Fraser, and during his last illness
insisted upon going to the office to help in
dealing with the strike. When not thus
engaged his thoughts were most of the time
with his two sons —Major C. O. Milne,
D. 5.0., and Captain C. J. Milne, ■whom he
had not seen since they left Sydney in the
early days of the war.
Brig.-General F. E. Johnston, eldest son
of C. J. Johnston, Speaker of Maoriland’s
Legislative Council, recently killed in
France, was a native of Wellington (M.L.).
Soldiering was his profession, and in the
regular army he first had a taste of the real
thing with the Dongola Expedition in 1596.
During the S’African melee he collected sun-
dry medals and clasps. In 1914 he was a
major on furlough in Maoriland and being
ordered to join the M.L. Expeditionary
Force left . as colonel commanding the
Infantry Brigade. He went right through
Gallipoli, then moved to France, and rose to
be brig.-general before a Hun marksman
ended his race. A young brother, Lieut.
Johnston, was killed last year.
A word from Cosme. the Paraguayan off-
shoot from Bill Lane’s New r Australia colony :
hen the Mad Bog broke loose, D. R. Stevenson,
of Oosme, reached for his gun and headed for
Asuncion. As he looked to be over the age-limit
the authorities didn t show much enthusiasm; but
by careful lying he managed to overcome their
scruples and lit out for the Front, where he was
kept busy until a few months ago, when he got him-
self pincushioned. After being patched up he was
sent back to Paraguay, and a few days ago the
whole settlement met the warrior at the bridge and
conducted him through the big monte to Cosm£,
where it gave him such a rousing Australian welcome
that the howling monkeys fled into the depths of the
jungle. Stevenson, with 53 years’ strenuous life
behind him, is about as tough a rag as ever swung
an axe or flourished a pair of tongs; but that little
home-coming broke him up.
The worm turned in Melbourne last week
and bit Adela Pankhurst. She was
then ordered to gaol for a month for
repairs. Adela came to Australia, where
woman-suffrage had long been estab-
lished. tor the purpose of explaining
the plans of the militant suffragettes in
Britain, which was to make mere men poli-
ticians look ridiculous until they gave women
the vote As it would have been too absurd
to attack man for doing here what she had
attacked him for not doing in Britain <sh
switched on to the war; and because ’Jj
of the men who talked talked on the BritUh
side, she began to chatter at Britain oVA
offer apologies for Germany. She doesn’f
really hate Britain. All that is the matfeJ
with Adela is that she isn’t married
hasn’t opinions; she has only an obscure
disease.
Somebody publicly suggests that Dave
Plennessy should be a sport” and relinquish
his grip on Melbourne’s gaud-mayoraltv R„*
Hennessy is as unlike a sport as any baker
on earth. He’s just a dull, unbending ~n
comfortable-looking person in pince-nez’ and
a stove-pipe hat. He wears his gaud-mavor
alty with a seriousness which infects the "air
Other mayors have been more fussy, but none
more conscious of their awful position He
loves being mayor, and he would hardly have
passed up his candidature for a sixth helnin?
if he hadn’t assured himself that he was
going to get it. Frank Stapley is said to he
a rival candidate, but if he is only hoping for

Twenty-four years have seen tremendous
changes in the Supreme Court Bench in N
S. Wales. In that time no less than a dozen
men have gone up to it from the Bar. But
in Vie., during the same period, only one
man has reached the well-paid berth.
Went West the other day at Hobart a
sure-enough centenarian, James Park, who
would have been 102 if he had lived a month
longer. He came from Scotland 77 years
ago as a youth of 25, tried Port Phillip f or
a couple of years, and then put in three-
quarters of a century in Tasmania. He
reckoned that a moderate use of whisky
combined with the climate kept him up gj
long.
Bed is woman’s last ditch. The cable
says that the Chateau of Queen-mother
Alexandra’s sister Dagmar, Dowager Czar-
ina, was searched; whereupon the lady in-
dignantly took to her nightie and the
searchers were afraid to intrude upon the
Czar’s mother and King George’s aunt in
that costume. Lola Montes knew all about
the last, strong refuge of woman. When
Sydney creditors pressed her she fled to the
outgoing steamer and undressed. That beat
them.

wanted to make sure she wasn't about
before I replied.”
Died in his sleep in Adelaide, A. R.
Mursell, Renmark’s most prominent tennis-
player in its early days, but for the last 20
years accountant to the big firm of G. and R
Wills at Port Adelaide. He was as good a
business man as he was a sport, highly
musical and highly popular—and only 54.
“Musketoon”: John Walker, one-time
Moderator of N. S. Wales Presbyterian
Assembly, who on his election guaranteed
to collect £lOO,OOO in six months for St.
Andrew’s College within Sydney Uni. and did
it, is now a chaplain with Billjim. His wife
is at a Red Cross depot in France; so is a
daughter. All of the not-so-old people's
seven sons joined the colors ; three have been
killed. The only member of the family not
in uniform is a married daughter, and she
wants to get into Red Cross work.
ON THE QUAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Ere where the town and ’arbor join their ’ands,
An’ rockeries an’ other buildin’s stands,
1 love ter sit, an’ think, an’ sit some more,
Watchin’ the red roofs on the Mosman shore,
The clouds that ’ang, an’ other clouds that float,
The street-cars, an’ the bustlin’ Manly boat,
The pub be’ind, whose gay doors swing an’ shut,
The warm streets bendin’ westward to the Cut,
The ferries belchin’ crowds of grim gazobs
Tearin’ like mad to ’old their daily jobs,
The sunnin’ dogs no ’urryin’ boot may rouse,
The sleepy square before the Custom ’Ouse,
The Deutscher wharf that dreams of glories past,
The far-off doorway of the First an’ Last —
Cobber, it’s bosker, take it straight from me,
’Ere in the sunlight, sittin’ on the Quay!
Yeh’d think, ter see me sittin’ ’ere so still,
Scrapin’ a match, or ’ummin’ for a fill
To stuff my pipe with every now an’ then,
1 ’ad no brains, like whips of other men.
I never ’old with skitin’ —wot’s the good ?
Or I might quote yer “Never Unnerstood,”
Or somethin’ similar and aprer po
(A bloke might call it) givin’ yeh the know
Of w’y I’m dreamin’ ’ere, as you might say,
Winnin’ the war my own perculyer way.
There’s crowds of blokes ’oo never, as they ought,
Are givin’ it a bloomin’ minute’s thought.
But ’earken, if yeh never did before;
I’m givin’ yeh the office— there’s a War !
There's tons of things a bloke will come ter see
’Ere in the sunlight, sittin’ on the Quay.
’Old ’ard a shake —it’s bonzer in the sun—
An’ don’t go orf before I’ve ’alf begun.
Plain as yer dial (an’ that’s plain enough!)
Yell'll see that some must scrap an’ others bluff;
An’ some (like us), obeyin’ Freedom’s laws,
Give lovin’ relatives to ’elp the Cause.
There's blokes ’oo love ter fight, an’ blokes, like me,
’Oo give their brains (an’ never grudge ’em) free.
Each in ’is little niche ’is bit can do—
I’ve give a nephew, an’ a pal or two,
A cousin, an’ a brother with a kid,
Two uncles, an’ a bloke I owed a quid.
It’s up to all to ’ump their burden, so
The bloke ’oo wants ter fight—well, let ’im go!
I’ll do the talkin’. That’s the lurk for me,
’Ere in the sunshine, sittin’ on the Quay.
“ ’Ere with a loaf er bread, some beer, an’ thou !”
(Wot was the tune of that old Persian cow?) —
Well, that’s just wot I feel like. Bet yer boots
Them gay old roosters, just like modern coots,
Knoo w’en ter fight, an’ w’en ter use the nut,
Put the ’ard word, or keep the dial shut.
I ’card a cove last Sunday talkin’ big,
Stuck, like Nathaniel, underneath the fig
Down in the Dom. ; ’e put the words across—
Fair took me fancy: “Economic loss,”
“Conservin’ brains,” an’ “Duty ter the State,”
An’ “Blokes can serve ’oo only stand an’ wait!”
I’d never felt, until ’e turned the tricks,
A bloke could be so noble, doin’ nix.
I’ll sit an’ wait —I got a ’ousemaid’s knee —
’Ere in the sunshine, baskin’ on the Quay.
Strike me, it's great! I never thought, before
I ’eard these things, jus’ wot we’re fightin’ for.
But w’en a bloke ’oo’s never understood
Gets wise, ’e wakes, an’ calls ’is country good ;
An’ w’en the blarst of war blows in ’is ears,
’Ee ups an’ says, “Blokes, cast away your fears!
Arise an’ gird yer ’ips ter meet the foe—
Or, if yeh can’t, then let yer cobber go!
An’ us yeh leave be’ind ter go an’ fight,
We’ll bless yeh in our little beds at night!”
Jus’ twig them skimmin’ sails like seagulls’ wings,
Them shimmerin’ waves, an’ all the other things,
An’ thank the brains I’m rackin’ fer their sake,
An’ also (if yeh’ve time) the blokes that make
This clolsey far nienty safe for me,
’Ere in the sunlight, sittin’ on the Quay!
VICTOBIA.
J. ALEX ALLAN.

The WILDCAT
COUMN
The Bank of Queensland. —This is an
amalgamation of the Royal of Queensland
and the Bank of North Queensland, and the
capital was arrived at by valuing all the
assets at bedrock. The North Queensland
had a capital of £162,500 and a reserve of
£40,097 as at December 31 last. In the
reconstruction the £40,097 has gone: either
it has disappeared altogether or it has been
transferred to a contingency account. Share-
holders, however, lost none of their capital
—at least, none that they had not written
off many years ago. The Royal, which had
not carved down capital so strenuously, had
to suffer for its neglect to have an earlier
cleaning up. It had 09,999 ordinary shares
of £9 paid to £0 10s. and 7112 pref. shares
fully paid to £lO. and £19,640 had been pre-
paid on the ordinaries. The agreement
arrived at was to refund the prepaid capital
in full, and as to the prefs. to give in ex-
change either fixed deposits of one, two and
three years’ currency, carrying interest at
a rate equal to and varying with the rate
of dividend, or ordinary shares —in either
case to the full face value of the shares.
The bulk of the pref. capital was ex-
changed for deposits. The old £6 10s. shares
were written down to £4, which meant
£280,000 in the new capital; and £7500
was issued in exchange for prefs. That
brought the capital of the ne.w bank to an
even £450,000, divided into 45,000 shares;
and it has authority to issue another 55,000.
The issued shares are fully paid. The whole
writing down worked out as follows :
North Queensland, capital . . . . Nil
North Queensland, reserves (approx.).. £40,000
Royal, capital .. .. •• 175,000
Royal, reserves (approx.) . . . . 85,000
Total . • • • . . .. £300,000
To what extent the old reserves have been
available for purposes of an inner reserve is
of course not visible from the accounts.
There is, however, reason to believe that
the new bank starts off with a substantial
contingency fund. At any rate, it was
announced that the representatives of the
Royal, on overhauling the North Queensland
prior to the amalgamation, had not found
it necessary to write down the assets —which
means that from that side of the amalgama-
tion there was £40,000 to the good over and
above the capital.
The writing-down has been directed
mainly to the advances, but to some extent
to the premises. The latter are valued at
£157,500; presently, no doubt, this amount
will be reduced by the sale of superfluous
offices, in Brisbane especially. For the new
bank’s first half-year profits have naturally
been affected by the fact that the economies
which can certainly be made have not yet
been possible—in regard to these now-super-
fluous premises, for instance. Also the half-
year has had to stand expenses of amalga-
mation and extra taxation. In the first half-
year’s accounts published after January,
1916, the two banks showed a profit of
£21,880. The new bank .is content to show
£17.220 for its first half. Out of that a div.
of 6 p.c. is paid ; this means 6 p.c. to the
old North Queensland shareholders and a
shade under 4 p.c. to Royal shareholders.
In the half-year prior to the amalgamation
North Queensland paid 6 p.c. and the Royal
44 p.c.; but the latter called for an amount
in excess of the disclosed profit.
The Bank of Queensland starts off well
equipped. Shareholders and public alike will
probably note one thing in particular: at
the head of it is a general manager who
was so careful that his published accounts
should mean what they said that when a
rival concern overhauled his securities with
a view to amalgamation it did not find it
necessary to write off a penny of capital.
Liabilities at, July 31 were £3.005.000; and
£1,204,835 of this, equal to just over 40 p.c.,
was liquid.
Amalgamated Wireless (Austral-
asia) , Ltd. :

That means 24 p.c. for the past 12 months,
the same as in the preceding year. The co.’s
capital is £140.000. of which £90,000 is
represented by patent rights, licenses and
exclusive agencies—these include the Aus-
tralasian rights in the Marconi, Poulsen and
Telefunken wireless systems. Wireless
itself is not, however, patentable; and the
Commonwealth Government has a system of
its own. The £90,000 is therefore not v-ery
productive capital at present, though the
parent English co., which holds many of the
90,000 shares, has apparently the utmost
confidence in the future —of the Marconi sys-
tem in particular. Probably that accounts
for an announcement in the directors’ report.
Six months ago the Board, believing that
“further immediate extension of the wireless
business of the co. in Australia cannot be
expected,” decided to invest some money in
outside concerns, and purchased a control-
ling interest in a motor-car agency. Now: —
The investment has so far justified its introduction
by paying interest at the rate of 6 p.c.; but in view
of the possibility of further extension in several
departments of our existing business, and in other
directions more closely allied thereto, the directors
have considered the advisability of releasing the
capital thus invested, and are pleased to report that
since the close of the half-year satisfactory arrange-
ments have been made in this connection.
For the present the revenue is prejudiced by
the practical prohibition of ships’ messages;
and it will continue so till the end of the
war. Afterwards much will depend upon
the attitude of the Commonwealth Govern-
ment towards private wireless business.
Against the profit for the past half-year
£2709 is shown as written off. Of this, how-
ever, £lOOO. has been added to a marine in-
surance fund. That fund is bundled in the
published accounts with a patents deprecia-
tion reserve: the total of the lot is £20,547.
Apart from patents and other goodwill
assets the co. has £46,432 in plant, stores,
etc., and £45.093 liquid and invested; and
it owes £12,500. But plant, stores, etc., of
this character are for the most part worth
just what they will earn. In other words,
i f the co. couldn’t make a profit by using
the plant, it could probably get very little
for most of it by an auction sale. So the
value of the shares is entirely governed, not
by the visible assets, but by the probabilities
of private wireless business.

This little bank goes a good way in a long
time: it has got as far as this in 52 years.
It would have got further if in the boom
days of 25 years back it hadn’t tried to go
faster and missed the step. Now, with its
one office, and that in a city whose old
glory of gold has departed, it is
content to plod along, and when it finds that
it is putting by more than a thousand in the
half-year up goes the dividend. In the
past eight years it has put by £15,000, and
that at 5 p.c. pays more than half the extra
11 p.c. dividend. Otherwise—well, here is
a bird’s-eye view over 20 years (figures are
as at June 30): —
1897. 1907. 1917.
Capital and reserves .. £142,801 £159,803 £180,764
Liabilities . . . . 202,435 238,658 239,375
Liquid assets . . . . 69,580 79,279 94,856
Advances . . . . 263,961 313,627 322,783
Pemises, etc. . . . . 11,695 5,555 2,500
A bank which has the use of little more
than £1 of the public’s money for every £1
of its own, and keeps 40 p.c. of its liabilities
liquid, can’t expect to make anything gaudy
in the way of profits; actually they show a
trifle less than 51 p.c. on capital and reserves
combined, and shareholders get under 44
p.c. Looking at it that way some impetuous
shareholders have suggested now and then
that if the directors can’t see their way to
launch out they should sell\ out. But the
directors stand pat. Ballarat is the only
town in Australia outside the State capitals
which has a bank of its own; and the
average Ballarat' man has enough pride in
the old place to want to hang on to all the
distinctions it has.

This co., registered in England, practically
owns the co. that owns the Brisbane trams.
The real owner is the also English-registered
Brisbane Tramways Co., in 150,000 shares;
the Investment Co. owns 149,893 of them.
The effect of this arrangement is that no
obstreperous person can go into the market,
buy a share in the tramways and then go
to the meetings and find out all about every-
thing. But the tramway co., though it nomi-
nally owns the system, doesn't put up the
money for extensions; this investment co.
does anything necessary in that way, and
during the past five years has spent on con-
struction, etc., the following amounts: —
1912 .. £51,596 I 1915 .. £70,068
1913 .. 56,376 I 1916 .. 32,194
1914 . . 103,751 I
That means £313.985 in five years; and the
only new capital which has been got in
during the period is £150,000 in ordinary
shares. A trifle has been got from the bank ;
the balance consists of undivided profit. In
these five years, as the first table shows, the
dividends received from the tramways co.
have paid the small amount which the man-
agement of this co. costs, provided the* divi-
dends and left £115,992 over.
Meantime also the tramways co. has in-
creased its renewal fund from £90,000 to
£150.000. Apparently, however, it has not
stored up any reserves to speak of. No
balance-sheet of the tramway co. is pub-
lished ; hut the investment co. supplies
enough material for this table: —

There is some doubt about £7500 of this:
that much profit was appropriated in 1915
for a fund to cover depreciation of invest-
ments. Expenses, it will be noticed, have
galloped along. In 1913 they were 60.7 p.c.
of the receipts; last year 71.8 p.c. What
taxes amounted to in the old days isn’t
known to this paper. Last year the bill
(including British excess profits duty) was
£00,660, or about 7s. 6d. in the £. And that
isn’t the lot; for the investment co. has to
pay taxes, too. The div. on ordinary shares
is paid free of income tax, but the pref. share’
holders have apparently to carry their own
burden. Which is no doubt why the 5 p.c.
was worth to them only £14,531 last year as
against £17,656 before the war.
The co.’s rights run to 1921: the under-
taking may then be acquired by the public
at a fair valuation, no charge to be made
for goodwill. The co. probably feels very
comfortable about it all; for if, as seems
highly probable, the State steps in with a
notice of purchase, the valuers will of course
have to take into account the price of tram-
way material and the cost of construction at
the moment; and on present appearances
construction materials are not going to be
cheap for a good many years to come.

This co. is doing its bit, and is nowadays
something better than an importer of drugs
and chemicals. There is, among other things,
a well-equipped factory at Chippendale for
the scientific handling of raw materials;
here, for example, various cod-liver oil pre-
parations are turned out, equal, it is
claimed, to any imported. Certainly, the law
sees to it that the materials are pure when
they are used, and the public can have an
assurance that the contents are also reason-
ably fresh when the bottle is uncorked.
The co. started under unusually favorable
conditions. The business had for years been
short of capital. What it needed was sup-
plied by people who had plenty of it; and
not a penny was paid for goodwill. A year
or two were spent in building and reorgan-
isation ; then the co. began to make the pro-
fits which had been figured on. And pro-
bably a bit more; for the co., having large
ambitions, had stocked heavily, and the
sudden outbreak of war is hardlv likely to
have done its profits any harm. It had the
good luck to be just ready for the oppor-
tunity that came its way. Its share capital
(£75,000) being insufficient, it raised £25 000
on debentures. “To strengthen the financial
position,” the directors put it. they propose
now to pay off this debt and issue 25,000
new £1 shares at par on January I next
These are to be offered pro rata to present
shareholders; payment in full is to be made
before the end of this year, “so that all
shares will rank equally for dividends from
January 1, 1918.” There is very little doubt
that a 10 p.c. div. is at least hoped for
During the past four years the profit, includ
ing the 5 p.c. interest on debentures, has
been in excess of the amount required to nni-
-10 p.c. on the £100,000; and there is good
reason to believe that, in valuing stocks
etc., liberal provision has been made for
contingencies. And every year the disclosed
profit has shown an increase. Apart from
the debenture debt the liabilities total
£37,751. Finance seems to be an easy pro-
blem ; in addition to £52,023 in book debts
there are cash balances and other liquid
assets £10,601. Other assets include stock
£75,034 and freehold, plant and fixtures
£18.158. Though additions have been made
to these latter every year and especially
during the past year they stand now
at about £2OOO less than in 11)14—which is
a further hint of a conservative policy of
writing-down.

There is a formal reserve fund of £25,000
plus unappropriated profit £31,688; total
£56,688. Deduct the goodwill item £22,500,
and you get the £34,1.88 shown above as true
reserves —the amount in excess of the capital
represented by tangible assets. In a case of
this sort, however, and taking all the circum-
stances of the hour into account, a co. has
a lot of justification in showing as capital
the money which it has spent in building
up the business. If it doesn’t, the rate of
dividend is liable to mislead onlookers.
It was all to the good of Australia that
this co. was charged with energy; and when
the war gave it a chance which the politi-
cians had refused, it jumped in, and the
subsequent proceedings have been full of
vigor. Some of the result is shown in this
comparison : —•
1915. 1916. 1917.
Liabilities ..£46,076 £75,730 £113,984
Freeholds .. 21,297 24,000 33,194
Plant, etc. . . 36,942 45,000 53,432

Stock .. .. 74,591 128,437 181,845
Cash, etc. .. 8,789 4,148 6,600
To finance the increased business and the
very much larger stocks which that and war
circumstances have called for the co. has not
depended wholly upon borrowed money:
during the past year 50,000 new £1 ordinary
shares have been issued. That makes the
share capital £185,618 —£22,500 in 8 p.c.
cumulative prefs. (“A” shares), £30.000 in
8 p.c. cumulative prefs. (“B” shares),
£118,737 in fully paid ordinaries and £14,381
in ordinaries paid to 10s. Of the additional
£147,510 put into the business during the
past two years £43,667 has come from share-
holders and £35,935 from undivided profits,
while £67,908 is outside money. Revenue,
besides providing this surplus of £35.935
after paying exes, and dividends, has pro-
vided substantial sums for depreciation—as
far as can be traced, something like £7OOO
during the past year, of which £5092 has
been struck off plant. Altogether a fine
record. And from the public point of view
the increase in the outgoings is even finer.
The wages bill is not disclosed; but in
general exes, (salaries, printing, commission,
etc.) £92,168 was charged against gross
profits last year, while in 1915 it was only
£64,468.

Primitive man’s first thought was for shelter, and
architecture had its origin in man’s need of a roof
to protect him from the elements. The first roof
was made from branches of trees. Then came tiles
made of mud, from then up to the twentieth century
there has been steady progress in roofings, until to-day
the acme of perfection in roofings has been reached in
Adamax. Adamax Asphaltum Roofing is the best
that money can buy. It resists the elements, will
neither rust, rot, sweat, crumble nor pinhole. It
is acid-, vermin- and water-proof. You can use
Adamax for roofing factories, mine buildings, farm
buildings, garages, etc. Adamax costs one-third the
present price of galvanised-iron, and the railage and
freight is almost one-third less. Write for catalogue
and sample. William Adams k Co., Ltd., Sydney,
Melbourne, Brisbane, Adelaide and Perth. — Advt.

Babcock and Wilcox Water-tube Boiler, so univer-
