water into 4in. tubes, which permit of rapid circula-

lin. plates used in the Shell Boiler being done away
with. — Advt.

Still, in spite of an increase of about
£90,000 in metals unrealised and concen-
trates in hand and in suspense, the financial
position is very strong. Of the £605.985 of
deposits, etc., over £BOO,OOO consists of cash
balances; £90,000 is in War Loan and
£200,000 is represented by the co.’s holding
in the Associated Smelters. On the other
side, of the £266,069 liabilities £193,800 is
owing on long-dated debentures —money
raised to buy an interest in the smelters.
Put it this way: in the past 21 years of
war the South has paid £480.000 in divi-
dends (the capital is £163.727), made all
necessary additions to and renewals of
plant, and put by £414,858. It is not sug-
gested that all this is surplus profit. But,
apart from whatever may be necessary as
a sinking fund, the only visible contingent
liability is the war profits tax. The direc-
tors say they have “found it difficult to cal-
culate what the co.’s position under the bill
will be, and until the measure is passed the
Board proposes to keep a considerable sum
available at short call.” Actual payments
on account of income tax during last year
were £41,101. The royalty paid to the
State was £5636. For the past half-year
the amounts actually charged against
revenue were £44,237, of which £27,500 is
an appropriation to meet taxes not yet pay-
able.

That works out at 13s. 2d. per ton of ore.
Part of this has been made out of previously-
produced zinc tailings, treated ijnder a
profit-sharing arrangement with the Zinc
Corporation. But as comparatively littld
has been delivered since the w r ar started, as
there are still 827,500 tons to deliver, and
as it is only during the last year that a
plant has been in operation for very profit-
ably treating slimes (of which there are
nearly 500.000 tons dumped), it can fairly
be reckoned that, given the same net prices
for metals, the profit should in future be as
good as in the past, calculated per ton of
ore. No estimate of ore reserves is pub-
lished this half: six months ago the manager
reckoned he had 34 million tons. At 13s.
per ton there would be £2,275,000 in that
lot. (There are 200,000 shares in the co.)
It is true that the metal markets have
been booming during the last couple of years
of war. But as against that note that this
calculation is based on dividends paid, and
whereas profits in the past two years have
amounted to £879,519, only £450,000 has
been divided. The ore treated during the
past year was 279,700 tons, and at that rate
there is about 12 years’ supply proved in the
mine above the 1270 ft. level. There is a
level at 1370 ft.; but so far the ore has been
prospected only by the drill, and then only
in the northern section. As to the quality
of the ore in the deepest levels, take this
comparison of last half-year’s feed to the
mill: —
Lead. Silver. Zinc.
1170 ft. level .. 13.5 p.c. 7.10 z. 13.4 p.c.
1270 ft. level .. 13.8 „ 9.2 „ 13.5 „
Average of mine 13.7 „ 6.6 „ 13.3 „
On the metallurgical side the most interest-
ing feature of late has been the treatment of
slimes. From 18,387 tons 2482 tons of
leady concentrates were produced worth
61.9 p.c. lead and 49.40 z. silver, and bur-
dened with only 7.8 p.c. zinc—a higher-
grade concentrate (because much richer in
silver) than the mill produces from ore, and
the recoveries compare like this:
Lead. Silver. Zinc.
Ore mill .. 72.2 p.c. 51.0 p.c. 8.1 p.c.
Slime plant.. 80.2 „ . 86.2 „ 8.3 „
So far as the ore mill is concerned it is,
however, only fair to say that, to suit the
smelters, a higher-grade lead concentrate
than usual was produced last halt-year,
which meant a larger escape of metals into
the zinc tailings and the slimes. So far nothing
has been done on a commercial scale in
getting the zinc from the slimesbut ‘ satis-
factory progress has been made in overcom-
ing the difficulties, and when the outlet tor
zinc concentrates improves we shall be able
to start production.” Incidentally, W. J.
Mcßride, M.C., the co.’s chief metallurgist,
who laid the foundations for this slime
treatment, had a good deal to do with
another great mine lately. It blew
up Hill 60. He is an Adelaide grad-
uate, and one of the little group of Aus-
tralian-trained metallurgists who have done
more than the rest of the world in develop-
ing processes for treating lead-zinc sul-
phides.

Twelve months ago the Exchange Buildings
were in the balance-sheet at £69,000, nothing
of consequence having been written off for
many years. They have now been reduced
to £55,000. Other assets, mostly liquid,
total £2884, less current dividend. The
share capital is £30,000, and the debenture
debt £15,000. The property with the hand-
some front and the fine position gives solid
security for the capital; and possibly if it
were handled with a single eye to revenue
it could be better employed. But it serves
to some extent a public purpose ; hence the
comparatively poor return —for the past
three years only a shade over p.c. on the
capital and reserves even as now written
down. The institution is a sort of commer-
cial club without the whisky and cigars, and
the shareholders are apparently satisfied to
get 6 p.c. or so on their money. Anyway,
they have made no visible effort to do any
more during the past 15 years.
Coal, Ltd., holding a big block of coal
land behind the best of the N.S.W. South
Coast group, has tossed out the United
States Finance Corporation. This combina-
tion wanted a 6 p.c. preference on all Ameri-
can shares for 10 years. This was agreed
to. Brother Jonathan, thinking he had putty
to mould, then put up the terms to 10 p.c.
preference for 10 years and £B2OO for what
he termed a publicity campaign. This settled
the deal so far as he was concerned, and
with Wall-street shunted the co. turns to
J. Bull for cash. An influential shipping
co. representing British capital in Australia
is the negotiator. It hasn’t much time to
waste, as labor conditions operate after
November.
The B. 11. Prop. Co.’s Is. is paid on
1.181.006 shares: SI 1.448 are held in
Europe, and a shilling on these takes
£40,572. Australia claims £18,477; Mel-
bourne gets £8383, Adelaide £8378, Sydney
£1263. B. Hill £179 and Port Pirie £166.
On a population basis Australia should pro-
duce 1,000,000 tons of pigdron per annum —
a ton to every five inhabitants. To manu-
facture that quantity the blast furnaces
would require to handle roughly 2,500,000
tons of ore and flux. Actually we are hand-
ling under 750,000 tons, of which the B. H.
Prop.’s lot is 100,000 tons a quarter. But
we shall not want one ton for every five
people until we have an enormous expansion
in secondary manufactures. For instance,
we should have to manufacture all the gal-
vanised-iron we require. That item alone
costs £1,000.000 a year.
* * * *
Bathurst people are going to add another
to the five thousand or so attempts that have
been made to prove that there is an ore
channel connecting the Broken Hill and
Pinnacles lodes. Between these two out-
standing features there is plain with occa-
sional lumps of rock protruding, and at
some time or other almost every protrusion
has been represented as the outcrop of the
functioning lode —the two points are six or
seven miles apart. Some day the lode may
be worked profitably south of the Zinc Cor-
poration’s Block 5, but so far the scores of
cos. which have spawned on the “White
Leads plain,” as it used to be called, have
got no serious encouragement. For that
matter, the Pinnacles itself has merely been
a sink for money. The new concern calls
itself the Caledonia, and the owners get
10.0(X) fully-paids in a 25,000 co., of which
7000 are reserved. If there is anything to
be found it wouldn’t be much use looking for
it with less than £50,000.
Edna Deeps has made more commotion in
the Exchanges of four capitals than any
stock within thedast four or five years. One
Perth and several Adelaide spec.-sellers were
hard hit; and last week Sydney 'Change went
into mourning over a casualty of its own.
That will further strengthen the market, of
course; for there will have to be the usual
buying-in.
* * * *
Few mines in Westralia have shown such
a sudden reversal of form as Edna May
Deeps. At the 4Soft. level a limited patch
of good ore was located; also a huge granite
bar. At this point all the glories of the
Edna May reef had apparently departed, and
it looked any odds on that the 560 ft. level
would be practically valueless. But actually
the 560 ft. level has opened up in a way that
compares very favorably with the best of
Edna May levels. Also, the reef is in some
measure making back south-west, and this is
all in favor of Deeps. Hitherto Deeps man-
agement has not been over-diffident with
official reports, but since locating the good
developments the sudden modesty assumed
has been a pitfall for the bears. Amalgam
is being piled up, and everything now looks
prosperous for Deeps, which, by the way,
was once offered to the Edna May Co. for
£BOOO. Westralian mining has not had such
a turn-up for a long time.
Edna Central got the South reef at No.
4 level about 160 ft. from the shaft. As in
the Deeps the first cut wasn’t much; but
that was not to be wondered at seeing how
things went at No. 3. The best stone lies
to the south-west, and manager Giblin is off
on the hunt thither. Perth is a bit “bully”
over things at the Central, probably because
folk there regard Giblin as a very safe man.
Possibly, too, they think Collins-street won’t
lose the chance to shove up prices if a few
good assays go along. Out east the values
of the stone dipping from the Consolidated
into No. 3 level are shading off. This part
of the mine isn’t being half so closely watched
as the South reef, for people haven’t reckoned
on the Consolidated strike as much chop.
Commodore, Meekatharra (W.A.), is back-
ing Kearns’s Macquarrie lease to give it a
lift. It is a hard go. The price paid
was £lOOO cash and 5000 paid-up shares.
Seeing that the co. got one-half, of the claim
not long since for under £5OO, Kearns makes
a good bargain. As old Commodore slimes
didn’t pan out profits, the co. had to call up
cash to pay off the vendor and make up
the shortage between gold won and working
costs. Hence the balance-sheet just out
doesn’t fill the Board-room with enthusiasm.
The new manager, Bell, looks on the
Macquarrie as a low-grade proposition
which may improve at depth. This is the
old story ; but with the Edna Deeps incident
so recent, nobody is prepared to say him nay.
Bell’s strong point is the development at
Nos. 1 and 2 winzes, where nice quartz is
showing. Still, yields on the Macquarrie
haven’t bulked up to samples.
* * * *
Owing to labor shortage last year, Great
Boulder (W.A.) could not keep its mill
fully supplied with ore. That, coupled with
the high price of stores, meant greatly in-
creased costs. Working profit dropped
nearly £52,000 (to £267,000), and to pay
the usual 3s. div. the co. had to tap its re-
serve fund to the extent of £lO,OOO. Ore
reserves are again less than before, showing
a decline of 54,900 tons in quantity and
£130,000 in value compared with the 1915
estimate. The reserves place the mine about
2i years ahead of the plant; and while an
appreciable portion of the tonnage milled
during the current year will no doubt be
replaced by fresh developments, there is no
disputing the fact that there is nothing to
look forward to in the bottom of the mine.
* * * *
Jack McDermott succeeds Bob Nicolson
in the management of the Ivanlioe (W.A.),
returning after an absence of about 10
years. Formerly he was underground man-
ager, but was induced to join 8., M. & Co.’s
staff and was sent to take charge of the Sons
of Gwalia. From there he went to London,
and after a short spell was sent out by
Govett and others to look after the Cam and
Motor gold-mine in Rhodesia. He has the
reputation of being one of Australia’s best
underground men.
* * * *
Last year was described by chairman
Richards at the Sons of Gwalia meeting as
one of continual anxiety. Shortage of labor,
higher wages and much larger outlay on
supplies had considerably affected opera-
tions and profits. Still, returns were satis-
factory, dividends were being maintained,
and in the bottom of the mine at 3529 ft. on
the underlay there was every sign of con-
tinuity. In regard to the co.’s American
interests, Richards said that the Montezuma
mine (California), in which the co. had sunk
some money, had not come up to expecta-
tions and had been abandoned. The Ply-
mouth Consolidated, in which the co. had a
considerable interest, was doing well and
was likely to be a permanent payer.
* * * *
Who was it let out the secret of the exist-
ence of 2oz. dirt off No. 9 rise at the Langi
South, Ararat (Vic.) ? Shares jumped on
the news; but that doesn’t prove that the
Langi South is no longer a stringer. Even
though 3000 fathoms from it have averaged
an ounce, what is the record? The outgo to
March 31 was £90,411, of which £69,000
came from calls, £11.700 from cash put to
the credit of the co. and only £7360 from
gold. This is since 1910, when the Cath-
cart boom gave the co. a chance to flutter
into life. To do only 3000 fathoms in seven
years’ existence is even worse than the record
of the poor old Spring Hill Lends. What
recent truck-loads from the No. 9 rise of
the Langi South go to prove is just what the
old bores showed in 1915: that in a big
gutter (100 ft. wide like the Langi there arc-
runs of gold of good grade. Yet a truck-
load or two from these do not establish that
the runs are continuous or wide. If they did
extraction wouldn’t have had to stop to allow
more ground to be proved.
This is something like mining. During
.Tune the mostly Sydney-owned Kampong
Kamunting’s two dredges won 86.80 tons
black tin, which realised £10,318. Working
costs were £2034, or 2.8 d. per yard.

PRACTICAL PRESCRIPTION
AGAINST STOMACH ACIDITY.
BY A SPECIALIST.
“Nine-tenths of all cases of stomach
trouble nowadays,” says a leading special-
ist. “are caused by too much acid. In the
beginning the stomach itself is not diseased,
but if this acid condition is allowed to con-
tinue, the acid is very likely to eat into the
stomach walls and produce stomach ulcer,
which may render a radical surgical opera-
tion necessary, even to prolong life. There-
for an ‘acid stomach’ is l-eally a dangerous
condition and should be treated seriously.
It is utterly useless to take pepsin and or-
dinary stomach tablets. The excess acid
must be neutralised by ‘the administration
of an efficient antacid. -For this purpose
physicians nearly always recommend taking
half a teaspoonful of bisurated magnesia
in a little water after each meal. Large
quantities may be used, if necessary, as it
is absolutely harmless. But be sure to get
the bisurated magnesia, as other forms of
magnesia have not the same action in the
stomach as the bisurated, and frequently
do more harm than good.”— Advt.

A WOMNA'S
LETTER
Sydney, August 27, 1917.
My Dear Arini, —
Rose Hall got a big influx of Maorilanders
last week. They’re just-returned men who
are waiting here, as guests of the Red Cross,
till the laid-up steamers get a move on
for the Dominion. Matron Fox and her
volunteers —the Paddington V.A.D.’s are
having their turn now —made up 18 extra
beds on a roomy balcony to meet the rush.
Sergeant Judd, of the Mounted Rifles, is one
of the most amazed men who came back —
amazed to find a strike flourishing with ban-
ners, bands and processions. “We could
hardly believe it,” he says, “for there are
two or three of us who have been nearly
three years at the Front —I left Auckland
in October, 1914. If these strikers could
only see what we’ve seen of the enemy’s
treatment of women and children, they’d—
well, I suppose they can’t realise what this
war means!” Sergeant Pepper, another
M.L. veteran, can only look across the open
space between Rose Hall and the Domain
and listen to the strikers’ band as a man in
a dream, who sees the industrial Nero
ghosts fiddling while the world blazes.
When the news from the Western Front
slackens the London cable correspondent
makes another guess at our next Governor.
The last ticket he took was on Arthur Steel-
Maitland —with three or four other Chris-
tian names in between. He is Parliamen-
tary Under-Sec. for the Colonies, and comes
of military people on both sides. Dad’s
name was Steel: the son became Maitland
when he married into the baronet Maitland
family. At Oxford he mixed classics and
rowing, and is still at 40 something of an
athlete. Except that he has ever so much
more experience of men—and women —he
would probably remind us more of the book-
ish Beauchamp than any other Governor
we’ve had.
A pai’ty of journalists who did Jenolan
Caves early this year had as companion in
toiling up and down the new Orient grottoes
the gentle Edmund Milne, just dead. He
looked a very sick man then, and after
attacking 1400-odd steps had very little
breath to spare. But with that he had he
pointed out “the wonders of the Lord,” and
quoted extensively from the Book, which he
could have followed up with a batch of
Psalms but for the distressing cough that
had to be noticed.
A.-D.-C. d’Apice has got the right answer
from Dorothy Maher, younger daughter of
the late Dr. Odillo Maher, of this hamlet.
A sister is the youthful widow of Dr. Roger
Hughes, who was killed in France some
months ago.
When W. A. Windeyer’s idea of an un-
fenced street-garden at Hunter’s Hill took
root and sprouted, he found that unofficial
gardeners were getting busy. Invisible
fingers picked the first blooms, and small
feet left their prints on the soil that was
to rear flowers for the War Chest. So he
got the weight of local juvenile opinion on
his side, distributed tools here and there, and
had a swarm of hon. gardeners aflame to
catch the culprit who might be led astray by
nodding sweet peas. Result, a handsome
hedge of blossoms, and everything in knicker-
bockers a special constable-in-charge.
Captain Jack Chisholm and his bride are
back from Egypt. She was Madge Giles,
a daughter of the Sydney Grammar School
dominie.
Sydney girls remember Gerry Cooper as
a first-rate accompanist and keen amateur
musician; also as a pleasant young man for
tea-parties. Now he is doing something
more useful, and has been doing it so well
as a lieutenant in the Scots’ Guards that
he has won the Military Cross. He is the
younger son of Willie Cooper, Bart., one of
this hamlet’s big propputy owners. There’s
an elder brother in the same Big Fight.
There will be no difficulty about a pass-
port for Miss Soubeiran, who goes to France
to see that the splendid results of France’s
Day fall into the right hands. Founder
of the French League in Sydney, this ardent
patriot has been exceptionally lucky in her
co-workers. But the hive in Macdonell
House is a monument no less to the tenacity
of the Australian kiddy, whose nimble
fingers have stitched many thousands of
little garments for baby Francois.
Sergeant Sinclair, now on the Vic. State
War Council, crossed the Murray last week.
He is the expert in tweeds who has intro-
duced the hand-loom to disabled Billjim.
Tall and blue-eyed, with a son at the Front,
the Scot is a keen business man, who years
ago blew into Invercargill-—the M.L. town
where everyone clings to a Tam o’ Shanter
nine months out of 12—from Caithness,
N.B. For the last 10 winters he has been
a Victorian. “When I began to talk about
hand-woven tweeds as an industry for dis-
abled soldiers,” he says, “there was no
enthusiasm. It seemed to strike cold. But
I hammered on, and got the chance to stand
or fall by an exhibit at the A.N.A. show
some months back. That satisfied the
authorities that there was money in the
scheme. * King George got the first suit.
The Governor-General had to wait for his
tweeds, but he is wearing them now.” The
Red Cross mothers the new industry in Syd-
ney, and the work will be in full hum in a
few weeks in Lawson House. Disabled
soldiers unlit for outdoor jobs can, Sergt. Sin-
clair claims, earn anything up to 12s. a day
of six hours at the loom. Samples of the
fine hand-woven goods are on view in Challis
House.
Jean, the nice-looking daughter of the
Bob Andersons, lost her fiance, J. C. Wright,
four days before her parents left Loudon.
She remains there with friends, and has
plunged still deeper into war-work to dull
the shock. Anderson, knighted for his work
as business organiser at Headquarters, lifts
his hat if you mention the military nurses
on the wrecked P. and O. Mongolia. They
were Sisters Manson, Despard and Foote,
returning to Australia on leave. “A perfect
godsend to everyone,” he says, “for the beach
was quite unsheltered but for a sail which
they tried to rig up over the sick.” Mr.
Winchcombe, the N. S. Wales M.L.C., with
double pneumonia, was brave and cheerful.
“They tell me I haven’t a hope,” he whis-
pered, “but I think I’ll beat them yet.”
A Bangalow farmer, C. W. Reading, hap-
pened to be in Belgium when the Mad Dog
broke loose. He joined the Belgian army
as a private, rose step by step, was deco-
rated for his gallantry in action, and,
severely wounded at last, was invalided to
Australia in the Mongolia. When the ex-
plosion came he tried to save the wounded
leg, but, falling, broke both limbs. “Amaz-
ing fortitude!” says a fellow passenger. “He
just gritted his teeth and tried to joke in
his torment.”
In the Mitchell Library is the first
attempt, so far as history knows, to make
the emu and kangaroo the heraldic symbol
of Australia. Painted on satin in 1806, by
women of the Bowman family, who were
early settlers on the Hawkesbury, it shows
the Bird and Beast on either side of the
Rose, Thistle and Shamrock, supported by a
shield. The art is crude, but the faded
colors, with the word “Unity” on top, still
grip the passer-by. This ancient flag was
designed for a Hawkesbury festival soon
after the news of the Battle of Trafalgar
had slowly worked its way to Sydney Cove.
When this troubled
planet ceases to boil,
Marie Narelle will
probably make a
tour of Australia.
The handsome singer
took her profes-
sional name from
Narellan, the little
N. S. Wales town
where she grew into
splendid woman-
hood. Like her
cousin, Eva Mylott,
at whose wedding-
party in New York
Marie was recently
hostess, she is built
on sumptuous lines.
And there’s a nature
sweet as honey un-
der the creamy skin. Music flows like run-
ning water through the family; daughter
Kathleen w r on a Royal Academy scholarship
a while ago. Marie’s second matrimonial
venture has been a success, and electrical-
engineer Currie considers himself a lucky
American. Yet with a beautiful summer home
on Long Island and most of the good things
of life, there’s something else she’s craving
for —a glimpse of Australia. Here is her
latest photo from New York.
Lieut. Bruce Hardy Arnott, a veteran at
21, with the Croix de Guerre to his credit,
adds another name to the toll of war. His
father, a lieu t.-colonel, is fighting in France,
where the lad has just been killed. His
brave mother, described as one of the
heroines of the lost Mongolia, who helped
and cheered others in the confusion of the
tragedy, recently returned to Sydney.
Melba comes overland on September 3, and
the Veterans’ Home concert is off till she
returns from the U.S.A.
Lionel' Lawson and fiddler Roberts, of the
Criterion orchestra, join the Richmond
Aviation School this week.
The journalists who belong to tlie-ah
“Institute” as distinguished from the sordid
Association fed Cyril Maude at Farmer’s one
day last week. They found a pleasant, dap-
per little man with a frank admiration tor
Cyril Maude —an attitude which gives an
almost infantile candor to his cheery prattle.
But he was half an hour late. Busy at a
meeting in connection with the Actors’ Asso-
ciation matinee, he forgot the time; and he
“expected to be fetched.” While the com-
pany was wondering how the strike cuisine
was standing the strain of a long stage-wait
a hurried pilgrim burst in, explained the
tragedy, and entreated the pressmen not to
let the chop get any colder.
About 10 years
ago Dorothy Wilson
(now Mrs. Wardill,
of Melbourne) sug-
gested to her friend
Ada Newman that
it might be possible
to found an Arts
and Crafts Society.
Mrs. Danvers Power
became the president
of the brand-new
association, and it is
still, in spite of the
war, an active con-
cern, with head-
quarters in Penfold’s
Building. Of the
founders, Ada New-
man, previously de-
voted to water-colors,
is in these days one of Sydney’s leading
china-painters, and some of her work graces
the National Gallery. Specialising in Aus-
tralian flora, she has for years rifled the
bush for the brilliant bursts of color that
make a sunset out of the young tips of the
gum-trees. These glowing tints she pre-
serves on delicate white and biscuit back-
grounds. She bakes with care —always
twice and sometimes four times; for the
firing’s the thing. There is a peep-hole in
her kiln, where you smell the temperature.
But it takes experience to sniff the air and
tell whether it suits the bush flower: some
like it as they get it in a heat wave at
Bourke; others faint if there’s more than a
w 7 arm glow—sunshine on ice, as Nature
serves it up in 'a Leura valley. Here is
Judith Fletcher’s picture of the good Aus-
tralian artist.
“Grumpy’s” best yarn was really against
himself. “She was a charming young Ameri-
can lady interviewer, but I noticed that she
was particularly keen about my pedigree. So
I said: ‘Er —you seem very interested in my
family. Are you—er—of particularly ancient
lineage yourself?’ ‘Why—yes—that’s so !’
she replied. ‘I come from Napoleon on one
side and Sir Joshua Reynolds on the other.’ ”
If ever a smart young woman pulled the silk
sock of an actor that U.S.A. damsel did.
Cyril Maude’s press-agents have plastered
both sides of the equator with the names of
his distinguished friends and relatives, and
Miss Starry Stripes met the situation nobly.
Lieut. Langlois Lefroy (British Army
Service Corps), son of Westralian Premier
H. B. Lefroy, has gained the Military Cross.
His pre-war occupation was raising sheep on
a vast run near Cue.
The next exhibition of Mrs. Ellis Rowan’s
water-colors should be rich in pictures
painted in New Guinea. She is at work on
flower-studies as far afield as the Bismarck
Mountains, where her hut is almost along-
side a volcanic crater owned by a herd of
cannibals. The latter, however, have
assured her of their goodwill and the balance
of power is steadied with tobacco. The
little Victorian artist’s only fear is that her
supply of paint will be exhausted before she
is ready to leave.
Almost anything can be an “outrage”
now. N. S. Wales Director of Education
Board lately issued a mild memorandum to
teachers asking them to allow pupils to col-
lect bottles for the Voluntary War Workers
and store them at the schools. Then up
rose a newspaper correspondent named
“Old Rags Next,” and a scornful laugh
laughed he. “This is one of the most
ghastly outrages ever inflicted on the public-
school teachers,” howled “Old Rags Next,”
and, swallowing his cane, expired in great
agony, but with the dignity befitting one
of the Old School.
The war has many coincidences, but this
one is near home. On July 24, 1915,
Lieut. Harry Robson, of Ballina, on the
North Coast, was killed at Gallipoli.
Exactly 12 months later his brother, Walter,
was killed in France.

There’s to be a bumper concert for the
54th and 55th Heavy Artillery Comforts
Fund on September 19 in the King’s Hall
Crossed over the Great Divide last week
at 93, Mrs. A. Hinton, of Mosman, mother
of Howard, well known in philanthropic
circles, and Muriel, who does a bit with he r
pen. Mrs. Hinton was a daughter of Gen
eral Muriel, who commanded in India about
the time Victoria took up the sceptre and
commenced her virtuous reign. Up till the
last the old lady, who was strong in Christian
Science beliefs, climbed the Mosman hills
with the agility of the best strike-time
walker, never halting by the way.

benevolent fund the fat harvest it hopes to
garner from a big matinee on September 4
Best seats are going for a guinea each.
Nell Black, with a sister and brother—
they belong to a musical family—was
lunched by the Voluntary Workers’ Cafg
committee the other noon. They have been
the Cafd’s voluntary fiddlers at meal-time
and now move off on a professional tour.
Walking up Wynyard-street, on the left-
hand side you’ll come on an alert-looking
establishment with the sign of the Red Cross
and making urgent appeal for wounded boys
struggling through a winter of suffering and
uncertainty; boys whose sturdy limbs the
German shell has shattered, or turned into
darkness the once keen sight. In this
anxious-to-please little shop that holds out
eager hands for your shillings and pence is
the most fascinating array of basket-ware—
tall and short, round and slim, all waiting
for a mistress. Wouldn’t you love a work-
basket woven in your boy’s battalion colors?
Carved trays and tables and caskets and' toys
—real Australian kangaroos and bears; and
whole families of rabbits—Mother and
Father, Peter and Baby. Peter Rabbit
rattles. A rocking-chair with two carved
’iroos on its sides to set the pace holds a
world of joy for some lucky youngster this
Christmas. Oh, there are a hundred things
to tell you about, but go and see for your-
self the leather handbags, and the bead
necklaces woven in tints of blue and copper,
pink and lavender, lilac and gold, and you’ll
then know that the depot holds a fit present
for every 7 member of the family on every
occasion. Christmas is drawing close.
All the latest theatrical male-arrivals
wear the top-coat with a waist and a skirt
that belonged to the Englishman of Du
Maurier’s day. It suggests (I don’t) cor-
sets.
There’s a new 7 use for the black sheep.
The back-country war-sock maker knew
that there’s usually one or two dark-com-
plexioned woollies in every flock, but gener-
ally not enough to make up even a bag of
fleece. That was the girl’s chance. Ac-
quiring a spinning-wheel she begs the black
sheep’s w r ool, and has the stuff ready dyed.
That’s the basis of many a sock knitted in
the tiniest country schools. Mostly it’s the
school-miss who does both the begging and
the spinning. Dear, mostly-forgotten little
school-miss! None of the excitements of
Australia Day or France’s Day come to her.
But as the wdieel flies round she is spinning
something more than wool: there’s a web
being w r oven round many a bush-mother’s
heart. And —well, perhaps round Bill-
jitn’s, too.
Yours affectionately,
VAN DORIAN.

THE “GLENSTER”
THE HAT OF DISTINCTION.
Exclusively-dressed women know exactly how high
the name of “Glenster” stands for Hats of distinc-
tion. Great indeed is our pleasure in announcing
that we have the Sole Agency in Australia
for the “Glenster” Hats. The present collection
embraces some exquisite straw tailor shapes. Each
entirely differs from its neighbor; each, the con-
ception of an artist for perfection of line; each a
study in simplicity and rarity of coloring. It is
fitting, therefore, that Sydney’s House of Distinction
should introduce the “Glenster” to distinctive
dressers.

Melbourne, August 27, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie.—
Rear-Admiral Cresswell, first professional
member of the Naval Board, is mourning the
loss of one of his three boys—Sub.-lieut.
Colin, of the Royal Navy, killed in action on
August 20. Schooled at Brisbane. Melbourne
and Osborne (Isle of Wight), the boy was
with the Minotaur on the China station until
he joined the Australia, going thence to the
North Sea submarine service. Lieut.
Edmund Creswell, of the Engineers, is being-
invalided from France, wounded. The eldest
son. Captain Randolph W., won the Serbian
Order of the White Eagle while with the
Camel Corps, and was mentioned in Sir
Archibald Murray’s dispatches. A Melbourne
Grammar boy, he is still 27 years young.
A Ballarat Anzac, Lieut. Harold Wanliss,
who got to France from Gallipoli, has been
mentioned again in dispatches after winning
the D.S.O. The young officer is nephew of
Colonel Wanliss and also of Iceberg Irvine’s
dame.
Jack, son of Admiral Bridges, has been
winged in France, and had a whiff of gas as
well. Mother and Sissie are handy in
London. Sissie was one of the first V.A.D.
triers at Melbourne Hospital, and is now
helping at the Anzac Buffet.
Captain Alan Currie, chairman of the
V.A.T.C. before he gave up sport for war.
has got the Military Cross. One of the Field
Artillery, he was severely wounded while
doing some big thing.
Syd Dalrymple, a jolly Toorak boy with
rich parents, has come a cropper while flying,
and mother is busy using the cable. Syd
was made a flight-commander in the R.F.C.
about the time that his pal, Gordon Ross
Soden, was wounded in France while test-
ing new aircraft guns.
Walbundrie, the Kiddle station near
Albury, has been bought by the Government
for repatriation purposes. When William
Kiddle died, he left Walbundrie to son
Geoffrey and daughter Rita. Geoffrey was
killed recently and Rita, the last of the
family, has just arrived from England and
is proudly introducing her invalided soldier
turned.
Melba visited the Point Cook aviation
school last week and gaily accepted an invi-
tation to go up where the rival lark sings.
When she got back to earth she was as
pleased as a flapper after her first dance.
Her pal, the dimpled little Lady Susan, was
also taken aloft by one of the aviators.
The Rajah and his missus Avere well estab-
lished at Menzies’. But on Wattle Day the
loungers at the Oriental were entertained by
the sudden arrival of a mountain of luggage,
a valet and an angry-looking Rajah. The
new guest registered, and then everything
was swallowed by the lift. “Whatever is
the matter?” yawned the loungers like weary
alligators, and immediately subsided Avithout
trying to answer their OAvn query.
The ShadoAV tAvo-step at Melba’s farewell
frivol to the Best People was a ripping
success. It is not a novelty in humbler
circles, having been a popular item at roller-
skating carnivals in pre-war days, when the
Avowser Avowsed heavily at it, but it has
only just moved up. In loAvly circles it is
termed “The Spooning Whirl.”
From a man friend Avho takes such things
very seriously:—
Architect Frank Stapley has suddenly come into
the limelight as the man who will probably snatch
the city rabbit-skins from David Hennessy on Octo-
ber 9. A majority of councillors want a change,
and this time, it is said, there is to be no mistake.
AVeedon and Cabena have both been mentioned ; but
during the past week or two matters have been
earnestly talked over by councillors, with the result
that Stapley’s supporters definitely declare that their
man will be at least one vote ahead of Hennessy
when the numbers go up, while any other candidates
Avill be fighting it out for third place in the blue
distance. Stapley is more genial and less ponderous
than Hennessy. Mrs. Stapley is a patron o f the
arts, musically inclined, and a practical dame of
sound common-sense.
Coombe Cottage, Lilydale, Avas turned out
by Melba the other night to give some Bill-
jims a party. The singer called up dancers
from Point Cook, Maribyrnong and Broad-
meadows, imported Di Gilio, the music-
maker, and asked a bunch of pretty Avomen,
including Lady Susan, Doris BlackAA’ood, the
tAVO upstanding Willie Clarke girls, Mrs.
George Armstrong, Everard BroAvne’s girleen
Gwynneth, and Mrs. Kerr-Pearse. The
hostess wore a moonlight-blue frock, and saAV
that everyone had a good time. Just before
dawn the sleepy hamlets of Lilydale and
Ringwood were roused from their rustic
slumbers by a whirring cavalcade of town-
returning guests and cheery soldiers singing
camp songs.
The Hon. Clement Charles Molesworth,
son of the present Viscount, has been killed
in action. Our own Charles Molesworth,
who used to grow wool in the Western Dis-
trict, is now direct heir to the title. He was
uncle to the dead soldier.
Melba and her daughter-in-law have been
staying at Federal Government House
with the G.-G. and Lady Helen. Mrs.
Armstrong came to town because her husband
the wharf-laborer was too weary to get back
to Coombe Cottage after his day’s work.
Incidentally, when George registered at the
National Service Bureau and put a hook
in his belt, the excitable girl pupils at the
Con. got almost hysterical with admiration.
One small fair Toorakese damsel, who culti-
vates a tiny voice, and whose papa has
money to roll in, wanted to volunteer straight
away as a coal-lumper. Her big contralto
pal considered the job of a tally-clerk would
do her. A couple of mezzo-soprani reckoned
they would prefer to take lessons in winch-
driving.
As soon as Adela Pankhurst was released
on bail, she rushed about holding open-air
meetings. The police moved her on, and
Spring-street had various visions of Adela
carrying her toy platform (a stool) and
streaking for a new point of vantage with
a large, rag-tag army at her heels. As
I watched her from afar, I was in some
way reminded of a harassed cat lugging a
helpless kitten from one refuge to another.
Ivo Whitton, who dropped his champion
golf clubs for the rifle, is to marry a daugh-
ter of the Curries of Derrinallum.
Adelaide’s enclosure: —
After an outburst of summer that frayed our tem-
pers the weather relapsed into winter with an
emphasis that flung snow along- the hilltops from
Mount Gambier to Petersburg. Heavy seas on the
coast removed a little more of the Glenelg break-
water and added to the damage that the previous
storm had done to jetties.
Returning transports loosed a stream of returned
Billjims, wounded or recuperating, but none the less
cheerful. They were taken by motors in enthusiastic
loads to the violet farms and through the hills. Most
of them had started back home from the Somme, and
not one of them had a shadow of doubt as to the
respective merits of Europe and Australia.
Bishop Wilson, who has been warning the heathen
at St. Andrew’s, Walkerville, since he resigned from
Melanesia, is to leave us at the end of the year to
bish. at Bunbury. He was a keen cricketer in his
pre-button-up-the-baek days, and once gave an Aus-
tralian Eleven some unpleasant leather-chasing when
lie was playing for Kent.
The S. O. Smiths, whose son has for months been
“missing,” have been officially notified that he was
killed in France in early November. His father is
one of the Yalumba Smith clan.
D. 0. Scott, of Itapunda, has been added to our
list of S.M.’s. He is to have either Port Pirie or
Mount Gambier. He is a son of the late W. D.
Scott, who for a good many years was Master of the
Supreme Court.
The crowning of Mrs. Kidman as Kapunda’s Aus-
tralia Day Queen took Lady Galway to that town
on Wednesday. She was the guest of the H. H.
Duttons, at Anlaby. Her Ex.’s appeal for funds for
prisoners of war is bringing in a good many sub-
scriptions. On Friday a “Bon Marche,” organised
by the Mayoress’s Red Cross Guild in the Town Hall,
added about £250.
About the same time J. M. Richman gave a block
of land at Prospect to be sold for war funds. It
was bought by F. W. Bullock for £6OO. Inciden-
tally the War Council has decided that the housing
of incapacitated or homeless returned soldiers is a
matter for the Federal or State Government, and
not the business of private bodies. Therefore, no
existing home is to extend its operations, nor are
any new ones to be started ; but the War Council’s
plans for the erection of a hostel are to be gone on
with.
Died at North Adelaide at the end of the week
R. de la Poer Beresford, whose father was private
secretary to Governor Mac Donnell and afterwards
Clerk of the Assembly. The widow is a daughter of
the late John Acraman.
The Moncrieffs’ handsome daughter, who left some
months ago for China and Japan, has said her
marriage vows to an American engineer named Ran-
dolph, who is keeping an eye on some railway con-
struction in China.
The trouble in our Returned Soldiers’ Association
came to a head last week, when a crowded meeting
was nearly unanimous in supporting A. E. Tait, the
secretary lately suspended, and in carrying a no-
confidence motion against the committee which sus-
pended him. Col. Weir and Blackburn, V.C., the
most prominent men so far in the R.S.A., then left,
and a mostly-fresh committee was appointed, on a
basis of “equality all round and no military distinc-
tions to be made.”
Resignations are flying as a result of a
row in the Malvern Red Cross. Some of
the Very Naicest put the blame of the whole
upset on to “those interfering men” who
have been exercising control where they are
not wanted. Not by the controlled ones,
anyhow.
Wattle Day was wet —a sad blow to the
hopes of the buttoners. However, a roaring
trade was done in trams, trains and cafes.
Mrs. de Bavay, just recovered from a severe
illness, had her motor snugly enclosed in
wattle branches, and made many sales in
C'ollins-street. Mrs. Michaelis and a bunch
of girls sold wattle from the Grand Hotel
steps and indulged in tea-parties indoors at
intervals. The other fashionable taverns
were tired this year, and had no shows. The
Overseas Club had a big cafef chantant in
the Independent Hall. Allan Wilkie and
Walter Hunt did the quarrel scene from
“Julius Caesar” in trousers. Lois sang (how
remarkably like Armes Beaumont he is in
his platform attitude!), Clifton Boanas
played, and Stella Power carolled.
The Foulidling Home Babies put out a
placard, “Babies At Home from Two till
Four,” and the Town Hall was rushed at a
shilling a head. The dumplings were care-
fully conveyed through the rain in fashion-
able' motors, and they and their nurses were
mobbed going and coming.
The undergrads are going to do their
whirling best for the Red Cross on Saturday,
September S, with a torchlight procession
from “the shop” to the Exhibition, where
they will incite citizens made sober by Time
to take part in a confetti battle and spend
their hard-earned on all sorts of vanities.
The Children’s Plain and Poster Ball in
aid of St. Vincent’s has been postponed
from September 5 to ditto 10.
There are still bright spots in strike-
stricken Melbourne. Melba looked in the
other morning at the National Service
Bureau, whence the wharves are being
staffed by volunteer workers, and offered to
do her bit. “I would be prepared to accept,
work at the wharf,” said she. “My son,
George Armstrong, is there already. More-
over,” she added, “I’ve a staff of 150 pupils
who are willing to enrol.” So we may yet
see Nellie sitting on the capstan-head of a
Norwegian timber-ship, setting the time for
a chanty.
The benefit given to poor, half-blind tenor
Lois was packed by the fashionable and the
rich. Balboni, whose concert dates com-
pletely hedged in the deserving Lois affair,
scored merely a handful of an audience in
spite of his great voice. The musical held
off indignantly because, so they said, Bal-
boni had based his programme, song for
song, on the well-advertised one sung by
Lois.
The doctors advise a difficult operation on
Lois’s eyes which may or may not stave off
blindness. The neat money results of the
benefit will come in very handy.
Millie Macaw’s concert (profits for the
trenches) was a flattering tribute to that
charming young songbird, the house being
crowded to the last seat. Millie, who is a
Melba pupil, wore a rich voice, a white
tulle-frock and an air of great assurance.
The programme was varied and unusual, and
included some dainty “Quality Street” tab-
leaux by Mrs. Oswald Syme and the Baillieu
children.
Melba caused a sensation with a surprise
song at her pupils’ concert last week. St.
Leger was billed to play Percy Grainger’s
“Colonial Song,” and half-way through the
Voice joined in from nowhere at all. The
listeners craned and twisted to see where
Melba was hidden, but couldn’t find her.
When she arrived at the concert she. was
conspicuous in a huge mantle of pale ivory
brocade etched in dark fur. She slipped out
of this and, in an unobtrusive black clingy
dress, dropped into a vacant chair between
two black-coated Governors. Sitting there
she sang, and the effect was electrical. St.
Leger had to bow repeatedly to the applause.
He came out time and again, grinning mod-
estly, until at last Melba gave him her hand
from below deck. In a storm of applause
she came on to the platform and sang again.
There was a great house. The G.-G. and
Lady Helen were there; also Governor
Stanley and his delicate little wife. Lady
Susan’s blue-snooded fair head showed among
the vice-regal bunch. Natalie Muir, the star
who won a Melba scholarship, trilled; and
Violet Coneannen, the little Westralian
brunette, scored heavily. This youngster
broke down some months ago, and Melba
petted her and packed her off home for a
holiday. The generous prescription acted
like magic. Peggy Centre, the coy little
Honolulu lady, had a turn; and Stella
Power got a great reception and the only
encore. She sang from behind a bouquet
of pink roses nearly as big as herself. Also
she was in blue, while all the other girls
but one wore white, with white shoes and
stockings (by command). The one was a
little American pupil, Marion Lloyd Jones,
who sang two eighteenth-century songs and
dressed in blue and white and powder to
match the period.

the National Service Girls’ Dramatic Co.

Co. is a new sign of the times.

THE COMFORTER.
"After last night, I don’t think I’ll touch
another drink as long as I live!”
"Well, cheer up, old hoy—perhaps it doesn’t
matter much. You don't look as though
you'll live long.”
“Captain”: On the Markham River,
New Guinea, native widows have some weird busi-
ness to go through after the boss has snuffed it/ The
bereaved one is locked up for four months, but is
allowed out at early morning and late night to weep
on the grave of the departed. Any small children
she may have are shut up also. Though the husband
is buried underneath the house his grave is sup?
posed to be at a more distant spot, denoted by a
pole, where all his worldly belongings—hats, orna-
ments, paddles, nets and weapons —are hung till they
decay. After six months, during which time the
widow must not leave the village, she may marry
again. Meanwhile she is not allowed to work in the
fields, the tribe thus giving her time to catch
another man. This custom applies to all the Lower
Markham villages, but not the coastal ones. Along
the Upper Markham the lonely missus is still worse
off. There the late hubby is planted in a shallow
grave covered with day wood only. In a couple of
months the widow takes the -head from the body,
cleans the skull, 'and must drink water at least once
out of it in the presence of the tribe, and always,
no matter where she may roam, she must carry it
inside a grass jumper, which she wears for the pur-
pose. The skull, by the constant friction against
her breasts, becomes polished like ivory. This
practice applies to the “big” men of the village only.
The rest of their bones are thrown into the river.
The bones of the lesser men are burnt. Widows are
known by a white grass affair that they wear oyer
their heads, much resembling in shape a monk’s
cowl. Widowers sport a black hat, more like half
a green coeoanut than anything else. They must
keep it on night and day till they slay a member of
another tribe. They are consequently rather keen on
visiting other villages. When a sorcerer is killed a
big pole is erected, and the natives that took part
in the obliteration act dance round and hurl at it
the spears used in the good work. A feast is part of
the ceremony. Any : good dog that has barked its
last is buried in the village, and a small bamboo
fence is erected around the grave, on top of which is
deposited spears and .jaw-bones of wild pigs. The
dog is supposed to rise at night and feed on the
bones, and if they’re not good enough he takqs. the
weapons and goes hunting for himself.
* * * * .
“Karee”: Strange how little one fefeM a
hit at first. I’ve seen chaps Qarry on until some-
body pointed out that they were covered with blood,
when they’d collapse with the discovery. I remem-
ber, on the Patagonian pampa, years ago, during a
wedding brawl, a stray shot lopped the discolored
bridegroom’s thumb off. He pointed excitedly at the
ground, rolling: “Dios de mi alma, what the blazes
is that funny little thing?” or words to that effect.
It was his own dismembered thumb. Another gauclio
got his eye whipped out with a naraja. His adver-
sary, touched by the sight, kissed and made Mends,
and, hoisting him on his caballo, started to take him
eight miles to the doctor. After the first mile,
Esteban, tired of being lashed on the cheek with his
eye, which was hanging out of its socket, casually
cut the shreds off and, wrapping the organ in his
handkerchief, tore away. On arriving at the doc.’s
estancia, he was greatly disgusted to hear that he’d
settled his own hash, and that nothing could be
done for him; so, after getting it dressed and
another nip of cana de Paraguay on top of his load
of aguardiente, he rode back into the wilderness.
“Cylinder”: Whoso sayeth that the moun-
tain duck is not a good table bird liveth in the
midst of bad cooks. I never had him served up to
me when I was domiciled in the East, but when I
lobbed into Westralia and dallied with one or tivo, I
said grace—“ Lord, this is the place for me.” What
a substantial steak can be cut from that noble
breast! . .Generally the black duck is supposed to be
the best of the family, but, in my opinion, the chest-
nut-breasted teal (king or mountain teal) is abso-
lutely On his own for the table—that is,, of matured
birds. But for a blithering beaut, as grub, I’ll
vote first place to the young mountain.
* * * »
“Imlay”; The grey thrush often enters
rooms and picks up crumbs, etc., but the blue wren
family are not quite so venturesome. However, the
4 -o’clock tea-party at a bush mansion that I know
has occasional visits from the turquoise-crowned lord
of a blue-wren family which lives in the garden.
Having collected biscuit and cake-
crumbs he bops out and joins his
harem. Wild magpies are fed in
winter at the same establishment.
A few taps on the plate of scraps
will bring the birds around the door.
One which, like the rest, disappeared
in the nesting season turned up after
about a year’s absence with a growth
on its leg and toes, resembling
“bumble-foot” in fowls. The little
chap uas caught and operated on, and
kept caged till the wound healed. It
alloM-ed the amateur surgeon to get
through the work without making
an\ r trouble, and when released again
joined the free-eounter-lunch crowd.
Writer has regaled yellow-breasted
robins, blue wrens and Jacky-winters (which will
pick up worms, etc., at one’s very feet) on insects
and bread and cheese out in the open. The yellow-
bobs went nap on cheese, but neither blue wren nor
J.W. would touch it.

“Sam OTI.”: The method of relieving
sores and galls as suggested recently by “L.0.U.,”
was not a bad idea about 20 years ago. We do 'it
different now. Instead of packing around the wound
with pads, etc., make a small slit in the lining of
the collar or saddle and take out a portion of
the hair where the pressure is. This allows the
sore to sink into the opening. The onlv time
a pad is wanted is when the trouble is on the back-
bone.

LITTLE BILLJIM: (according to instructions): “An' please watch over Daddy wherever

“Leo Lear”: The Tasmanian. Government
should act on “Ornis’s” plea on behalf of the mutton-
birds on the Bass Strait Islands, for if they arc
not protected there they are doomed, despite their
seemingly countless numbers. Of the thousands
that settle on the mainland few fly ofl to sea again.
The fox sees to that, the mutton’s habits making him
an easy catch for the imported smell. In the sum-
mer of 1912-13 I wandered round the lonely wild
headlands at the back of Portland (Vic.), and found
the coastline for miles strewn with bodies of
dead mutton-birds. In every case the fowl had been
torn open and some of the internal organs taken.
Foxes, which are thick in the locality, are alone
responsible. For the muttons it is islands or extinc-
tion. Incidentally, until then I did not know that
the birds wandered so far West. Anyone seen them
still nearer sunset?

“Ocker” : For the information of
“Trafalgar,” who asks for records of big shearing
tallies. George Myles, 53 years old, has shorn over
400,000 sheep, and is still turning them out. This
year, at Yorkshire Downs (N.Q.), lie was barbering
up to 160 per diem. But for real old-timers “Tra”
falgar” wants to get out Whitecliffs way. One
there, known as “The .Tap,” has been wool-hawking
for over 50 years, and still does his century and a
half. Neither of these men are booze enthusiasts.

Birds which breed on the bare
sand and soft earth, such as dottrels, oyster-catchers
and plover, rely to a great extent on “protective
mimicry” for the concealment of their eggs, i.e.,
the coloring of the shells most perfectly harmonises
with their surroundings. But the hooded dottrrl
(silvery grey-brown with velvety black cowl around
the head and cheeks) and the red-capped dottrel
(pale brown with white underparts and a rust-red
crown), strangely enough, leave a score of tiny
tracks radiating in every direction from
their nests, which are mere shallow
saucer-shaped hollows in sand or soft
earth, so that they are easily discovered.
The oyster-catcher and black-fronted dot-
trel, on the other hand, fly to and from
their nests, and thus leave no guide to
their whereabouts. The spurwing plover
sneaks away from its eggs, while the
plains, or black-breasted, plover ap-
proaches or departs on the wing, and
sometimes endeavors to carry the egg-
away, one by one, in the beak when the
home is discovered. Plover and dottrell
chicks so match their surroundings that
they disappear at one’s very feet as if
by magic, melting, as it were, into the
ground. Immediately one of the parent
birds utters the danger call — very
different from its ordinary note —the
chicks take cover. In other instances it
is the bird alone which is protectively
colored. Thus the large egg of the
white-throated nightjar, blotched and
spotted with black on a ground of cream,
is a most conspicuous object on the hare
earth on which it is laid. But the
plumage of the bird itself —a harmony
in browns, greys, white and black —most
perfectly matches the ground tints of the desolate
ranges on which it most frequently nests; hence it
easily escapes notice when squatting, still as ileath,
cn its single egg.

MICK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mick the mailman’s going on his last, long ride,
A crowd of men behind him and the leader's pacing
slow ;
It’s a smoother track to travel than the one he used,
to go,
And the horses with their nodding plumes are step-
ping it with pride:
For they know they bear the pick
Of the coaching kings in Mick,
Mho never flogged a willing horse or spared a
slacker’s hide.
The black coach has started and the long day’s done.
’Twas little time he had for rest this side the boun-
dary-gate,
And now he will not worry when the inward mails
are late,
Or the plains are parched and withered in the hot,
December sun ;
He will not care a jot
If the roads are bad or not,
Or whether on the watershed there’s heavy rain or
none.
Mick has left his record on the wide, grey plains.
No one caught him running late when all the tracks
were glue,
And never flood was big enough to stop him getting
through ;
Though the creeks were running bankers in the
sodden winter rains
It was bullock out and in,
Wet and frozen to the skin,
IVith nothing but a peg of rum to pay him for his
pains.
Ilalf-a-score of townships knew his lean, brown face;
Forty homesteads watched his coach come swinging
round the bend ;
Splitters in their lonely camps found in him a
friend ;
His flashing headlights marked the time in many a
lonely place,
Where a loud, excited hum
Told the boys the mails had come ;
And the rumble of his muddy wheels broke the
bonds of space.
Money makes the mare go, runs the old, loud lie;
Tell me did he battle for his wage of fifty bob?
Or was it faith and honor, and a liking for his job
That made him run to schedule time in weather wet
or dry?
Did terror of the sack
Make him forge along the track?
Or was it the same manly pride that shows men how
to die?
Westward go the black duck on their long low flight,
Deeper on the straggling town the twilight shadows
fall,
And Mick lias started outward on the biggest trip of
all,
With a light load on the buckboard, and his hooks
and traces right;
AVhen the bells for closing chime
He will struggle through on time,
Though the Jordan runs a banker and the road is
dark as night.
VICTORIA.
BANK DALY.

"It.C.D." : Re “L.’s" story of the old gin
placing her grandchild to her own breast for sus-
tenance after its mother’s death (B. 26/7/’l7). An
even more remarkable case came under the notice of
an Australian girl nursing in the backblocks. When
black mother died the piccaninny was suckled by a
virgin; at least, the foster-mother was unmarried
and without child. The milk was brought to the
breasts by a process of poulticing, but the only in-
gredient in the mixture that the white woman could
discover was mud.

was 7ft. long. Now work out the size of the fish
he must have found. By the way, the fattest pigs
around the port are those which have a daily meal

“Itan”: Amongst the divining-rod sceptics
i> ‘'Petifi' (B. 2/8/’l7), who doubts the power of
the Rev. E. Pocknall, of Tenterfield (N.S.W.), to
ascertain the depth of flows by means of a perpen-
dicular rod. These doubts arise mainly because
there have been so many failures to find water
where the rod lias seemingly indicated its presence
to persons who could not tell the difference between
mineral and water, or soda, magnesia and other sub-
stances. Pocknall has so developed his knowledge
that he can overcome all these difficulties, and* he
has one rod which will turn for nothing but fresh
water. A man out west who had the same views as
"Petifi" wagered that the expert could not tell him
how many streams were cut by a bore and the
respective depths. The diviner supplied data quite
exact in most instances and within a very few feet
in others, and won the wager. If “Petifi" has any
money to back his opinion with, I’ll see that he gets
a flutter.

The iUKlerer": Tough bushwomen?
" ell, on the Manning in the olden days lived a Miss
Kelly, who on one occasion was grimly taking a
couple of her hired servants to Port Macquarie to be
corrected. On the way a flooded stream had to
be crossed, and one of the men, after a big struggle,
sated the hard-faced spinster from drowning. She
-aw to it that he got the lash all the same. She
had her home at Brimbin, near Cundle, and one
night gave some drover- permission to camp their
cattle in her yard. The drovers slept by their fire,
and next morning sent the camp boy to the
house for a cup of milk for the tea. Some 20 yards
from the house he saw a fine calf lying dead, and
mentioned the matter to Miss Kelly. “Yes,” she
answered; “I thought in the dark last night it was
one of those men poking about, so I p it a bullet
through it.”

“Norbell”: “Daybreak’s” title of the
tethered horse which couldn't be located and so died
miserably (B. 9 8/’l7) reminds me of a picturesque
yarn the police-trooper at Harrietville (Vic.) told
me years ago. When Dungey, afterwards a well-
known Melbourne sleuth, was stationed in that re-
mote townlet away back o’ Bright, he set out in
pursuit of a notorious evildoer who had announced
his intention of shooting on sight any policeman
that came after him. Dungey tracked the quarry to
an almost* inaccessible part of the ranges and tied
his horse to a tree whilst he continued the trail on
foot. He duly overtook his man, who opened fire—-
a trifle late. Dungey dragged the body down the
mountain side till he struck a hatter’s mining camp,
where he borrowed a packhorse, and so lumbered the
remains to Harrietville. Then he went baek to look
for his prad; but the country in that part is so wild
that it took Dungey, an experienced bushman, three
days to find the unfortunate animal. When dis-
covered the poor brute had gnawed all the bark off
the tree to which it was tied and had scooped a
fair-sized trench round the roots, but it was alive.
When Governor Loch camped out on Featliertop for
a few days Ducgey acted as his mounted orderlv,
and did the job so well that his Ex. had him trans-
ferred to Melbourne to act in a similar capacity
there. Afterwards he was promoted to the C.I.D.

“H-E.lt/’: The crow sits on a limb near
my poultry-run and yells, “Starved! Starved!'’ Pre-
sently a fool of a fowl advertises that she has done
it again, and if she runs away to tell a friend, the
crow flaps from her open nest with the egg in his
bill. Once 1 tilled his favorite raiding nest with
iggs that the incubator had said were rotten; but
these he left strictly alone. Even then I did not
credit him with special knowledge until I mixed
good ana bad eggs. The old burglar sorted them
out successfully, so I conferred upon him the degree
of Doctor of Devilry.
* * + *.
Ornis : "I).K (B. 2G/7/T7) says that
Hinders reckoned the number of mutton-birds he
saw in 1791) (December 9, 1798, correct date) as
220,000.000. Flinders's statement reads: “I think
the number could not have been less than a hundred
millions.” From particulars given in the ex-
plorer s narrative, A. J. Campbell computes the
number >een at 132,000,000. But it is humanlv
impossible to even approximately estimate the num-
bers present in a great flock of birds passing at a
rapid rate and whose outer edge is invisible. I
speak as a birdologist who has seen a number of
great flights at various times. The largest one I
have witnessed was in -October, 1910, when I was
camping on a small island in Bass Strait. The
leathered stream flowed from west to east for four
solid hours. The birds were closely massed and
flying low. The far side of the seemingly endless
procesMon appeared merged into the waves in the
distance. It was, as far as the eye could reach from
east to west and out to the north, a swift-movie,
pulsating river of feathers. An attempt to even
roughlt guess at the numbers that went bv would be
ridiculous. It was a sight never to be forgotten.

- £t a> biff a net y ield from that lot as the average
rip-and-tear cocky gets from 500 acres. These big-
area farms, poorly worked, are one of the seven
curses of Australia.
* * * *
A.H.C.’: Re black cockatoos. ‘TI.Y.E."
(B. 12/7/’l7) seems to think that a flock of
30 is rather extraordinary, and this I find to be a
prevailing impression. In some parts of the Gulf
(Q.) the red-tailed variety are almost as numerous
a- the white, and here’s my experience of a mob.
Me were camped one morning in Xoventber on a
plain lying between the Saxby River and some miles
of sand-ridge forest. At about seven o'clock we
could hear the long-drawn-out cry of the black cocky
in the direction of the timber, and presently dis-
cerned a fairly large school. In their slow, lumber-
ing flight over broad, open ground, it is a simple
matter to count them, so two of us got on the
job. In this flock there were over 90. Other flocks
followed them, six in all, numbering from 80 to
120 in each. They all settled on the plain, and
'here must then have been betwen 600 and 700 bird-
feeding together.
* * * *
"Bill 2E" : How long does the emu sit on
her eggs before the chicks appear? I once came
across this big fowl on a nest and had to ask her
twice before she (or perhaps it was he) would rise.
After considering the dozen eggs a good deal I con-
cluded that they were very much addled, so left
them. Six weeks later I happened along again and
was surprised to And my old friend still on the job,
and the eggs appeared to be the same as on my
last visit. I've heard it said that if the eggs are
touched the bird comes back and kicks them to
pieces. This one didn’t.
BILL COMES HOME TO-NIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is no time to worry
With trifling things to-day.
Though the cowyard i- a slurry,
The late rain spoilt the hay,
The wheat was pinched and rusty,
The spuds have got the Idight—•
Still things are not too dusty,
For Bill comes home to-night!
The earth shines like a feather
Pluiked from a parrot’s breast;
The sky, this Autumn weather,
Is like a blue-wren’s crest;
A magpie choir is singing
An anthem of delight,
And through it I hear ringing:
■‘Bill's coming home to-night!”
Though it was hours too early
I've twice been down the track,
And Bill's old sheep-dog, Curly,
Walked with me there and back,
And now sits, brown eyes blinking,
As though he- read quite right
The one thought I am thinking—
“ Bill's coming home to-night!”
Mum's busy cooking tucker,
The table's ready set,
Just waiting for the sucker
That’s in the oven yet;
There’s Bill's old favorite briar
Filled ready just to light,
The arm-chair's by the fire,
For Bill comes home to-night.
My eyes grow wet while watching—-
They must be getting weak.
The trouble, too, seems catching,
A tear rests on Mum’s cheek ;
Dad sings and whistles loudly,
To show he is all right,
And tells old Blossom, proudly,
“Bill’s, coming home to-night!”
TASMANIA.
H. W. STEWART.

A very excellent and successful singer was
lately lifting up his voice in Australia. His?
fad is joinery, and he invariably puts up at
a suburban establishment where be can rig
a workshop. During his Melbourne season
he lived at St. Kilda, and as a mark of
appreciation for favors received at the hands
of the gardener he built the man a fine pine
wheelbarrow. The gardener was enraptured.
“Look ’ere, Mister ,” he said. “If you
can make wheelbarrows like that, why d’you
go round singin’?”
“Jwosp”: Australian Ronald Bryarn is
doing remarkably well in the U.S.A. I re-
member him supering in 11. B. Irving’s co.
with Ernie Pirie Bush, of Maoriland.
Bryam jogged to the States shortly after
Bush went with Niblo, and the young ’uns
got into a condensed version of “Her Hus-
band’s Wife” at the ’alls. Bryam then sup-
ported Elsie Ferguson in Boston, aud while
at that centre of culture accepted an offer
as leading man for the best local stock co.
At latest he was playing lead in “The Angel
in the House,” the delightful comedy in
which H. R- Irving scored two years ago.
“Glen II.”: “J.F.H.” (B. 9/8/T7) men-
tions the death of Johnny Wallace, whilst
playing in the orchestra at the Geelong
Theatre Royal, as “probably the only in-
stance of such a tragically sudden exit in
Australian theatrical history.” There is at
least one other —that of Walter Dalgleish,
for years one of Bilanderson’s leading stage
villains. Walter, while playing at Perth,
had a fatal seizure, just when he was about
to propose to the heroine in “The Soldier’s
Wedding,” and died well in the limelight.
“First Old Man”: “Stargazer” (B.
16/8/T7) expresses surprise that dumb-show
performers of w 7 ell-remembered pantomime
troupes have not come to the fore in the pic-
tures. The Martinettis retired from business
years ago—long before the big boom in
movies. Paul, the star mime, could, I imagine,
have made a huge success on the screen, but
pantomiming is a strenuous game and he
wisely left it to younger men. There has
been very little dumb-show on the vaudeville
stage of late years, and what has survived
has not been in the same class as the Martin-
etti, Lauri. and Lupino offerings^of my youth.
However, there is still the Ivarno troupe,
capital fun-makers, and many of the mem-
bers have become filmers. As “Stargazer
may know, Charlie Chaplin himself was
originally a Karno comedian, and first
appeared in America in the sketches of that
combination.

KID STAKES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Audiences ought to try to keep the fresh outlook
of a child upon the theatre.’’ —Kathlene Macdonell.
Once on a time the footlights were
The lights of fairyland;
All heroines were wondrous fair,
Grand passions very grand.
To sit and at the chorus gaze
Alone seemed worth the money;
And in those far-off, happy days,
E’en funny men were funny 1
But I’ll he shot
If I can spot
A Lauri in the present lot.
Unfortunately, too, since then
We’ve found out many things.
Stage villains once seemed living men.
And cabbages were kings.
We did not glare in helpless rage
At heroes mild and mincing;
And all that happened on the stage
Was strikingly convincing.
We thought (oh joy!)
The panto “boy”
Wore legs like those without alloy!
Stage fairies now are not so fair;
We’ve come to understand
The limelight as the light that ne’er
Occurred on sea or land.
And no more w'ill we know that dear,
Delightful childhood’s rapture,
Until we can the atmosphere
Of childhood’s day recapture
And laugh away,
Absurdly gay,
Or cry once more when at a play.
If we in sailor suits and socks
Could to the playhouse go,
With female friends in frilly frocks,
Designed their knees to show,
And if a kindly management
Of toys kept a profusion,
And gave each girl a doll, each gent.
A drum, the old illusion
We might attain,
And ease our pain
By “kidding” we were kids again!

Business, Bobbery, etc
“C. J.W.”: Here is a claimant for the
heavy-weight champion belt —a Berkshire
sow with a touch of the British black in her.
She was sold by William Drummond to
Messrs. Crisp, of Tenterfield (N.S:W.),and
scaled 4601 b. dressed weight. Dimensions:
76in. from nose to butt of tail; 63in. girth
under forelegs; height at shoulder, 32in.;
across shoulder, 20in.; width across loins,
15in. She was reared on boiled wheat, bran
and kitchen waste, without milk at any
time. With a litter of six she brought
£2O 10s.
“F.N.T.” : What becomes of the raspberry
seeds after the cordial-makers have expressed
the juice from the berry? The other day I
tasted some suspiciously “wooden” rasp-
berry jam. The flatness of the flavor called
to mind a factory that flourished before
pure-food laws were numerous. Nothing was
wasted in those good old days. The ripe
raspberry was unjuiced for cordial-making
and the remaining seedy pulp was passed'
along to the jam department. The finished
“raspberry” jam consisted of (1) pale pulp,
(2) coloring, (3) guava or apple juice, and
(4) sugar. The law still allows the “sophis-
tication” (and the improvement, by the
way) of strawberry jam with apple-juice,
so long as the whole strawberry is included
in the product and in a certain proportion.
It would be interesting to know if somebody
in the right place is ignoring the hyper-
sophistication of the now somewhat scarce
rasp.
“D.F.”: Pansy-seed is another of the
little things that Australia used to import
from Germany because it “couldn’t be both-
ered” to do the saving. As a result since the
Ilun product was blocked from the market
pansy seed has been so scarce that it has
risen from 10s. to 30s. an ounce, and even
then you cannot get any guarantee that it
will come true to color.
* * * *
“8.M.”: Don’t know if it is the rain or
the war that’s bringing them out, but at a
N. S. Wales sheep acreage there have lately
arrived via a labor agency the following
samples: No. 1, booked as handy man, was
an ex-tailor and resembled old “Pro Bono
Publico.” Asked to kill a turkey, he said
he was afraid to, as he heard they scratched
badly. No. 2 arrived as a boundary-rider.
Questioned, he owned Up that he was a ship’s
steward. He looked the last word in com-
mercial travellers. No. 3, intended for a
groom, said he was an ex-station manager,
but had all the evidences of being a “tank”
out of action. When asked to clean boots
he answered that he’d never cleaned them in
his life, not even his own. He was as proud
of it, too, as if he’d won the Battle of the
Marne single-handed.

“Hospes” : It’s a hard world for us girls.
One of us writes to Melbourne Herald legal
column stating that she is in receipt of an
invalid pension, and wanting to know
whether her friends can carry out their
threat to stop it if she accepts from “a very
old bachelor friend” a room rent-free in
exchange for her reading aloud to him. “And
can I recover damages from my friends,”
asks she, “if they make statements ipiputing
to me a want of chastity?” Solon answered
“No” and “Yes”; so there’s one at'least of
us who can be saved from our friends.
* * * v - *
“Rustler”: One of the drawbacks to
“gumming” in the Wheatstone system of
telegraphy in use between all the capital
cities is the stickiness of everything after
a few minutes’ work. Gumming at 180
words a minute does not leave any time for
the washing of the hands, and they soon
become dirty and uncomfortable. Many
experiments had been tried to overcome this
without reducing efficiency, but none were
successful until a young telegraphist re-
cently tried mixing equal parts of ordinary
soapy water with the gum. With this mix-
ture it is now possible to gum all day with-
out inconvenience, and the tapes stick quite,
as well as they used to.
* * * *
“J.J.S.”: Somebody has sent the Win-
the-War Government for a slide on butter.
In February Federal Ministers decided to
guard against a winter shortage, and the
trade, which in each State has an Advisory
Committee to control the Pool arrangements,
was called in to suggest a workable scheme.
The buttermen’s idea was that the Govern-
ment should buy up enougli to meet the
shortage at a fixed price, and sell it out as
required with storage and interest charges
added. This was agreed to, and £lOO,OOO
borrowed from the Commonwealth Bank to
provide the needful. When the details were
settled, butter supplies began to fall oil, and
all export was stopped to meet immediate
and storage demands. So acute was the
shortage that the buttermen were successful
in inducing Minister Ted Russell to advance
prices a penny a pound. And then the
figure began to work. Butter became plenti-
ful and now there is a surplus. But con-,
sumers are still paying a penny a pound more
than usual, and the Government dare not
lower the price, because if it did it would
be losing money on the heavy stocks it
holds. The traders in Flinders-lane and
Sussex-street who handle the grease are
smiling, and the S. M. Herald is gleefully
remarking that Ministers will be taught not
to meddle in a business of which they know
nothing. But the public pays—the public
pays.
“C.F.E.”: When the Vic. Railways shoved
up freights the Associated Fertilisers’ secre-
tary wrote a pathetic letter weeping for the
“poor farmer.” This brought forth a man
who sneered at the Fertilizers’ grief as an
artificial product. Then one of the captains
of the industry called in his stenographer
and dictated a long screed in which he
talked pathetically of “faithful service” ren-
dered to the farmer by himself and friends.
This is some of the faithful service ren-
dered 1914-1917. Superphosphate has been
bumped up in price per cent. Dried
blood has soared per cent. Ammonium
sulphate has ballooned 48 per cent. Grant-
ing for the. first-named that the war has in-
creased freights, why should the buyers
suffer a decrease per 20s. expended of from
103§lb. (1907) to 791 b. (1914) of water-
soluble phosphoric acid? This simply means
that the wheat farmer has to increase the
amount of fertiliser per acre. It also means
more freight charges and handling. To. the
potato farmer sulphate of ammonia is a
necessity owing to its quick action on a
quick crop. This is a product of the gas
cos., and was largely exported before the
war. It therefore, in the absence of ship-
ping, shouldn’t be dearer now. Dried blood
is useful for all vegetable and orchard
growths. “Faithful service” has pushed up
the price and decreased the valuable nitro-
gen contents.
South Australia has treated one of its
fcrests (at Glenelg River, on the Vic. border)
to a 50-foot watch-tower, which gives an
outlook for miles around. It is said to be
the first application to an Australian forest
of the fire-detection device common in cities.
* * * *
“Scientificus”: While quite agreeing with
“Glen Iris” (B. 9/8/T7) that agents and
dealers make the easiest profit out of poul-
try-farms, I disagree when he denies the
possibility of making a living by selling eggs
and fowl meat while buying feed at retail
prices. I can show him a run of less than
1000 hens from which the owner makes not
less than £5 per week profit in his spare
time. The whole of this comes from the sale
of eggs and old hens. Without doubt many
muddlers fail at the gentle art, and they are
mostly the unmethodical suburbanites whom
“Glen Iris” extols. In reality the fatuity of
the average Mr. Suburbs in poultry-rearing is
only concealed by the prodigality of his
wife in the kitchen. Where the family waste
is large the poultry-feed bill is small; but
they are dear eggs at the price.
* * * *
“Bogan”: Here and there a waybacker
still prefers to bank his money with Mother
Earth, never dreaming that the old lady
occasionally lets her depositors down. A
worried-looking man came into a N. S.
Wales country bank one day recently. He
handed a bottle, half filled with a grey dust,
across the counter, and asked if the Bank
would give him what he reckoned the con-
tents were worth. He explained that
a year or two previously he had buried
a roil of notes in the bottle and on digging
it up again discovered that the paper had
partially mouldered away. The manager
was whistled up and decided that he
wouldn’t chance it; but I believe the chap
was put in the way of getting back a bit of
his own in Sydney later on.
“Centurion”: There never was such a
country as Egypt for eggs, or such a people
as its inhabitants for turning the egg into
hen with a view to turning her back again
into egg. Every village in cultivated Egypt
has its incubator, a weird building with fur-
nace attachments and many small rooms
opening into each other. Sometimes there
are a couple of these chicken-factories, and
then the rival proprietors occasionally fight
among the eggs with disastrous results to
the profits. The floor of the rooms is
covered with a mixture of loam and chaff.
On this the eggs are laid, and the proprietor
goes ceaselessly through the chambers, turn-
ing the eggs over. This is done day and
night, the family supplying the reliefs.' The
necessary heat comes from the furnace, whicli
is fed with manure cakes, the expert, who
has inherited the job from his ancestors,
keeping the right temperature in some
mysterious fashion. From every dozen eggs
given him he is bound to return the investor
six chickens. All over he keeps as his pay-
ment. Incubators of 100,000-egg capacity
are common, and at El Marg, outside Cairo,
is one that is said to deal with half a million
at a time.
THE MURRAY MARINERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ah, you who make the streets’ unrest, how little do
you know
Of this the land in which you live, and laugh, and
older grow!
How many of you know the routes the hawking
steamers take —
The fussy, paddle-wheeled affairs, with barges in
their wake ?
But we the children of the flats through which
the Murray flows,
We know and love the steamer-store that peddles
scran and clo’es.
We know each little stopping-place, we know the
staring eyes,
We know the needs of river-folk. Is one of you
as wise?
And though at times we take the track, or train it
to the sea,
And though we watch the cargo-boats, low-laden by
the Quay,
We don’t forget the inland boats, but, in our minds,
again
We sail the routes that lead by bank, and spur,
and road and plain.
We leave the town of Morgan, and the first gay
port ahead
Is Lake Victoria Station, and then Ned’s Corner
Shed.
Then onward to Ned’s Station, and thence to old
Kulnine,
And further on to Moorna, and the Moorna stretch
a-shine.
From Moorna on to Anabranch, and thence to Went-
worth—-than
Which river-port there’s none more dear to any
sailor-man!
And next to Kelso, then—if but the “water” lets
us go—
To sell our remnants to the folk of quiet Tapio.
What know you in the city’s roar of this, and what
we see
Of life upon the river’s banks, its joy and tragedy?
Ah, little do your wisest know, and little do they
care,
Who never knew the hawker-boats, nor stared the
river-stare 1
N'.S.W.
R. J. C.
