ineffectual. Hough powders, such as cuttlefish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

STRENGTH, AND HOW TO GAIN IT.
INSTEAD OF EXERCISES, PATENT
FOODS AND MEDICINES, TAKE
PHOSPHATE WITH YOUR MEALS.
The demand to-day is for men and women
who are strong in every sense of the word —
possessing the physical strength necessary to
endure hardships and fatigue; the mental
strength to grapple with difficult problems ;
the nervous force which endows the body
with vigor and vitality; the will power to
triumph over adversity and turn defeat into
victory. But such glorious strength is im-
possible so long as your nerves are weak
and exhausted, and therefore if you would
be really strong you must first care for your
nerves. Weak, exhausted nerves need food,
and it has been proved in numberless cases
that the only food element they can or will
absorb readily and naturally is phosphate
in the well-known form of bitro-pliospliate,
which most chemists stock in sgr. com-
pressed tablets, so that if you feel your
strength is failing from any cause, you
should get a supply of these bitro-pliosphate
tablets and take one with every meal. Prac-
tically all of the minor ailments afflicting
mankind, as well as many of the more seri-
ous maladies, can be traced to nervous ex-
haustion and lowered vitality, and probably
this explains why such a remarkable im-
provement in the general health is invari-
ably noticeable when bitro-phosphate is
taken as directed, and the nerves are thereby
revitalised and made strong.— Advt.

POLITICAL
POINTS
If we had only been as shy of the Yankee
I.W.W. shirk system as we are of the Yankee
Taylor work system there wouldn’t have
been any trouble.
The Labor wirepullers may not hold the
undated resignation of every well-paid Par-
liamentarian ; but, until the secret union
ballot is restored, they have the undated
resignation of every humble unionist in their
keeping.
Seeing that every Australian politician
who can work the oracle debits the country
with a few thousand pounds for going to
England on alleged public business, Andrew
position is altered to . Why Com-
missioner.
ANOTHER STRAW—THANK GOD!
HE : “Couldn’t think of enlisting now, you
know. They’re coaling the bally trans-
ports with non-union labor.”
The N. S. Wales State Government has a
chance to get back to sanity, and appar-
ently is not going to take it. The Common-
wealth Government has just borrowed- -44
millions from hard-pressed J. Bull to lend
to the States; and Holman is understood to
have done something towards raising another
big State loan in London. Most of Australia’s
present turmoil is due to wild borrowing and
wilder spending, which produced wholly arti-
ficial conditions. Meantime, politicians have
been buying support with loan money ; and
the constituencies have been thus demoral-
ised. And instead of seizing the present
opportunity to get down at once to the hard
facts of life, the Government is coquetting
with the old curse. We shall have to pay
the price in the future as surely as the
country is to-day paying the price of the
same sort of folly committed in the past.
Thus a member of the N. S. Wales
Assembly the other night:—
Is it a fact that the Government, taking advan-
tage of the miners being on strike, intends to work
the coal-mines with black labor?
It the intention was to ask whether “black”
labor was to be employed the question was
legitimate enough. If the object was to
find out whether there was a scheme hatch-
ing to put brown brother on the job the
HIS COUNTRY.
THE DEVIL: “You’re wanted! Your
country needs you.”
question was merely a waste of time. The
Government could have no such intention,
because it could not get such labor, or if it
could the Commonwealth law would have to
be turned upside down before it could be
employed. But as the question has been
raised, it is a proper time to ask whether
Labor still believes in a White Australia.
In theory presumably it does. The plank
is in its platform, and whenever the ideal
is mentioned it is mentioned as a matter of
course. But it is a tragic error to take it
as a matter of course. Neither the Labor
party nor any other party will achieve a
White Australia by supporting it merely
with its mouth. Speeches won’t keep this
country white. Development is the one thing
that will do it—and development is merely
another word for work intelligently directed.
That being so, what is Australian Labor
doing to-day to keep Australia white?
“Rimu” knows how to do it: —
The extent to which strikers and
their wives are using trains and trams
which they pleasantly describe as
“scab” and eating meat which is pro-
claimed “black” suggests a Gilbertian
method of dealing with the insane busi-
ness. Let any person who goes out on
strike to hold up any public conveni-
ence be prohibited from making use of
it during the period of the strike and
for 12 months afterwards. There would
be no victimisation about this. The per-
son who says “Let the public walk and
be damned to them!” could have no
grievance when he was compelled to
walk himself. Neither could the man
who cut off the public light have any
kick if an ill-used community com-
pelled him to go to bed in the dark.
Similarly, people who block food sup-
plies ought in reason to do without
food themselves. The spectacle of a
lot of striking slaughtermen turned
vegetarian would be a wholesome one ;
but the finest object-lesson of all would
be provided by those immoral persons
who blocked the transport of beer.
Wearing the year-long badge of thirst,
they would hang sorrowfully about the
corners of joy, no man daring to
moisten the tip of their tongue. It
would be a holy world and thoughtful
by the time the new scheme had
worried through a strike or two.
Because Hughes was too busy winning the
war by handing over the metal business of
Australia to a few rich people and Pearce
was too busy winning it by blinding the
people to the size of the job they had in
hand, the Commonwealth Labor Government
allowed two years to go by without passing
any law taking for the' nation the war
profits that a few individuals were making.
When the Labor party burst, nothing was
still done by the Makeshift Ministry. Now,
when Bigjohn Forrest has pushed the matter
forward, over three years after the war
started, there is naturally such a howl against
collecting tax on made so long ago
that the Treasurer has carved out the first
H months- of -the war-time and proposes to
collect the tax only from July 1, 1915. As
a matter of fact, some of the wickedest war
profits made at all were made during that
period; for prices of foreign goods bought
at low pre-war prices were then rushed up
outrageously. But in scores of cases those
profits have been divided long ago, and the
shares, interests or other property have been
sold; so that, through that muddling which
marks almost everything that Hughes has
anything to do with, the people who got
away with the profits won’t have to pay a
tax.
The much-talked-of Coalition between
“Liberals” and “Nationals” in South Aus.
is still not fixed up. And as. the weeks go
by the less is the chance of its being fixed
up at all. The offer of Peake to throw over-
board three of his own crew and take in
three of Vaughan’s, seems to hold good still;
but the other side wants it to imply an
agreement not to contest each others’ seats
at the general • election which may come
almost any moment, and must come next
March—-unless the life of this Parliament
can be prolonged, which is not likely. Also
the present Government has appointed Noel
Webb, S.M., as a Commission to investigate
the land-purchases of Vaughan’s Govern-
ment ; and there is an obvious difficulty in
Government and ex-Government sitting on
the same Ministerial benches while one lot
is practically on its defence. A newer
feature is that many of the National men
who were lately and claim still to be Labor
men, are restless at the idea of any alliance
with Liberals. Old John Verran is a typical
example. Many Liberals are about equally
restless at the idea of any alliance with
Labor. In all this murk one thing is clear.
If the Liberals and Nationals combine there
will be either a new Radical party or a new
Conservative party—perhaps both —or a
wholesale reversion of ex-Labor Nationals
to the Labor party.
The N. S. Wales Arbitration Court last
week cancelled the “industrial” registration
of various striking unions. But this doesn't
mean that the unions are no longer regis-
tered as trade unions. Under the N. S.
Wales law a trade union may be a trade
union and still not be registered
to enjoy the benefits of the Arbi-
tration law. What happens when
the second registration is cancelled
is that the union cannot come into
Court to enforce an award or apply
for a new one. So in cancelling
the registration the Court says in
effect: “Well, strike if you like,
and win that way if you can ; but
you shall not play with a double-
headed penny.”
“5.8. T.” : Two brothers forfeited
two selections comprising 2560
acres on the Upper Clarence
(N.S.W.), being unable to make a
living out of them. These have
been surveyed into 400-acre blocks,
on which inexperienced Billjims are
to take their chance! It would be
bad enough in any case to ask Bill
to struggle with 400 acres of coun-
try from 1280 acres of which the
original experienced selectors could
not make a living ; but it is more
disgraceful in this instance be-
cause, not far away, there are
7000 acres of extremely rich allu-
vial flats still occupied as a cattle-
run. All over the country, I
believe, it is the same: areas for
which no profitable use can be
otherwise found are being pushed
on to Bill wherever possible. Find
out what almost any country bank-
manager is doing with the hope-
less blocks that have been on his hands for
years.
Bar well, the young lawyer who has become
Attorney-General of South Aus. in his first
session in Parliament, is making a bid to
get that crowd of enemies which is supposed
to be the mark of a strong public man. The
stand he made in the Assembly over the Six-
o’clock Closing Bill set the teetotal crowd
against him; now he has challenged the
hotelkeepers who evade the Act. He says
that 1079 men were counted fentering a single
hotel in a Week during prohibited hours, yet
the signalling code was so good that the
police found nothing wrong whenever they
entered :
The law is being flagrantly flouted. I, as admin-
istrator, am being flouted, and I should be unworthy
of the office I hold if I allowed things to remain at
that. If I do not take action it will be said that
what I set out to do cannot be done. It can be
done. It is now the law-defying publicans and myself
for it. The public need have no fear or doubt about
the result. Illegal trading by publicans will be
stopped, and I undertake to stop it.
And the Adelaide wowsers are rubbing their
eyes and asking, “Is Saul also among the
prophets?”
One aspect of the Melbourne lumpers’
strike is strictly humorous. Originally
the lumpers refused to assist in exporting
foodstuffs until prices locally were reduced.
Apparently this sort of half-strike was no
good, so they insisted on having one centra:
“picking-up point”—a place where they can
be engaged—instead of the previous * half-
dozen points along the wharves. This bein'-'
refused a whole-hog strike ensued. The
point of this “picking-up place” joke is that
the men are paid Is. 9d. per hour for the
time occupied in going from the place of
engagement to their job. in the case of the
Railway Department’s work at Port Mel-
bourne this payment sometimes reaches 5s
per day per man, though many of the lump-
ers live at the Port. The shipping com-
panies pass all these charges on to the con-
sumer —they have to, there is no option-
vet the lumpers originally struck over the
'high cost of living! Now rhey are asking
2s. 6d. per hour, which will, of course, re-
duce the cost of living still further!
The N.S.W. Department of Agriculture
has a serious charge to answer in regard to
the non-production of linseed. Australian
linseed-oil mills are importing now about
£25,000 worth of seed per month—£3oo.ooo
a year. Practically all of it is used in N.
S. Wales. And there is every prospect of a
far greater quantity being required in the
future. Yet so far the Department has done
not a thing to have the seed grown here and
the money and employment kept in the
country. On the other hand, it seems to
have steadily discouraged anyone who talked
about the possibilities of adding this new
rural industry to the very few- we have
already, and to have done it in defiance of
evidence that the seed grows in. compara-
tively poor land in countries very similar to
our own ; in defiance also of encouraging ex-
periments made at Wagga and Orange. It
has gone even further. Lately Meggitts, the
principal users in Australia, put in a clean-
ing machine to grade seed w-hich they pro-
posed to give away to people who would grow
experimental plots; whereupon the Depart-
ment demanded a guarantee that no seed
should be thus disposed of. Failure to com-
ply, it w-as intimated, would mean refusal
to allow any seed at all to be landed. It
is bad enough that this Department fails so
dismally to encourage agriculture; it be-
comes intolerable when, through blank igno-
rance, it discourages the very thing it is
PUTTING HIM TO THE TEST.
BENEVOLENT OLD GENT: “Man, my heart is
full of gratitude to the likes of you!
How can I ever repay you for what
you’ve done for me?”
BILLJIM : “Well, buy us a pint, and I'll give
you a written receipt in full settle-
ment.”
supposed to foster. In this matter of linseed
the Department's failure to develop the in-
dustry means not only that the oil mills have
to import; it means also that flax mills
don’t exist. And because they don’t exist we
import most of our binder and other twine,
our tow, our -crash towelling, our baggmg
and a dozen other things which we can grow
and make for ourselves as well as anyone
can grow and make for us.
THE NEWEST GRUNDY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the Vic. Assembly Mr. M. Hannah brought for-
ward a motion favoring the abolition of State
Governors. Mr. Mackey said that if it were known
in Germany that the Vic. Parliament had adopted
such a resolution its significance might be miscon-
strued. /
Be not deceived by sophistry,
This great fact none can blink:
If we pay off the Governor
’Twill snap the final link
That joins us to the Motherland —
And what would Wilhelm think ?
The news in distant Potsdam would
Occasion wildest glee ;
I have no doubt that it would send
All Berlin on the Spree;
And later bn an Iron Cross
For Hannah there would be.
Economy and commonsense
Are outraged while we pay
Six Governors, it is alleged
By some. How little they
Appreciate the vital point:
What would the Kaiser say ?
Here is our chance to strike a blow
’Gainst Wilhelm and his mob,
For of his last despairing hope
The Kaiser we will rob
If we will but agree to keep
Young Stanley in his job.
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.

COMSTOCK’S “DEAD-SHOT” WORM
PELLETS.
A SAFE, SURE, AND RELIABLE SPECIFIC FOR
THE ERADICATION OF WORMS IN CHILDREN
AND ADULTS.
The general symptoms of worms are a
tendency to pick the nose and a sensation of
itching in the anus. Other symptoms are
nervous affections, such as fretfulness, irri-
tation of temper, wakefulness or disposition
to excessive sleep, disturbed sleep, and grind-
ing the teeth, sudden starting out of sleep as
if from fright, dizziness, spasmodic move-
ments of the eyelids, dilated pupils, per-
verted vision, temporary blindness and deaf-
ness. General convulsions are not infre-
quent in children, and symptoms resembling
water in the head have been ascribed to
worms, and have ceased on their expulsion.
The long round or stomach worm is the
commonest kind of worm from which man-
kind suffers, and is of a light, transparent
color, resembling the ordinary ground worm,
except that the extremities are more sharply
pointed than the ground worm.
Young people are more subject to these
worms than middle-aged or elderly people.
They live in the bowels, and sometimes make
their way into the stomach and throat, caus-
ing sickness, vomiting and choking. Gener-
ally one or two would be the most a person
would be afflicted with, but sometimes a
much larger number is met with. The length
varies from four to eight inches, but young
ones an inch to two inches long are also
found. The first thing to do is to rid the
system of the worms, and Comstock’s “Dead
Shot” Worm Pellets, taken according to
directions, are an effective remedy for the
thorough eradication of the long round or
stomach worm —the common enemy of the
child, the youth, the middle-aged and aged.
A light, nourishing diet, out-of-door exercise,
and, if possible, a change of air, will greatly
improve the general health.
Threadworms or pinworms are of very
common occurrence in children. They are
very small, from one-sixth of an inch to half-
an-inch in length, and resemble cotton
thread —they are sometimes called seat
worms, and reside in the lower bowels. The
chief symptom of this worm is severe local
itching, restlessness and irritability, and a
frequent desire to urinate, picking at the
nose; loss of appetite and anaemia are also
observed. To get rid of the worms and their
eggs their nests also must be removed, and
that requires a specific remedy. Comstock’s
“Dead Shot” Worm Pellets, taken according
to directions, on an empty stomach, followed
by a dose of opening medicine, will succeed
where others fail.
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets
are packed in glass tubes, with full directions
in each, and are for sale by chemists and
stores generally at 3s. 6d. per packet, or six
packets for 205., and if unable to obtain
locally will be mailed post free on receipt of
price. Prepared only by The W. H. Comstock
Co., Ltd., 23 Lang Street, Sydney, N.S.W.;
Farish Street, Wellington, N.Z.; and 21
Farringdon Avenue, London, E.C.
NEGLECT.
The healthy man or woman is usually the most careless,
and fails to observe the little indications of coming
trouble. However healthy persons may be, they need
to watch themselves, because a little indigestible food or a little too much food or drink upsets the
whole digestive machine, and unless Nature receives some assistance trouble is certain to follow;
first, perhaps, by a common bilious attack, and then other serious digestive troubles. The sensible
man or woman, however, keeps a sharp eye on the little signs of ill-health and takes a remedy like
Dr Morse’s Indian Root Pills to aid digestion and help Nature over some obstruction caused by
too much food or drink. There is no better remedy for this purpose in this or any other country,
and their ever-increasing use by all classes of the community bears testimony to their high efficiency.
The best advice that can be given is to always have a bottle of these pills at hand, and when the
first indication of trouble appears in the form of Constipation, take a dose. The result is certain
to be effective, and in a simple manner the system is put right again. No remedy at any price is
better or more carefully prepared than Dr. Morse’S Indian Root Pills. They are the
Standard Remedy of thousands; used in the city, town and country with equally good results, and
sold at a price within the reach of all.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The effect of the great uprising of the pro-
letariat about nothing in particular has been calami-
tous; to Sydney suburban racecourses. Also, it has
been a real good thing for the promoters of pony
flutters. The pony flutters nearer to the city than
the full-grown nag. The proceedings can be reached
by tram or boot. To get to the horse events trains
or motor-cars or the rabbit-oh limousine are neces-
sary ; and Rosehill and Warwick Farm are, for all
practical purposes, served only by train. Already
a meeting on each of these courses has been aban-
doned, and the question of what is going to happen
to the next meetings—one at least of which is usually
of some importance—is veiled in gloom. Meanwhile
the pony people are bearing up against increased
patronage with a stout heart.
* * * *
The Maoriland contingent, which pro-
vided Sydney punters with a longi-priced winner a
few weeks ago at Rosehill, repeated the dose on
Saturday at Canterbury Park. Ample, winner of the
Flying, is the property of G. L. Stead, who sent over
The Toff lately. In April last, at Wellington, she
won over six furlongs with Bst. 91b., and since arriv-
ing at Randwick had done at least one very smart
gallop. On Saturday she carried 7st. 81b., and as
the public backed Merriwee down to 2’s and
Heatherbrae to 3’s, bargain prices were going about
the stranger. But that didn’t stop her hopping
away in front soon after the start and leading the
procession home. A nice Ally.
* * * *
There were seven races at Canterbury
Park (Sydney) on Saturday,, and one favorite
arrived. But strikes are no good to Israel, and Ike’s
harvest was materially less than it would have been
with such running under ordinary conditions. Gen-
erally speaking, the prices on offer by the books were
better than the machine yielded. Thus a pound on
each winner would have either extracted £94 from
Ike or £7i 12s. from the tote. But a backer of
seven seconds would with a pound on each have
taken £B7 15s. from the pay-out window. On
seconds there were dividends (on 10s.) of £l3 10s.,
£l5 4s. Od. and £8 16s. 6d. The tote turnover at
Canterbury was £2063; at the pony meeting held
during the afternoon, £2372.
* * * *
English-bred, Maoriland and Australian
horses figure in the nominations for the Chelmsford
Stakes w.f.a., to be run next month, during Tatter-
sail’s Club meeting at Randwick. Stead’s queer-
looking Sasanoff won this race last year and made a
name for himself later on at Flemington. He is in
again this year, and with him are Kilboy, the other
Stead’s Toff, and Kilflinn, who had a win from
Desert Rose in M.L. It looks as if the local moke
may have to hurry up a lot.
* * * *
The alleged mistake by which any Mel-
bourne Cup entrant that wins a w.f.a. race will
incur a heavy penalty shoidd give pause. to punters
who are disposed to back top-weights’ or crack
three-year-olds in “doubles” or straight-out. No-
body knows, not even the owners, whether the
temptation of a rich, easy-won stake may not be
too strong in October for even a Cup favorite to
resist. The dead-bird in hand for a w.f.a. event is
worth two future handicap chances.
* * * *
Horse-owners south of the Murray have
heen wondering whether the strike is going to let
them get the nags through to the Spring meeting at
Randwick; also whether if they arrive they will get
them back again in time to be of any use at Caul-
field. Wherefore there have been some alterations in
the odds market and much speculation as to whether
the immediate, mission of some horses may not have
been altered. For instance, Court Jester, who has
ruled as a warm favorite for the Metropolitan, was
suddenly backed early in the week for Caulfield,
where he is much less favorably handicapped than in
the Sydney event. But it may all be a jest.
* * * *
The 14.1 handicap run at Rosebery
(Sydney) on Saturday was one of the best pony races
seen for a long time. At the word “Go” Lady Lid-
dell and Careymoore, a pair of greys, cleayd out
from the field, and at the straight . entrance were
level, with everything else hopelessly beaten. Up
the running they came, with F.vans and Porter riding
all they knew. Just on the post Careymoore appeared
to have the race won, but the lady put up a supreme
effort and made a dead-heat of it. As the owners
could not agree to divide a run-off was necessary.
After a spell of about 20min. the pair took the track
again. Careymoore was favorite this kick, mainly
because he had 481 b. less weight. Up to the Leger
it was the first race over again. Then the weight
told its tale, and the favorite landed the bacon. It
was in keeping with these happenings that the sires
of the dead-heaters should be Merv (imp.) and
Moorefield —-the two most successful dads of ponies
in the State.
*■ * # *
“Nemo” : Though it seems very little use
protesting against the manner in which the totalisa-
tor is misused on unregistered courses, it may be
worth remarking that on Wednesday at Ascot
(Sydney) the machine was still open and doing
business on the third division of the Flying Handi-
cap while the race was being run, and the windows
were not closed until Clanfera, the winner, had
passed the Leger-stand, a furlong from home, with
a commanding lead. He was one. of the leading
division right through. Is it any wonder the public
squealed ? Accidents like this certainly do the
machine no good.
Charlie Barden, the jockey who was picked
up out of the smash at Canterbury Park (Sydney)
a month ago with a fractured skull, is out of hospi-
tal. But the youngster wants a long rest. The
“Baron” Brown horses running at Randwick Spring
meeting will be ridden by Bracken.
* * * *
The Grand National Hurdles in S. Aus.
was won unexpectedly by Westendale, which rural
name is really an allusion to the stuff made by the
S.A. Brewing Co., of which his owner, A. E. Nott,
is manager. The same owner won the same event,
also unexpectedly, last year, with Hobgoblin. The
dividends this time were £l9 Is. on the winner,
13 19s. on the second. Westendale was bought
in Sydney, but is Maoriland-bred (by Birkenhead).
He is the horse that lately paid the record dividend
of over £lBO for a second place.
* * * *
The committee of Tattersall’s Club in
Adelaide has been formally attacked by a section
of the members on the most thoroughly unexpected
charge that ever affected a club committee—-the
charge that it had not raised the price of drinks.
The complaining section, headed by S. J. Jacobs, the
chairman who has just resigned, thought that the
club should not undersell the hotel bars, which have
had to raise their charges for spirits; but a large
and excited meeting finally agreed by 70 votes
to 56 to leave things as they are.
But then everybody quarrels about everything
in Adelaide just now. H. P. McLachlan, the new
chairman of Tattersall’s, has devised a new starting-
machine intended to give each horse a kind of sep-
arate stall and an equal chance. Sporting writers
have mostly hailed it as a splendid idea; but 56
licensed jockeys have weighed in with a formal pro-
test against its introduction, and the S.A.J.C. has
asked starter and stipendiary stewards to report.
* * * *
Murrangong, winner of the Grand
National at Adelaide on Saturday, is the first Mal-
ster to win over big timber.
“Punter”: It was not the Australian
hurdle-race winner Polygamist that went so close
to having his wings singed on the steamer Benalla,
which caught alight in mid-ocean, but a fellow-
traveller named Nareisse and a female companion,
Lady Fariman. The Caulfield horse did not reach
Australia until several months later, and, by the
way, he was not landed without excitement. It
was a Saturday afternoon, and just as the hand-
some Britisher was somewhere in mid-air, with
trainer McCann hanging on by almost his finger-
tips to the horse box, the lumpers cried “smoke-oh,”
and, of course, everybody stopped. A little manipu-
lation, however, soon landed the racer in Australia.
Nareisse and Lady Fariman also belonged to Wootten,
and journalist Moody, of Adelaide, had an interest
in them. Lady Fariman flew over three hurdles at
Caulfield in great style one day, and the jockey,
thirsting for more, attempted to go another round.
She fell. The anxious but unfortunate boy broke
his collar-bone, the mare her neck. Nareisse,
reckoned by Wootten to be the best two-mile hurdler
in England, got so fed-up with human kindness dur-
ing the voyage that trainer McCann could never
reduce him to a racing state, and had difficulty to
keep him on four sound legs.
* * * *
One Maoriland outfit fell heavily into the
soup on Saturday. It consisted of the hurdler
Luperino, the nag’s owner Charters and the jockey
Foreman. The scene of the mishap was Williams-
town (Vic.). Luperino had won at Moonee Valley a
fortnight before, and a week later ran fourth on the
same course. On Saturday he was produced at
Williamstown, and was well in the betting at s’s and
6's. He ran fifth, the winner being Tenacious, also
from M.L., ridden by E. J. Rae, who figured in the
book as trainer of both horses. At Moonee Valley
Tenacious finished third the day Luperino won. On
Saturday she started a firm favorite at 5 to 2. The
stipes came to the conclusion that Luperino, Charters
and Foreman could do with a couple of years’ holi-
day. Rae, it transpired, was not the horse’s regular
trainer. When he came over with Waimai and
Tenacious the other horse was brought along and
joined the party, but was trained by Charters.
Wherefore Rae was not granted a holiday. Beyond
explaining that the sentence was for “dishonorable
practice” nothing more was revealed. Another of
those incidents in which the alleged offence is
shrouded in gloom, for no apparent purpose. The
proceedings in all such cases should be laid bare.
First Principle, who handled welter
weights better than any other horse racing round
Sydney, seemed to have lost his form when he went
off to Bananaland a few months ago. But it was
only a rumor. He had list. 131 b. hoisted aboard
for a seven-furlongs trip at Brisbane on Saturday,
and the local punter was so certain he was a
back-number that they laid 5 to 4 “on.” They
cashed in, too, the l|cwt. arriving at the right end
of the field.
The Maoriland ’chaser El Gallo was
found dead in his box the other day at Wellington.
He has gone where all the honest nags go. He twice
annexed a big Auckland double —the G. N. Steeple
and Hurdles—and that is the kind of thing only
done by champions, and then not very often.
“B.B.”: Talk of old-time cheap-jacks
recalls a thing that used to puzzle me. One of the
princes in the business in Maoriland sang a song
whose chorus was:—
“Could I only back a winner
What a different man I’d be!
I d spare enough to share enough
To show my charity.
I’d be like Plunger Thompson,
And give a champagne dinner
To all my friends who’re here to-night—
Could I only back a winner.”
I gathered that “Plunger Thompson” was a notorious
Australian sport, but I’ve never been able to track
his pedigree or performances. Who was Plunger
Thompson? Joe, the Leviathan, who made the pot
over Don Juan in ’73? Or was there another to
whom the description “Plunger” could more truth-
fully be applied?
* * * *
“Con Drew”: Cohen hadn’t hooked a bet.
Gradually, at the prompting of Isaacs, who was
partnering him in the bag, he rose from 2 to 1 der
fieldt to 5 to 2, and, finally, in a burst of agony,
soared to 3 to 1, then to 7 to 2. The result was
astonishing. Several sharpshooters who had been
hanging round with a bored look suddenly sprang
towards him. But Isaacs got in first. “I 11 have
you.” he yelled, thrusting himself between the punt-
ers and his surprised partner. “Seffen pounds ter
two Spotlight, and I’ll put der monish in. He
produced two notes and handed them across. Cohen
took the money, and placing it in the bag waved the
punters away. “That’s all I vant,” he announced.
A wild gang dashed up led by a repellent-looking
person in a faded bowler. “Spotlight?” Queried its
leader breathlessly. Cohen shook his head, and the
gang dashed off. Presently some more punters torna-
doed past. “What price Spotlight f” they questioned.
“Aind't there no other horth in ther bletthed
raith?” wailed Cohen. “Vat’s th’ matter with em
all?” He hailed an adjoining bookmaker Vat
brice are you bettin’ Spotlight?” he queried. The
fielder shook his head. “No money,” he answered.
“Have a look at the seratchin’ board. Cohen did
as directed, and was enlightened. Out of an original
field of eight only three remained. Turning he faced
his partner. “That Spotlight must be er pretty
goodt horse, Isaacs, aind’t it,” he observed. Veil,
answered Isaacs, “he can bead them other two
horses that’s left in. I know that.” Hm! said
Cohen. “How much haf ve laid agen him? Only
that seffen poundt ter two you laid me, chuckled
Isaacs. “Is that all?” remarked Cohen. “Only seffen
poundts ter two ? Veil, veil! I neffer let a paltry
bedt like that stop me, Isaacs. Book me seffen
ter two der same horse and then ve knock off.

“F.W.C,”: There’s an old mare at Glen-
reagh (N.S^W.) —or was until a few days ago, when
she was shot—with a record. Fifteen years ago R.
Play ford bought her, six years old, for £l. She
had 14 foals, nine of whom are doing duty on the
farm, and others have been sold for £162. The
last of the family is a fine young colt six months
old. The old lady was barely 14 hands high, but her
progeny took prizes in the show-ring, in big classes,
“best draughts,” as yearlings and two-year-olds.
* * * *
“Karee”: Mention of that Calcutta sweep
prize of £66,666 (B. 16/8/’l7) sets me wondering
whether they still hold in Buenos Avres the Christ-
mas Day Race Sweep. A ticket cost 20 pesos, and
the drawer of the first horse was loaded with
£BO,OOO. Prior to the war there was a sweep every
year in France, at 20 francs a ticket, first drawer
£40,000 cash. The last winner was a woman who kept
a barracks canteen. As she was a thrifty woman this
sudden rush of affluence didn’t go to her head. She
insisted on all her soldier debtors squaring what
they owed her before she shouted. The lady also
added a pile to her sudden windfall by publishing
the love-letters sent her from all over the world by
absolute strangers.

There was a notable scatter of trotting
horses in Yankeeland recently, amongst those that
came under the hammer being The Harvester, one of
the most famous light-harness animals on earth. He
is credited with having put a mile behind in 2min.
lsec. He changed hands for £6OOO.
• • • •
Billjim hears some queer stories from
home. The Bulletin last mail received about 175
letters of amazed protestation from men in Egypt and
Palestine. They had just been leading a cable
message from Australia in an Egyptian paper to the
effect that returned soldiers had started an agitation
for the abolition of the V.R.C. and A.J.C., and the
writers want to know whether the end of the world
has arrived or whether they ought to settle down for
good in the Desert and start training camels to leap
through the eye of a needle.

habitant, was only 101 (he was open to lay long-
odds just before he died the other day that he was
102) he bumped against the church door or some-
thing one night, and appeared with two black eyes
next morning. An inquisitive crony turned up, as
usual. “Ahl” chortled Jimmy, “next time I have a
scrap, I'll take on someone older than myself.”

“Matey”: Major Horace Kingsmill,
D. 5.0., who has gone West in France, was a Sydhey
hockey enthusiast. In peace days he was sec. of the
Howitzer Battery Hockey Club. That club (and
battery) took up all of his spare time. Ilingy was
a military enthusiast first, a hockey-ite second; he
would talk guns by the hour and sandwich in
between.

“Peg-Leg” : For two years Francis Birtles
has been talking about crossing Australia by aero-
plane (B. 23/8/’l7), and at latest he had bought a
machine and was trying it in Victoria. His original
intention was to make a flight from Port Augusta
to Broome, but the chances of a spill in the vacant
North-West caused him to abandon that idea.
“G.N.”: Among the champions past and
present my vote goes to Stan V r ickham as the best
place-kick (Union or League game) Australia has
produced. Dally Messenger was certainly a
wonderful hooter, and put up some extraordinary
performances, but for consistent kicking, from all
angles, on wet or dry grounds, and taking into con-
sideration the difficulties under which a kicker
labored from the absurd Union rule of another player
placing the ball and the opposing forwards charging
like money-lenders, the wriggly player of a decade
and more ago is entitled to go up top.
* » * *
C. Fraser (Balmain), who topped the
score-sheet with 67 points (seven tries and 23
goals), was the most noticeable all-rounder in N. S.
Wales Rugby League this season. Last year he also
headed the list with 95 points (nine tries and 34
goals). Wally Messenger (Eastern Suburbs),
who was close up to Fraser with 58 points, headed
the 1914-15 statistics with 73 points. Harold
Horder, the fastest sprinter in the League, annexed
17 tries —the best individual effort of the year.
* * * *
A. Oxford, the hefty South Sydney for-
ward, put a flash in the Kangaroo v. The Rest of the
Rugby League game on Saturday. Besides scoring a
try, Oxford left-footed four goals—ll out of The
Rest’s 20 points. In premiership football Oxford
only booted one goal for his club during the season,
mostly because of opportunities being given to less
reliable feet. The Kangaroos played as well as age
allowed; but the younger generation lasted
longer and won comfortably by 20 to 13. The fix-
ture attracted about 13,000 barrackers, and the
admission money was passed along for patriotic
purposes.

Eugene Yolaire obliged again at Brisbane
Stadium on Saturday, Matty Smith being the opnosi
tion party. There were only a few chapters, but each
was chockful of slapbang and general upheaval
Volaire boxed in to the top of his form, teaching the
erratic Matthew the value of a wallop placed without
frills on the right spot. As early as the fourth
clash it was evident that the Frenchman was plot
ting the downfall of Mr. Smith with eveey possi
bility of success. He pulled it off in the seventh
just after the Smith family had made a most earnest
effort to assert itself. That hook of Yolaire’s cer-
tainly seems to be impregnated with ether.
Eric Barbour, who smote a merry 101
(out of 242) against the Army and Navy in Eng-
land last week, first got into the limelight with 356
for Sydney Grammar against C. of E. Grammar in
1909. Later, in premiership cricket with Sydney
’Varsity, he scored .highest aggregate (778, average
97.25) in 1912-13, and was second to Warren Bards-
ley in 1914-15 averages with 55.50 for 10 innings.
Barbour’s hits in 1913-14 inter-State cricket yielded
an average of 61.00. It runs in the family. Eric’s
dad, G. P. Barbour, was a great all-rounder with
Sydney ’Varsity, and one of the pioneer N.S.W reps,
on the Australian Board of Control.
In the Inter-State test stake decided at
Corowa last week Brown Hawk, winner of two
Waterloo Cups, was defeated for the first time since
he has been trained by Tom White. The Victorian
team for the stake were: Brown Hawk, Anxious Pen-
nant, Jumbunna King and All Comedy. The N.S.W,
dogs were Corsair (imp.), Achieved, Salonica and
White Verse. The inclusion of White Verse in the
team had caused a lot of quarrelling amongst the
selectors, some of whom were of opinion that she
was not class enough. However, she was put into the
team when it was found that Beauty Spot was not
available, and in the first round she was the only
N.S.W. dog that got a flag. At that stage it looked
as if Victoria, with three dogs to one N.S.W. animal
left in, had the champion ribbon safe. In the next
round White Verse downed Anxious Pennant and
qualified to meet Brown Hawk in the final. The
pair were well slipped to a strong racing hare.
White Verse led the Victorian by three lengths, and
after a little work had been done drove in and killed.
Whereupon there was great excitement. White Verse
is the first N. S. Wales animal to win the stake.
“The Colonel”: Why do eels so strictly
fast on moonlight nights? Recently I fished Lake
Elingamite (Vic.), which abounds in the delicacy,
several times a week for a couple of months,
but never once got a nibble by moonlight. Once,
with three others, from sundown till sun-up, we
tried every part of the lake, and although using 18-
carat tackle and the choicest white grubs, we caught
nothing. On the outward voyage we “set” 20 baited
lines, which were also neglected. Subsequently, on
a very dark, cloudy night, a fresh north wind blow-
ing, with one mate, and using the same tackle and
bait, in less thaiv two hours I hauled in 751 b. of
lovely silver eels ffb to 5111). Incidentally, only eels
reside in this lake, and they must breed there
because Elingamite has no connection with the sea,
From “The Oldest Inhabitant”: —
“Dr. Lang’s Church” in Sydney has just had its
93rd anniversary. The foundation-stone was laid in
1824 by the then Governor, Sir Thomas Brisbane,
who was a strict Presbyterian, but later on quar-
relled with his tempestuous pastor. Lang’s great
hobby was the Australian College; for this he im-
ported teachers, lay and clerical, from Scotland. For
Lang everything had to be Scotch; Scotch masons
had even to be imported for the erection of the
College buildings in Jamieson-street. The land on
which these premises were erected was part of the
estate of John Jamieson, who opened up the street
in 1831 and sold the frontage. Before he had paid
a shilling for the land the doctor had built two
houses (now demolished), and after 10 barren years
Jamieson threatened to seize the two for the amount
due on the land. In addition there were heavy debts
on the College buildings and for purposes connected
therewith. In the circumstances he was obliged to
dispose of his own personal property, which included
his late father’s grant of 2000 acres at Bong Bong
and Sutton Forest (for which he received £l5OO a
fourth of its value) and the buildings now known
as Petty’s Hotel. The College was closed in 1853,
and in the end politics and public spirit left Lang
a very poor man. On his death in 1878 the Govern-
ment offered “to do something for the family,” but
the offer was declined —almost indignantly. A
brother of the “old doctor” is buried under the
pulpit in Scots’ Church; and the old Lang family
pew has been left as it was.

The Australian Wood Pipe Co. (Syd-
ney) was formed about the middle of 1909.
A deficit of £7462 to begin with represented
patents and other goodwill items, plus pre-
liminary exes.: —
Paid Net Pro-
for fit avail-
Good- able for De-
June, Profits. Dividends. will. Reserves, ficit.
1911.. £4,277 lOp.c. = £1983 £ll £2294 £4819

It still doesn’t »look very cheerful; but the
directors are as optimistic as ever: —
Good inquiries for pipes are being received, and
it is hoped that owing to the great scarcity and high
prices of metal pipes, wood pipes will be more exten-
sively used by the Government departments than
hitherto. If wood pipes were used in place of
metal, large quantities of iron and steel would be
liberated for war purposes.
And not for war purposes only, but for use
in many industries in which there can be no
substitute for metal, which industries are
now seriously menaced by the short supply
of iron—especially pig-iron—in Australia.
Six years this co. has been at work, and
there ought to be fairly conclusive evidence
now as to whether it is at all necessary to
use metal for water pipes. If it isn’t it is
a tremendous waste to put it there, especially
in a country of big distances, where cartage
is such an expensive item. Whether every
wood-pipe customer has been satisfied this
paper doesn’t know ; but the co. certainly has
a bunch of testimonials large and flattering
enough to compel the attention of anybody
with water-mains to lay. Meantime, while
the management is persuading people that
the wooden water-pipe isn’t a new’, unproved
thing, profits, as a rule, are poor; they have
averaged under 5 p.c. p.a. during the past
three years. And that is all the poorer
because there is no water in the capital: the
few thousands that were represented origin-
ally by preliminary exes., patent rights, etc.,
have been made good out of accumulated
profits. Capital is £70,000; ordinarily this
is about enough to finance the co., but
lately, owing to shipping difficulties, stocks
were piled up while the chance offered, so
there are now sundry creditors for £26,504.
Still finance is easy enough. A much more
difficult matter will probably be to get con-
tracts in the years of depression—especially
in the construction industries —that are
ahead of us.
* * * *
The Thermo-Electric Ore Reduction Co.,
with works at Luton (Eng.), having pur-
chased several mines in North Queensland,
is applying to the Federal Government for
permission to ship wolfram direct. It de-
clares that while the price of Australian
fholybdenite delivered in London is fixed at
£525 per ton, Germany has been paying
Norway £2OOO, and America is buying at
£lOOO. Considering this. North Queensland
producers want to get as much of the £525
as possible, and cut the middleman out. Most
miners would like to see all ores treated and
refined in Australia, but short of that the
present monopoly, created by Hughes, is
about as popular as Hughes himself.
* • * *
Westralia produced £159,987 worth of
minerals other than gold for the half-year
ended June 30. On that date the total pro-
duction had amounted to a little over 4J
millions sterling. Copper and tin accounted
for over three-fifths.

“.T.E.0.” on Australian oil possibilities:—
G. D. Meudell writes (B. 26/7/T7) about oil and
its possibilities in Australia, and suggests boring the
continent thoroughly in search thereof. It would be
a good thing if oil could be found in this country,
but it is useless to spend large sums in drilling with-
out first having reasonable indications. Australia
has many of the geological features which have
accompanied oil in other places, but these features
are not at all a proof of its existence, any more
than granite is a proof of the existence of tin
or osmiridium. Oil is chiefly found in strata of the
tertiary and permo-carboniferous systems-; tin is
almost certain evidence of granite. Without some
surface indications of oil it would be a mere waste
of monel' boring blindfoldedly in the vast areas of
sedimentary deposits that exist all over the con-
tinent. The money could be much better expended
on the development of the proved shale deposits of
N. S. Wales and Tasmania, which are, without doubt,
capable of producing Australia’s requirements in both
fuel and volatile oils.
A couple of years ago, owing to a discovery of
bitumen washed up on the southern and south-
western shores of Tasmania, a mild boom took place
in the State. The Tasmanian Oil Wells Co. was
formed for the purpose of boring land held adjoin-
ing the property owned and worked by the Latrobe
Shale Co., which was at the time under offer to the
Tasmanian Government. A prospectus (copyright by
G. D. Meudell) was issued. This document stated,
among other things quite as amazing, that “to find
oil by boring in Tasmania is a certainty.... It would
not take more than six months to reach the oil
sar.ds... .This is essentially a poor man’s invest-
ment.” Unfortunately for the poor man, the project
was not allowed to mature, because the Federal
Government, upon advice, disallowed the flotation.
Another company was formed and floated to bore for
oil at Bruni Island, near Hobart. Mines Minister
Ogden secured the services of the Commonwealth’s
oil expert to report upon the prospects, and in
his conclusions he stated “that tp bore for petroleum
on the evidence which is available is a waste of
public money at a time when money is required for
urgent national purposes and for legitimate enter-
prise.” However, money was raised from the public
and spent in the search for a gusher, which up to
the present has not arrived. The Minister was
severely criticised by interested persons for interfer-
ing with enterprise, but his action was an effort bo
enlighten investors, and, so far as results have gone,
has been justified. Dr. Wade’s report on the South
Aus. oil wells (where it is recently reported that
“shark’s teeth” have been discovered in the- bores)
was equally definite, and is also so far borne out
by results. Legitimate mining enterprise should be
encouraged, but there are many more promising and
safer directions in which money can be applied than
in blind-stabbing for oil at a time when money is
scarce.

Why Look Old ?
You need not use hair dye to conceal grey hair.
Grey hair adds years to anyone’s appear-
ance. The objections to dyeing the hair are
too numerous and too obvious to mention.
Few people are aware that a simple, old-
fashioned recipe, that can be made up at
home, will quickly turn grey hair back to a
natural color without injuring the hair in
any way. One has only to get two ounces
of concentrate of tammalite from the chemist
and mix it with three ounces of bay rum.
With a small sponge apply the lotion to the
hair for a few nights and the desired change
will have been neatly accomplished. This
simple formula has given great satisfac-
tion for generations to those in possession of
it.—A pvt. ff!

THIS instep-strap shoe has been specially
designed to look trim and dainty
beneath those delightfully feminine
frills and flounced hems that have
returned to vogue again. It is a particularly
pretty shoe, with a fine air of individuality
about it. The front part is patent leather,
and the back and strap are black flowered
brocade, set off by an attractive slide and
ornament of dull silver. The picture gives
some idea of the happiness of this combina-
tion, but the shoe itself is far more charm-
ing than the picture. Made in the dainty,
short-fronted shape and with that smart
Cuban heel which makes walking so comfort-
able, it really is a “find” in the way of
shoes. And ladies will see other and equally
delightful shoes in the latest Bostock ship-
ments, especially if they view them at once.

taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability, is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

Strong Enough for Every Service
The tyre that has to be
“nursed” to get mileage from
it has no place in either country
or town service- in Australia.
The roads, or what sometimes
are called roads by courtesy,
will not let you get mileage
from any but the best tyres.
On the wood blocks or
asphalt pavements of the city
itself any properly inflated
tyre should show results —
But for the suburban high-
ways and byways, and for the
main-road travel —where dis-
tances are long and conditions
are severe, where trouble is
trouble in dead earnest the
cheap tyre is a delusion, a
snare, and always a disap-
pointment.
Goodyear A.W.T. Tyres
are built strong; enough for
o o
every service—they need no
pampering in city street, sub-
urban avenue or on the rough
country road.
Goodyear Tyres are all road
tyres from every point of view
—ready for all the road has
to offer and aide to withstand
all threatened trouble when
it comes.
They are the sum total of all
J
that is best in knowledge,
materials and method in the
general tyre-making field.
Goodyear I yres are the
natural outcome of a manu-
facturing policy that refuses
to recognise any but the
o j
highest quality in materials
and craftsmanship.
Your car should be shod with Goodyears,
if you believe that the best is inevitably
the most economical in the long run.
Buy them of the Goodyear Service
Station Dealer: there are more than
500 already established throughout
Australia, and the number is added to
ever) 7 day.
The Goodyear Service Station Dealer
is eager to help you get out of every
Goodyear Tyre the final mile of service
we have put into them.
And he has other things to offer you
in this endeavor to secure you in
getting value from your Goodyear Tyre
equipment.
Tubes, for instance. . Goodyear Tubes,
built to ensure the long service Good-
year Covers are capable of delivering if
you will let them.
And that handy package of tyre pre-
paredness known as the Goodyear “Tyre
Saver Kit,” which contains every essen-
tial first-aid to tyres.
Meet the Goodyear Service Station
Dealer the next time you want any
advice on the much vexed question of
tyres. There is one somewhere near

BANANA LAND
The stupid Northern Railway strike might
never have occurred had the contemptuous
term “scab” not been applied with such em-
phasis during the preliminaries. When the
downing of tools started, Mackay and Cairns
were going to let the Townsville men settle
their own little affair, which wouldn’t have
taken long, but Townsville retorted that if
they did they would be scabs. The effect
was magical. Down went implements at
Cairns and Mackay with a clank. That
the Northern men thus scabbed on the pub-
lic did not of course get a moment’s con-
sideration. It never will until the public
falls on the responsible parties with heavy
penalties.
Passed out in Sydney, George Birch, of
Rockhampton, a partner in the solid Banana-
land firm of Birch and Carroll. Birch hailed
from the Cold Country and hung out his sign
over the Union and Criterion hotels at
Rockhampton for some 30 years. In 1917
he hitched up with E. J. Carroll in the
show business, and to-day the firm is run-
ning 21 film picture palaces besides renting
film to 65 others.
A Lullaby.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Afar in the mountains the spiders of even-
ing
Are; weaving their grey webs to bury the
day;
The brownies are driving the butterflies
homeward,
The. earth has its sun-scatter tidied away.
The creek shines no longer a ribbon of silver,
The golden is gone from the laurel-tree’s
crest;
’Tis time that all birdies and babies were
sleeping,
So hush thee to rest.
Oh, hush, for the black wolves of night-
time, the shadows,
Have stolen the starlight and hidden it deep.
A goblin’s abroad, and the fairies are fright-
ened ;
But mother is holding thee, little love —sleep.
And to-morrow, when sunbeams are gilding
the gum-trees,
When the wind is awake, and the little birds
sing,
With my heart for a castle, the world for
thy kingdom,
I’ll crown thee a king.
Thy treasury filled with the gold of the
wattle,
And sequins of sunlight the leaves flutter
- down,
And the blue sea will lend us a strip of her
satin,
All sheeny with sunshine, to weave thee a
gown.
Thy sceptre a bright poinsettia blossom,
Thy herald a red robin carolling near,
And then we will make of a little grey
’possum
Thy courtier, dear.
So sleep thee, my small one, and dream till
the morning
Of seashell, and sunbeam, and flower-trim-
ming fay,
Of the songs of the creek, and the golden
leaves’ laughter,
Till shadows are lost in the bright light of
day.
Oh, hushaby! hushaby ! goblins come creep-
ing,
’Tis time little birdies and babies were sleep-
ing.
QUEENSLAND.
ERNESTINE HEMMINGS.
Pig at Biggenden.
Randolph Bedford goes exploring:—
Biggenden is 50 miles west of Maryborough and
on the railroad to Gayndah, which again extends
itself to Mudingburra and there stops, maybe for
years, before taking its last leap to the border of
Queensland territory, out by the setting sun. Big-
genden hopes to spell iron-mines one day; at this
moment it spells pigs. All trains takes them east
and south to that death whose resurrection is pork,
bacon, lard and smallgoods. White, black, yellow,
red and black and tan, they leave Biggenden in
two-decked cars after shrieking for half a day and
a night.
I struck Biggenden on a pig-sale day, and was
impressed with neither sweet scents nor harmony.
One, who used to be a boar, and went out of that
business 12 months ago, was treated to dentistry
by the painful method before entraining. His
tusks promised torn pig on the journey, he being
a powerful brute and quarrelsome, so the gentlemen
who were acting as his forwarding agents seized him,
held his mouth open by a rope hitched around his
upper jaw, and then knocked out his tusks with
DIDN'T WANT TO BACK A DOUBLE.
“You see, Doc, it’s this way. I want a girl; my wife wants a boy!”
“Well, then, why do you worry?"
“I’m afraid neither of us may he disappointed."
a hammer. The expostulatory remarks offered dur-
ing the operation make me suspect that his den-
tists did not use cocaine. But that there was neces-
sity for dentistry he proved by running amok
as soon as he got into the loading yards. Unable
to appreciate the loss of his tusks he tackled any-
thing in sight, until three smaller and more active
pigs beat him to a standstill. Than that there was no
other standstill in the yard, nor in the trucks. The
gentlemen pigs fought little, except to get all four
feet in the trough and act like sugar-refiners. There
was in their greedy rage something Of the resentment
of a parson under interjection. But the lady pigs
scrapped and screamed for the pleasure of it, re-
senting the nearness of one another and filling the
welkin with shrieks and obscene noises.
I left this clamor at Biggenden, and went
south through the forest land to the foothills of
creeper-grown scrub —tall, black pine-woods upon
their flanks and skirting the winding, shining, little
creek half hidden by scrub and vines and creepers.
So to the bismuth mine in the hills, and the bismuth
mine’s burned-out mill-house. All the way up that
creek such bad immigrants and undesirable aliens as
lantana and prickly-pear are eating the sane woods
and grasses as evil devours beauty.
Biggenden Mountain was opened up many years
ago by a company that Huntingdon-milled the iron
for a result of bismuth that does not appear to have
paid well. A fire took the mill-house after the co.
had handled a few thousand tons, incidentally having
to elevate tails because of building at the toe of
the hill instead of somewhere about the knee. The
present owners bought it three years ago for bismuth,
but have not crushed a ton yet, the building of the
new mill forcing the labor to loaf on the job because
they have to wait for tools and plant. Meantime
all the talk is not of the ore, but of the gangue.
The law gives the lessee the metals he applies for
the right to mine, and reserves the others; so the
lessee can mine bismuth but not iron. It is a high-
grade iron, too, turning out an excellent quality of
pig of high tensile strength, and the Government has
reserved the ore here and elsewhere for its essay at
iron production. A high Protection is already exist-
ent without a duty, and not all dependent on freight;
for pig in Pittsburg is worth 45 dollars a ton. That
condition will probably persist for some time after
the war and during the work of reconstruction; and
by that time Australia will probably have lost out
of politics the imported little men who bleat of
foreign trade.
In the open cuts and shaft and tunnel and crosscuts
and winze on Biggenden Mountain there are 100,000
tons exposed; and lateral extension visible on surface
should make half a million tons more than probable
on development. There are other good-looking iron
shows in the district, and enough to start on, any-
how.
When I reached the railway again the pigs were
still in their double-decked cars on the side tracks,
and still acting like Opposition criticising Govern-
ment. The long night was punctuated by the
shrieks of the lady pigs—scratching each other's
faces with their sharp hoofs; trying for shoulder
bites; making insulting remarks about the charac-
ters of their rivals. It sounded like nothing else
so much as one of those meetings for women which
are helping to distinguish Brisbane just now; but
in the shocking noises there was a suggestion of a
Rev. Yellow Pup using language calculated to pro-
voke a breach of the peace on July the Twelfth.
Still, one may be doing the pigs an injustice; no
man may tell of the abilities for language of meet-
ings for women, and of sectarians, until he has seen
them in the conditions of double-decked pig-cars
waiting for a train. But, maybe, one of these
days
That night the snorer in the Sounding Board
Hotel had it all his own way until the devil entered
into the swine again, suddenly taking possession
of the night, and evicting sleep; a few small,
unsexed pigs saying nothing, seeking the corners to
find peace, and putting flat noses out to smell the
moonlight, while the battle of the old-lady pigs
raged in the middle of the truck.
There is nothing so perverse as a pig, unless it'
be man or woman. And either of them can run
amok without losing their tusks by the painful
method of extraction.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, August 25, 1917.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
A garden party at Govt. House on Thurs-
day afternoon was the social event of the
week. The executive of the Red Cross and
the V.A.D. girls were the guests, and her Ex.
was assisted as hostess by Mrs. Cozens.
At other pleasure parties during the past
few days Mrs. Lex Craig was hostess at
one; Mrs. Donald Fletcher, of Eurella,
managed another; Miss Kitty Ryan (girls
and kewpies were her guests) presided at the
Coo-ee Cafe; and Mrs. Jim McWhirter
rounded up the clans to honor Mrs.'George
Johnson, of Sydney.
Several women who worked hard to estab-
lish the Residential Club for returned sol-
diers are feeling disappointed. When the
coaxing of money from the public began it
was advanced as a bait that the object was
to provide a cheap but good home for
otherwise homeless warriors. Now. the
management, which is in the control of the
Returned Soldiers and Sailors’ Association,
has fixed the tariff at 255. a week, notwith-
standing that in tow r n there are several
boarding-houses which make special reduc-
tions for returned soldiers —255. a week, or
less. What the organising women of the Club
want to know is whether the patriotic estab-
lishment is to be a money-making concern,
and, if so, who is to get the profits?
Capt. F. Hinton, back from the Front, is
in luck. Miss Margaret Largey has agreed
to do her best for him in future.
Two well-known Bananalanders went over
the hill during the week. One was A. J.
Kirk (54), stock inspector, who died sud-
denly at Normanton; the other E. Temple
Curtis, a well-liked Customs officer, who
died suddenly at Clayfield. He had been in
the Department since 1883, stationed, in
turn, at Maryborough, Gladstone and Thurs-
day Island. His widow was a daughter of
the late Lawrence Smith, one of the best-
known of. the earlier Brisbane settlers. An
only son, Lieut, de Burgh Curtis, is in France
with the A.I.F.
Bowen is congratulating Mrs. ,7. M.
Doyle on her recovery from a long illness
which followed the death of her husband,
one of the oldest shipping identities on the
Australian coast. He managed the
A.U.S.N. Co.’s Sydney wharves for some
years until 1890, when he came north and
controlled Burns. Philp’s extensive Towns-
ville shipping interests. He had fine com-
mand over men and was as effective as a
couple of Arbitration Court judges in set-
tling local disputes. He had been in
retirement for 12 years when the end came.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
In five years Wilthorn railway station, on
the Cooyar line, has produced the following
traffic: 39 passengers (fares 335. 7d.), goods
outward £27 ss. Bd., ditto inward £l4 10s.
9d. Kates, on the Goomburra line, has had
30 passengers (fares 20s. 7d.), outward
goods revenue 10s. 6d., and inward ditto
£2 14s. 4d. Britain and Australia having
no use for money just now, and every pre-
sent railway line being congested like this,
let’s borrow another 10 or 20 millions to
open up more ebuntry!

In the Overland Big Four-cylinder touring
car and the Overland Light Four-cylinder

are in proper balance to produce lasting satis-
faction.
The Big Four is finished in a deep rich

These two models exemplify the remark-
able values made possible by tremendous pro-
duction.
Size does not of itself constitute greatness.
An industry is as great as its service to
the public.
But size does usually constitute oppor-
tunity for service.
In the case of Willys-Overland, size origi-
nally was an indication of foresight and courage.
It constituted an opportunity
new values in motor cars —this was Sfservice.
Growth came as a reward of this service,
and with growth came opportunity for further
service—further revision of values.
Today’s immensity means experience,
facilities and resources, plus the public con*
fidence, which gives these things stability for
yet greater service.
The Willys-Overland ideal of service is in-
tegrity of value in • the product throughout

“THEY COME AS A BOON AND A BLESSING TO MEN,
THE PICKWICK, THE OWL AND THE WAVERLEY PEN.’*
Miss Gladys Cooper Explains
Some Beauty Secrets.
The Popular London Star Favors Simple Methods.
I have been asked to give a few simple recipes that I know, either through personal
use or by observation, to be valuable to the toilet, and which are within the reach of the
average woman. In these days of £lOO facial treatments and elaborate and expensive
beautifying processes my suggestions may read like lessons in economy, but they are
not especially so intended. They are merely practical suggestions, in which the keynote
is “effectiveness.” All the materials or ingredients which I mention are either already
at hand in the home or may be readily procured from the chemist. Fortunately Ido not
suffer from the ailments or troubles enumerated below, but some people who are not so
fortunate have told me their experiences, and with your permission I will set forth some
remedies which they have found to be efficacious.
Home-made Hair Tonic.
My acquaintances say that a good stimulant
to tlie natural growth of the hair is boranium.
A very simple, effective and safe stimulating
lotion made up by mixing one ounce of bora-
nium with i pint of bay rum. This may be
applied to the hair roots occasionally with the
finger-tips with good results.
Complexion Renewals.
Complexion experts advise me that a normal,
healthy complexion is constantly renewing it-
self by dropping off tiny flakes of worn-out
tissue, thus revealing the fresh young skin
underneath. They sa.v that when this process
is cheeked by age, expo-
sure or some of mauy
causes, the complexion
becomes dull and ugly.
The rational treatment
recommended is to help
the skin perform the
natural functions of
“shedding” worn-out tis-
sue. For this purpose, I
am informed, there is
nothing so good as pure
mercolized wax, used for
a few nights, just as you
would use a face cream.
It is claimed that it pos-
sesses a special affinity
for the effete scarf-skin,
which it quickly removes
by absorbing it. The face.
I am assured, will soon
look much younger and
prettier under this treat-
ment.
The Curling Iron.
Don’t use a hot iron to
cur] your hair. Some of
my friends make the cun-
ningest sort of curls
wherever they want
them simply by damp-
ing the hair with liquid
silmerine before retiring
at night. When the hair is dry in the morn-
ing it will be softly curly just where you
want it to be. This method is perfectly harm-
less, even beneficial to the hair, and the curls
last a long time. The liquid is quite pleasant
and neither sticky nor greasy.
How to Shampoo.
Most women, I am informed, do not know
how to use stallax properly when shampooing
with it. Unless the hair is naturally very
oily, a stallax shampoo may sometimes leave
it rather dry. But I am told if you will apply
olive oil freely to your hair and scalp just
before shampooing with stallax, the result is
most delightful. The hair will be left clean,
soft, bright and wavy, the olive oil having
properly balanced the action of this wonderful
hair cleanser.
Is Powder Necessary?
A great many women object to using pow-
der, for various reasons. The following
formula is a good one: Dissolve an ounce of
MISS GLADYS COOPER.
Photo Wrather & \Buy.
cleminite in four tablespoonfuls of water, or
witch-hazel, and use it as a face lotion,
smoothing the skin with the lingers until it
is dry. This method is perfectly harmless.
I am told that a really beautiful, natural, vel-
vety bloom results and remains for many hours
quite unaffected by the most trying conditions
out of doors or in the ballroom or theatre,
and that it gives a much more natural appear-
ance to some skins than does ordinary powder.
Grey Hair.
I have observed many attempts of many
people to conceal grey hair. Some of these
experiments were amusing, some disastrous,
and some were success-
ful. Personally I believe
I shall let my hair turn
when the appointed time
comes, but if I were
Foing to try to evade it,
would give a trial to a
real old “grandmother”
formula that would prob-
ably do the work. This
formula, I am informed,
has been used with de-
grees of success for many
generations, and consists
merely of two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite
mixed with three ounces
of bay rum. It is ap-
plied to the grey hair
a few times with a small
sponge, and ladies tell
me it appears to darken
the hair to a natural
shade, not like a dye, but
gradually and naturally.
Miscellaneous Hints.
A few useful hints from
my friends on aids to
beauty, are as follows:
Theeyebrowsand lashes
may be made to grow
stronger and darker by
applying mennaline, a
specially delicate pomade, with the hnger-nps
at night. , „„
Rouge is often obvious, and many
therefore prefer to use powdered colliandum,
which gives a most natural tint. _
The lips may be prevented from drying ana
chapping by touching them with a stica
of prolactum, which also gives them a goo a
Freckles are most effectively combated with
ammoniated mercury creams, of which jeua-
line is the best.

culture in general was written by Miss uiaays
Cooper at the request of the manufacturers or

“Umfaan” sees a colleague go over: —
When John Alexander Hobson died in- a French
hospital from an accidental injury, the last chapter
in a tragic fight ended. Hobson was at one time
a major in the Royal Horse Artillery, but, partly
through assisting a subaltern with Government
funds, he came under the ban in India during a Kit-
chener Spring-clean. He was “broke,” and, landing in
Melbourne penniless, swept out and. worked around the
Markets. Afterwards he was made a staff-sergeant-
major ; and in Brisbane many a Citizen Force artil-
lery officer spent the Sunday afternoon at “Hobby’s ’
getting wrinkles for exams. Generally “Hobby”
received a new pair of gloves as his reward for a
pass. On the outbreak of war, mainly through the
efforts of Major “Gus” Hughes (now Colonel and
D. 5.0.) he left as battery-sergeant-major, and on the
Peninsula used as a sergeant-major the “Hobson-
Molyneaux” patent for laying telephone wire, of
which he was the principal inventor. After the
evacuation he was granted his commission. Just
afterwards he came into £6OO a year, which grati-
fied the old chap mightily. But he was more grati-
fied still to be received cordially at the Cavalry Club
while on leave in England. His accident occurred
at a chateau, where he was instructing at a corps
artillery school back of the line. Slipping down a
flight of stairs, he broke his thigh, and died two
days later of abdominal injuries.
“Half-moon”: On Cape York there is a
cocoanut plantation of 100 acres which is
one of the best attempts at the business
about Torres Strait. Tropical traders are
slowly waking to the possibilities of the
cocoanut, but the Queensland Agricultural
Department characteristically sleeps on the
subject. With the assistance of Adriano
Hernandez, editor of the Manila Agri-
cultural Review, I have gathered some
facts about the cocoanut industry in the
Philippines. There are 30 million palms
bearing and 24 million immature. In 1915
the export of copra was 131,558 tons. For
the year ending June, 1916, the export of
oil was 3,242,866 gallons—-value £622,533.
They used for home consumption in round
figures 500,000 gallons—value £74.000 —the
manufacture of the smaller mills. The cen-
tral or big mills handle the export trade.
The small mills owned and operated by in-
dividual farmers are scattered through the
region. In these the nut is husked by the
sharp-pointed tapasan at an average of 100
nuts per hour. The kabiawan, a revolving
brush with strong sharp teeth, extracts the
meat at the same rate. The meat having
sieved and ground, is boiled in metal bowls,
husks and shells providing the fuel. After
boiling the liapitan, or press, extracts the
oil (60 per cent.) by several crushings.
Most of the implements are home-made.
With two presses, one grinder and two meat
extractors three men will extract oil from
2000 nuts in a week. Quantity 50 to 70
gallons. When I chew over these figures
and then think of the empty North with its
possibilities, I feel like heaving the biggest
nut available at —well, not at Billy Hughes.
He’s winning the war for us, I hear.
Sydney Telegraph, reviewing Isaac
Macrossan’s book, “The War After the
War,” quotes him as showing that “a world-
wide freeze-out business campaign against
Germany will be very difficult to carry into
effect.” Already, says Macrossan, Germans
by thousands are becoming naturalised citi-
zens of Holland, Spain. Switzerland and
Denmark, building factories there and ship-
ping the product into the strongholds of
Germany’s enemies, stamped with neutral'
names. “Much of the ‘Swiss’ chocolate you
buy in Paris was made by Teutonic hands.”
The Telegraph’s comment is:—
Apparently it may be confidently anticipated that
as soon as the war is over—and perhaps before —
there will be an influx into Australia of imports
from Holland, Spain, Switzerland and Denmark, and
the bulk of them will be of German make, the Ger-
man manufacturer having taken the necessarv steps
which entitle him to call himself a Dutchman,
Spaniard, Swiss or Dane. How is this danger to be
guarded against by consular certificates of origin?
The only way to guard against the danger is
for the Australian Parliament to get busy
right away and replace the gaping palings
°f. this country’s ludicrous tariff fence by a
scientifically-built Protective tariff wall of re-
inforced concrete clamped with steel, high
enough to keep out foreign imports and
give Australia the chance of making for her-
self what she requires. But, of course, the
Telegraph doesn’t suggest that.
Australian artist Alek Sass sketches New
fork’s latest Bohemia: —
A block square and half as high, built in the form
of a huge H, and located up town in New York,
you'll find the Lincoln Arcade—and Bohemia.
Frank Nankivell has his movies studio here; the
MaeDougalls of Sydney brass-plate the “Headquarters
of the Scottish Theatre of America” (she was with
Julian Ashton) ; R. G. Russom, of N. Y. Times, is
a neighbor ; Harry Julius works in my studio ; Louis
Esson arid his wife are across the hall; Albeit
Goldie comes in to talk high-finance and Dulcie
Deamer to rest her nerves and enthuse about Coney
Island; and Aleck (Cruickshanlc and Milo) Laing
“rests” between here and his studio, where he is
making the funniest “animated” I’ve seen. The
“perfesh” sends its quota on Sundays. Howard
Chandler Christy is the last of the American old-
school to stay on. The Futurists and Cubists are
swarming now. They eat chop-suey by night and
sleep by day. They hang their pictures always on
their ceilingp. This way they can admire their art
without rising. Marcel Duchamp, whose “Nude
Descending a Staircase” was the talk of two con-
tinents, is perhaps the most famous of the new-
comers. Owen Johnson lived here and got the local
color for his book, “The Woman Gives.” And we
have for everlasting scandal —The Baroness! The
Baron being a prisoner in The Tower of London,
the Baroness consoles herself in “thees so crude
America” with her dog and parrot, two young alliga-
tors, a pair of turtles, some white rats and a
monkey. Her street costume is mostly beads, bits
of stained glass and a cross between a bathing-suit
and a sunset. It was to interview her that the star
reporteress came with a photographer from one of
the big N.Y. dailies. The interview over, the
Baroness was asked would she be so kind as to pose
for a picture. The Baroness was too kind. Delight-
edly and unashamed, she stood up and casually-
dropped her cloak —which was her complete attire
that day. The photographer didn’t get a picture.
Some men are rattled so easily.
Eleven retired C. of E. bishops from
Australasia live in England. Which almost
for certain means that Australasia has had
11 C. of E. bishops who never regarded this
country as their home.

A SUGGESTION
To Overcome the Present Deplorable Position that Australia Finds Herself j h
Regarding Her Failure to Adequately Reinforce Her Soldiers at the Front, and
so Maintain Her Honor in the Eyes of Her Allies. \ ’
The actual position to-day.
A. (1) The Commonwealth Government has fixed the monthly Reinforcements reouirei
at 7000 men per month.
(2) Experts all declare this figure to be quite unobtainable under the present system
unless men quite unsuitable for the work are accepted.
(3) Even if the number were attained it would not comprise the quality of material
required. At present a large percentage of the recruits we are getting are boys and elderly
married men.
Difficulties in the way of immediate rectification.
B. (1) The Prime Minister, before the elections, definitely promised that his Govern
ment would not introduce Conscription by Regulation or Statute during the life of the
present Parliament.
(2) He further promised that even a Referendum on the question would not be sub-
mitted to the people “unless the tide of b:\ttle should turn against the Allies” (see Benalla
speech)
(3) The Nationalists as a body, tacitly or otherwise, accepted these pledges and went
to the ballot behind their leaders.
These pledges inviolable.
C. (11 Any attempt to break pledge B 1 will almost assuredly estrange not only the anti-
conscriptionists in the population, many of whom must have voted National at the elections
but will also lie strongly opposed, even by many firm believers in Conscription.
(2) The belief of the people in the honor of Australia, and in the honor of her public
men in high places, will be grievously shaken if either of pledges B 1 or B 2 are disregarded.
(3) Any attempt to break pledges B 1 or B 2 will certainly mean a general strike, and
possibly risings in various centres.
(4) Another Referendum means that the Government breaks pledge 82, because they are
not ready to give back tbe positions they gained by making the promises B 1 and 82.
The solution of the difficulty.
D. (1) A complete Conscription measure should be passed through both Houses, on the
definite understanding that it should not become law until ratified by the electors at an elec-
tion. so that the electors could clearly understand what was the proposal of the Govern-
ment. „ ■ ... ,
(2) The Government would then say: “We find our position is an impossible one, and we
now leave the matter in the hands of the electors."
(31 The Government should cordially invite the Opposition to help them draw up the
fairest possible Conscription Bill, and then go to the country on it. each side agreeing that
they would honestly abide by the decision of the people.
