New Stomachs for Old
By Arthur True Buswell, M.D.
EUGENE CHRISTIAN
THOUSANDS of people
who suffered for years
with all sorts of stomach
trouble are walking
around to-day with en-
tirely remade stomachs.
They enjoy their meals
and never have a thought
of indigestion, constipa-
tion or any of the serious
illnesses with which they formerly suffered
and which are directly, traceable to the
stomach.
And these surprising results have been
produced not by drugs or medicines of any
kind, not by foregoing substantial foods, not
by eating specially prepared or patented
foods of any kind, but by eating the foods
we like best correctly Combined.
These facts were forcibly brought to my
mind by Eugene Christian, the eminent Food
Scientist, who has successfully treated over
23,000 people with foods alone!
In a recent talk with Eugene Christian
he told me of some of his experiences in the
treatment of various ailments through food
—just a few instances out of the more than
23,000 cases he has on record.
One case of what proper food combina-
tions can do was that of a man one hundred
pounds overweight, whose only other discom-
fort was rheumatism. This man s greatest
pleasure in life was eating. Though con-
vinced of the necessity, he hesitated for
months to go under treatment, believing he
would be deprived of the pleasures of the
table. He finally, however, decided to try it
out. Not only did he begin losing weight at
once, quickly regaining his normal figure, all
signs of rheumatism disappearing, but he
found the new diet far more delicious to the
taste and afforded a much keener quality of
enjoyment than his old method of eating, and
wrote Christian a letter to that effect.
This instance of the efficacy of right
eating I have simply chosen at random from
perhaps a dozen Eugene Christian told me
of, every one of which was fully as interest-
ing and they applied to as many different
ailments. Surely this man Christian is doing
a great work.
There have been so many inquiries from
all parts of the United States from people
seeking the benefit of Eugene Christian’s
advice and whose cases he is unable to handle
personally that he has written a little course
of lessons which tells you exactly w'hat to eat
for health, strength and efficiency. This
course is published by The Corrective Eating
Society of New York and Melbourne. .
These lessons, there are 24 of them, con-
tain actual menus for breakfast, luncheon
and dinner, curative, as w r ell as corrective,
covering every condition of health and sick-
ness from infancy to ohl age and for all occu-
pations, climates and seasons.
With these lessons at hand it is just as
though you were in personal contact with
the great food specialist, because every pos-
sible point is so thoroughly covered that you
can scarcely think -of a question which isn’t
answered. You can start eating the very
things that will produce the increased
physical and mental energy you are seeking
the day you receive the lessons, and you will
find that you secure results .with the first
meal.
If you would like to examine these 24
Little Lessons in Corrective Eating, simply
write The Corrective Eating Society, 100
Flinders Street, Melbourne, or 7 Moore
Street, Sydney-. It is not necessary to
enclose any money with your request. Merely
ask them to send the lessons on five days’
trial with the understanding that you will
either return them within that time or remif
215., the small fee asked.
Please clip out and mail the following form instead of writing a letter, as this
is a copy of the blank adopted by the Society and will be honored at once.
CORRECTIVE EATING SOCIETY,
100 Flinders Street, Melbourne, or
7 Moore Street, Sydney.
You may send me prepaid a copy of Corrective Eating in 24 Lessons,
remail them to you within five days or send you 21s.

# * * *
“Simon”: George Burgess, whip of the
late N.S.W. Labor Government, who fol-
lowed Holman into the Nationalistcamp only
to be unhorsed at the recent elections, has
been found a job inspecting stock routes and
reserves. It recalls that Burgess once found
Holman a job. Some 24 years ago Burgess
was in charge of the South-western branch
of the A.W.U., then the A.S.U. Holman had
just stepped forth a free man after the
Daily Post trouble. The future looked very
gloomy indeed—and then Bill received a wire
offering him the position of organiser. Of
course he jumped at it, and soon was jogging
along the roads in a sulky with a pack horse
hitched behind and the Daily Post night-
mare fading farther and farther into the dis-
tance. One day he alighted by a brook to
gaze upon himself, like Narcissus, or else to
get a drink. He reckons he probably forgot
about the horses. The pack-animal, how-
ever, made the nearest pub 14 miles away
all right, but the sulky-prad, after freeing
himself, is thought to have suicided in the
bush somewhere. Another trip took Holman
away for a whole month, during which time
he rounded up a couple of thousand shearers,
rduseabouts and off-siders, and fairly talked
them into a solid, silent and concrete mass.
Several of ’em even offered him their jobs.
Burgess heard Holman’s report of all this in
silence, then asked, “How many tickets did
you issue?” “Tickets?” said Holman. “I
didn’t know I had to attend to details like
that.” The “details” (at 20s. each) were
the very existence of the organisation, of
course. Bill has worked more or less on the
same lines ever since.
« * # #
“Peg-Leg”: Just-dead Robert Scobie,
M.L.A., was as hard a proposition as ever
faced the gallery. He didn’t speak; he
rumbled, boomed and brrrred from the depths
of his waistcoat, and even if the men on the
floor understood, him, all that came up to
the boys resembled a trombone aggravating
the low notes. That’s why he got less space
than worse men who had occupied a seat for
only a month. Incidentally the man we used
to call in as interpreter when Scobie spoke
was musical critic on one of the dailies.

“N.E.W.”: The attempt to find rhymes
for “orange,” “silver,” “meadow,” “window,”
“chimney” and some others has exercised
the ingenuity of many verse-carpenters.
“Orange” and “silver” would seem to be
absolutely rhymeless. Horace Smith made
“slim knee” rhyme with “chimney,” and
“D.McC.” (B. 5/7/’l7) seems to think that
in “sinn’d so” he has found a rhyme for
“window.” I think he should see an ear-
specialist. Austin Dobson had a try at
“window,” and this is the best he could do:
.... as only one’s feminine kin do,
Some muslin-clad Mabel or May; —
There now! I’ve broken the window !
Bluebottle’s off and away.
I guess I can do better than that myself:—
A RHYME AND A RECIPE.
Take 6f eggs from a dozen to twenty,
Arid whisll to a froth in a bowl,
Adding raisins and currants and plenty
Of brandy to flavor the whole;
With some flour to make dough — very thin dough
Then#tir it and sweeten and bake,
And yoirll have, with a rhyme for your window,
An excellent cake.
I have some doubt about the recipe, but I
think the rhyme’s all right.
ON APPEARANCES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“They were farmers and the sons of farmers —
men who had gone with stout hearts and keen-bladed
axes into what would appear to the townsman to be
the impenetrable recesses of the marsupial section of
the animal kingdom. The forests had fallen at their
bidding.” —Sydney D.T. on the Volunteer Workers.
It would appear... .Nay, what would it appear?
They went with axes keen and stubborn heart,
They told the forests in their might to clear
That they might tear the kangaroos apart;
And the impenetrable wombat broke
Before the force of their heroic stroke.
It would appear that in their splendid zeal
The wallaby was rent from stem to stern ;
And, punctured grandly with their fearless steel,
The whole marsupial section had to learn
That no recces was free from these bold men
Who wore the bowyang and the number 10.
It would appear.... nay,. let the thing appear.
What must be will be, sages all agree.
Their corded muscles in their strength uprear
The goods some merry ship may bear to sea....
“Hut that impenetrable paragraph
Mooes through the section like an ink-gorged calf.
N.S.W.
MARY McC.

The Turn of the Road.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
"VHERE confident, calm I strode,
\ \ I walk with hesitant feet;
For at yonder turn of the road
What shall I meet?
The youth of the day has gone,
And my shadow goes before ;
I know that the road runs on—
I know no more.
I have travelled a goodly way.
As one at a glance may see,
Since the East and the break o’ day
Called out to me.
Though the highway be hard to miss
With its signs and stones and such,
The worst of the road is this —
It turns too much.
For a part of its length it flows
(Too brief is that stretch, alas!)
’Twixt hedges of palm and rose,
O’er fern and grass.
There Follies flutter and flit,
And Fancies, fleeting and frail,
And a host of roysterers sit
At cakes and ale.
The Follies fade with the hour,
The Fancies dwindle and die;
And the nectared heart of the flower
In time grows dry.
The cakes and the ale are done,
Apd the roysterer’s moment gone;
Rut under the slanting sun
The road winds on.
Good-bye to rose and fern,
And the hedges at each grass-side !
Who knows at the next road-turn
What pleasures bide?
What pleasures? ’Tis turned, and lo !
Hot miles and a driving dust
’Twixt trees that are grey as woe,
Gnarled limbs out-thrust!
Yet Hope is a bright allure,
A light in the hearts of men—-
At the end of a league, for sure,
’Twill turn again.
Though the highway be hard to miss
With its signs and stones and such,
The best of the road is this—
It turns so much !
Towards the turn of the roadway yon
I walk with hesitant feet;
When I come to the turn anon —
What shall I meet?
N.s.W.
RODEBIC QUINN.
Mr. Belchamber.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
UP above Sydney Bay the great pine-
trees stirred their horizontal
branches softly in the gentle nor’-
eastei’, and the green hills and valleys of the
island seemed to sleep through the after-
noon sunshine.
Mr. Dumaresq Belchambor paused in his
work on the hillside, and refreshed his
meaty soul with long and meditative staring
across the l'oofs of the settlement, and the
soldiers’ barracks, and the two rectangular
enclosures of the old gaol, and the men. and
the white line of surf on the reef, and the
deeply-blue sea —into the world that lay
beyond the round rim of the horizon.
He did not see Kingstown, or little
Nepean, or the high hump of Phillip Island
to the south, or any part of the lovely and
lonely Paradise Lost in which lie stood.
Norfolk Island did not exist for Mr. Bel-
chamber whilst he stared out over the edge
of the sunlit world of waters, and out of
IS4G into 1844, and into London Town
where it crushes about Change Alley and
out along the valley of the Thames, green
and beautiful, below Richmond Ilill.
He saw Ranelagh, and Cremorne Gardens,
and Newmarket, and a little house at
Hampstead, with a frail and fair occupant.
He saw the coffee-room at White’s, he saw
Piccadilly and Pall Mall, and the changing
of the guard at St. James’s Palace. He saw
the Brighton road, and Sussex in the sum-
mer-time, and the Ship Inn. He saw the
sour face of Mr. Sergeant Foljambe, for the
prosecution, when he handed the forged
cheque to the foreman of the jury at the Old
Bailey, and the grim one of Mr. Justice
Horwood, Recorder of London, as he sen-
tenced him, Dumaresq Belcliamber, for life,
lie saw all these things, and many others -
as a man may when he stares over the
edge of the world. But he did not see the
huge form and the damnable face, with side-
whiskers, of Head-Constable Zachary Brad-
shaw as he stole along the hillside behind
him.
Round the western end of Phillip Island
the white sails of a brig gleamed, quite sud-
denly. against' the deep blue of the sea and
the light blue of the sky, and she curtseyed
and danced across the waters on her last
slant but one before, beating up against the
steady breeze, she should drop her anchor in
the patch of good holding ground opposite
Government House.
The unexpected sight of the Government
brig from Hobart Town —mails and prison-
ers—brought Mr. Belchambor back to Nor-
folk Island and IS4G. It brought him back
to the fact that he was on the hillside, with
an axe in his hand, for the purpose of cut-
ting firewood out of a fallen pine-tree for the
kitchen fire of his master, Lieutenant Bag-
shott of the 96th-wthe same Sammy Bag-
shott whom lie had ciccroncd in London in
’43, before that gentleman had found it
expedient to exchange out of the Guards
into a marching regiment, as a means of
countering the importunities of other gentle-
men with hooked noses and Old Testament
surnames: the same kindly Sammy Bag-
shott who had generously rewarded Mr.
Belehamber’s former patronage in the some-
what exclusive circle in which he had moved
iu London, by extending his own patronage
to Mr. Belchamber when the latter came,
hideously ironed, to Norfolk Island. Mr.
Belchamber sighed and turned round to
resume his chopping, and was immediately
seized by the throat and kicked in the groin
by Head-Constable Zachary Bradshaw.
It was a gi'ip of iron that he felt com-
pressing his windpipe and his carotid and
his jugular, and forcing his tongue out
through his open jaws, and he was aware of
the hideous countenance of the ex-convict
constable close to his face, and smelled his
hot, rank breath, as that pillar of discipline
.gaped down his throat, ere he flung him
away with a curse.
“Ye b —d, ye!” bellowed Mr. Brad-
shaw. “I’ve got ye now, ye dog—l’ve got
ye. Ye’ve bin chewin’ baccy! I’ve got ye.
I’ll learn ye who’s who, ye carr’on! I’ll
learn ye t’ git y’r lootinant t’ git me a
riprimarnd. Here's th’ bit I found in y’r
dirty gob—an’ here’s th’ stick it come off.
So now, ye b —d, so now!”
The constable took a half-chewed morsel
from his own mouth, and held it up before
gasping Mr. Belchamber. From his ti’ouser
pocket he drew half a plug of the black
tobacco which was issued weekly to the
prisoners, and which they might smoke or
chew in the Lumber Yard during meal
hours, but nowhere else, and at no other
time —under penalty of the Civil Command-
ant’s Court, and its inevitable sequence of
the triangles. And the convict constables
had the right to assail prisoners—as Mr.
Bradshaw had assailed Mr. Belchamber—
wheresoever and whensoever they chose to
look into their gaping mouths for the
“track,” as the nicotine stain on the tongues
of those who chewed was humorously termed.
The track was conclusive evidence.
Mr. Belchamber neither smoked nor
chewed. Nevertheless, he was perfectly well
aware that Head-Constable Bradshaw had a
clear case against him, as clear cases went
in the island, and that no protest on earth
would save him from the flogger and from a
return to the gaol-gang. And this last con-
sideration was the one that drove him mad.
For this is what it meant.
Instead of sleeping in the kitchen at the
back of Sammy Bagshott’s quarters he
would be locked each night, at seven o’clock,
into one of the sleeping wards of the prison,
with seventy or eighty of the dreadful crea-
tures whose manhood had been blotted out
by the System of Norfolk Island—there to
remain until five next morning. If you have
read Havelock Ellis, or Kraft-Ebing, you
will understand the meaning of this. It
cannot be explained here.
So Mr. Belchamber went mad. And Mr.
Bradshaw’s Nemesis prompted him to jeer
at the madman.
“So ye see, ye chicken-livered b —d, ye
done y’self no good. We’ll put th’ cross-
marks on y’r back, an’ ye’ll sleep in the
Gaol —in number one ward. We’ll see how
ye like that, ye swell! We’ll see how
long ’tis till th’ gang takes t’ callin’ ye Miss
Fanny. A fine day, me Lord Belchamber,
ain’t it? A fine”
It was Mr. Bradshaw’s last worldly obser-
vation. The broad-bladed axe flashed in the
sunlight, and when they lifted him in the
morning one side of his bullet head flopped
over on his shoulder, and some of his very
inferior cerebral tissue was spilled upon the
grass.
* * * *
The Bight Reverend Robert William Will-
son, D.D., Roman Catholic Bishop of Hobart
Town, excellent priest and good man, sat in
the stern-sheets of the first boat that came
ashore from the Governor Phillip on the
following morning. As - the convict crew
rested on their oars whilst the coxswain, hav-
ing got the two trees on Nepean Island in
their proper alignment, waited the psycholo-
gical moment to yell “Give way!” so that
the boat might shoot in past the end of the
reef on the crest of a roller, Dr. Everett, the
principal medical-officer of the establishment,
inquired of Major Childs, the Civil Com-
mandant —an anxious man with a worried
expression—“ Who’s the Devil-dodger,
Major?”
The Commandant frowned —perhaps at the
form of the question.
“Most unfortunate, Doctor —I think it’s
Bishop Willson.”
“O Lord —and Bradshaw’s not buried !”
“No —-most unfortunate business.”
Major Childs was the kind of man to
whom most things are “unfortunate.” He
knew that he was "unfortunate” in his posi-
tion —unlike his successor of the following
year, the redoubtable John Price, who felt
that it was the height of his good fortune to
make everyone else on the Island realise that
they were unfortunate —and deplored all
things accordingly.
The Commandant met the Bishop at the
top of the landing steps, and received his
letters of introduction from the Governor of
Van Diemen’s Land and the Comptroller of
Convicts. lie broke the seals hastily, and
glanced at them.
“A hearty welcome. Bishop.” he said, with
an attempt at geniality which was a dull
failure. “We shall endeavor to make your
stay pleasant.” he added lamely.
The Bishop’s smile was genial, and his
strong face lit up as he replied to this
greeting.
“I hope so. Major Childs —I hope it may
be. Certainly, your island is a lovely spot,
a gem of the ocean. But my mission to it
is less one of pleasure than of endeavor.”
“Now, what the devil does he mean by
that?” grumbled Dr. Everett to himself, as
he walked up the pier behind them.
It was not long before the Commandant
realised that the Bishop had not visited the
island for the good of bis health, or in any
spirit of whole-souled admiration for the
convict administration of Van Diemen’s
Land -and its dependencies. While they were

PILLS.
seated at dinner that evening, Dr. Willson
was at some pains to make this evident.
It was a small party. Major Harold, the
officer commanding the three companies of
the 96th Regiment which garrisoned the set-
tlement, the Rev. Mr. Rogers, senior Roman
Catholic chaplain, the Bishop and tbe Com-
mandant made it up. There was to be a
kind of state dinner on Saturday night, to
extend an official welcome to the eminent
churchman—who would have beejl genuinely
welcome had he been more of a churchman
and less of a reformer and philanthropist—
and this present was an informal affair.
The talk was of the System. That was
one of the System’s peculiarities—it monopo-
lised the mental processes of all who had
to do with it. The Bishop’s inquiry on a
point concerning the fruit-growing capabili-
ties of the island had elicited a complaint
from Major Harold to the effect that certain
prisoners, unknown, were raiding his orange-
trees. And this led to a succession of anec-
dotes, humorous and otherwise, of the preda-
tory habits of prisoners. The Bishop realised
that, if the prisoners could not escape from
Norfolk Island, Norfolk Island could not
escape from the prisoners. So he l’esigned
himself to the inevitable, and discussed con-
victs, with a knowledge that the Comman-
dant found almost embarrassing.
“The poor men !” he sighed. “Do you
know, Major, I often feel that the rest of us
are very lucky?”
“Lucky! How so, Bishop?”
“That we have not been found out—or we
might be here, too.”
“Thrue for you, me Lord,” assented Father
Rogers, sipping his port.
“I am very interested,” the Bishop went
on, “in a case you have here. A young
man, who has sinned undeniably, but wffio has
been fortunate in having influential friends
at home —friends who have been able to
make interest on his behalf with the Queen
herself, and consequently, of course, with
the Secretary of State. So much so,
that Sir Eardley Wilmot has en-
trusted me with a pardon for him, which,
in accordance with his Excellency’s instruc-
tions, Major, I will hand to you in the morn-
ing, when I have unpacked my baggage. A
young man of good family—a nephew of
Lord Dumaresq. One Belchamber —you will
know of him, of course?”
“Belchamber!” gasped the Commandant,
staring at the Bishop.
“Belchamber! Oh, th’ howly saints pre-
sarve us!” muttered Father Rogers.
“Belchamber ! Good heavens !” ejaculated
Major Harold.
There was a silence for a few moments,
whilst the Bishop looked from one to the
other of his companions, a little astonished
at the evident consternation his mention of
the man Belchamber had produced amongst
them. At length, deeming himself more
unfortunate than ever, the Commandant
spoke. But not before the Bishop had in-
quired wonderingly:
“Why, what’s the matter?”
“Well, you see. Bishop—the fact is—er—
it is very unfortunate. This young man
Belchamber —a well-conducted prisoner, I
think I may say, Father Rogqys? You knew
him, I think.”
“I did. Not wan of me flock. But I knew
him. A dacint lad—more’s th’ pity.”
“He was Bagshott’s servant,” put in Major
Harold. “Bagshott swore by him.”
“Was?” questioned the Bishop, mystified.
The Commandant hastened to explain.
“You see, Bishop, this man Belchamber,
very unfortunately, yesterday, had occasion
to murder —that is to say, he did murder —-
one of the constables. The head-constable,
to be precise. And then he absconded to the
bush. Parties have been searching for him
all day—without success. But this after-
noon his hat was found on the edge of a
cliff near Anson Bay. I feel certain —we all
feel certain —that the wretched man has
jumped into the sea—has committed suicide.
Every inch of the island has been searched.
Perhaps it was best. A little more wine,
Bishop?”
“God rest his soul,” said the Bishop in a
low tone. “And his victim.”
“Amen !” said Father Rogers aloud, and,
to himself: “’Tis little rest that blackguard
Bradshaw will be after gettin’.”
“Your news has a little upset me, Major.
I knew this young man’s people. They lived
near Nottingham, where I worked for many
years. I think, with your permission, I will
retire. It is very sad.”
“Most unfortunate!” sighed the Com-
mandant.
For five days the good Bishop investigated
Norfolk Island, and by the end of that time
had come to a conclusion that Norfolk
Island and Hell were synonymous terms.
Perhaps in no time in its history had the
place been in a worse condition. The well-
meant. and wilfully obstructed, experiment
in kindness and commonsense which Captain
Machonochie had been permitted to attempt
in the administration of the penal settlement
had proved a disastrous failure —though,
judging it seventy years afterwards, it is
difficult not to agree with the disappointed
Machonochie that it might well have suc-
ceeded had it only received more sympathetic
and intelligent backing from the Administra-
tion in Hobart Town. The best and most
successful prison system in the British Em-
pire to-day is that of New South Wales, and,
if you study it, you will find that it is very
largely that of Captain Machonochie, R.N.
Major Childs —a well-meaning, rather
weak man, and too much of a gentleman for
such work —was reaping the fruits of Mach-
onochie’s failure. Discipline was bad, crime
was bad —unnatural crime was awful—and
the place w T as a blot on civilisation. Bishop
Willson has left in on record that Major
Childs said to him as he was about to
depart: “You are going home. For God’s
sake tell them the truth about this place!”
Dr. Willson did tell a select committee of
the House of Lords the truth, and to the day
of his death was always certain that the best
of his life’s work was done when he brought
about the abandonment of Norfolk Island as
a prison settlement.
Sick at heart, the Bishop gave himself a
holiday, and, refusing companionship, took
some lunch in his pocket and set out to ex-
plore Mount Pitt, lie had much to think
over, and a seething memory of horrors that
he wished-he might forget.
He passed through the station at Long-
ridge, skirted some of the fern-smothered
ravines and gullies about its base, and
ascended the hill by the spur along which
the bridle-path runs to-day. Mount Pitt is
only a little over a thousand feet in height,
but the view from the summit is magnificent.
There is blue sea all round, as you get it
from a masthead, and, at your feet, eight
thousand of the most beautiful acres in all
the world. As he stood gazing from the hill-
top, the Bishop felt that the beauty of the
world he looked N upon, and drank in great
draughts, almost washed away the evil taste
the same world had left in his soul.
“I say!” said someone behind him, in a
weak voice.
Startled by this interruption of his soli-
tude, the Bishop looked round.
At the edge of the little space that had
been cleared on the summit crouched a gaunt
figure, too weak to stand erect. Hollow-
eyed, unshaven, clad in the rags of the rough
convict uniform, hatless and tousle-headed,
Mr. Belchamber glared with dim eyes at the
Bishop.
“Food !” he croaked. “Food !”
Without a word, the Bishop took the pack-
age of sandwiches from his pocket, walked
towards the unhappy man. and held it out.
With his teeth Mr. Belchamber tore the nap-
kin from it, and wolfed the bread and meat
in gulps, like a famished dog. Great bolts
of it nearly choked him. When it w T as gone
he collapsed on his face in the grass, his head
pillowed on his arms, and sobbed in silent
spasms that it hurt the Bishop to watch. He
said nothing for a little time. Then he
stooped over the convict and laid a friendly
hand upon his shoulder.
“You are,” he spoke gently, almost whis-
pering—“you are Dumaresq Belchamber?”
The man looked up, and then rose to a sit-
ting position, supporting himself with one
hand.
“I was—l used to be,” he said, “when I
was alive.”
“And you killed this man Bradshaw?”
“Yes.”
Infinite pity surged through the Bishop—-
sorrow for the bitter ill-luck of the thing
because the Governor Phillip brig had not
brought him with the pardon a day sooner
to the island. There was no pardon now.
Nor would he tell him of it. The unhappy
man must suffer. The Bishop saw it all.
In three days’ time, if he were captured, he
would sail back to Hobart Town with the
Bishop, to stand his trial. Not many days
after that he w r ould die the shameful, miser-
able death that the Bishop had seen so
many men die. Better to die now—infinitely
better. But the Bishop could not counte-
nance self-destruction. Neither could he kill
the man himself.
“They will hang you,” he whispered.
The miserable man looked up. and the
Bishop caught a light in his eyes, that some-
how relieved him.
“They will not!” said Mr. Belchamber.
“God help you, my son,” said the Bishop
holding out his hand and grasping the weak
one which Mr. Belchamber extended f, ~
God receive your soul!” t( * ee My.
lie retained the hand a moment and iv
lips moved in prayer. Then he turned
strode hurriedly over the brow 0 f ail(1

And a laughter that ran
Over Moina’s black hair
To the heart of a man;
With the thorn-bush in leaf
And the wet clover green—
Och, April, you thief,
Is it love that you mean?
’Twas her mother’s white goat
On the side of the hill
And the rain on my coat
With the sun laughing still,
And the thought of her eyes—
Sure, my heart is a gift,
In the black of surprise,
When her eyelashes lift!
’Twas the word that I spoke
With the wind blowing clear
And the small sob that broke
In my throat full of fear.
“Och. Danny,” she said,
“There’s the white cream to set
And the pigs to be fed
And you’re plaguing me yet!”
Would she slide past the door?
Och, her tongue was too wise;
But I listened far more
To the look in her eyes—
“ Sure, stay and be kist.”
But she turned by the wall
With a fine-lady twist
Of her head and her shawl.
’Twas the Spring in the air
And the green of the world,
And the black of her hair
Set me mad where it curled.
“Och, Moina, come out,
Girl of dreams, and be kist”—
But she hit me a clout
With the white of her fist.
Would she slide past the door?
Sure, her mouth w r as too red,
With the cheek of me sore
And those eyes in her head.
Troth, I kist her too well—
Twenty times at the least.
“Now. Danny, we’ll tell
A small word to the priest.” s
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WEIGHT.

“Nerves”
Our nerves are similar to an intri-
cate network of telegraph wires.
Controlled and nourished by a
portion of the brain —known as
the nerve centres —the delicate
threadlike nerves radiate in all
directions throughout the body.
So long as the nerve centres are
capable of continually supplying
nourishment to the nerves, the
nerves will remain strong and
healthy. But directly the nerve
centres become weakened by
overwork, worry or anxiety, they
are unable to transmit the
necessary nourishment, and the
nerves become worn out and
“on edge.” Then it is that a sud-
den sound makes you “jump”
—you get irritable—you suffer
from neuralgia—you are rest-
less and depressed. In this con-
dition there is nothing to equal
